Causality and Created Things
beginner
5 min
Definition of Agent: Will and Choice as Essential
Ghazali defines the agent and refutes natural causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's definition of a true agent (al-fāʿil) as one who acts by will and choice, and why he rejects the philosophers' view of necessary causation.

Core Idea
A true agent is a willful cause, not a natural cause.

Ghazali opens his critique by establishing what the term 'agent' really means. He argues that the philosophers misuse the word when they apply it to natural causes like fire or the sun. This lesson presents his definition and the initial contrast he draws between voluntary agency and natural necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the opening lines where Ghazali defines the agent. Note the conditions: will, choice, knowledge.
The phrase 'with will for the act by way of choice' is the core of Ghazali's definition.
2
Contrast with natural causation
Notice how Ghazali contrasts this with the philosophers' view of necessary emanation. Understand his example of the lamp and the shadow.
3
Explore the metaphor argument
Understand why Ghazali says calling natural causes 'agents' is an excessive metaphor that goes beyond proper limits.
He acknowledges commonality but stresses that the specific manner of causation is missing.
4
Check common language
See how Ghazali uses everyday language—walls and stones are not called agents—to support his point.
In ordinary speech, we say the living being acts, not the inanimate object.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الأول العلة، فنقول: الفاعل عبارة عمن يصدر عنه الفعل مع الإرادة للفعل على سبيل الاختيار، ومع العلم بالمراد، وعندكم أن العالم من الله تعالى كالمعلول من يلزم لزوماً ضرورياً، لا يتصور من الله تعالى دفعه، كلزوم الظل من الشخص، والنور من الشمس وليس هذا من الفعل في شيء، بل من قال: إن السراج يفعل الضوء، والشخص يفعل الظل، فقد تجوز، وتوسع في التجوز توسعاً خارجاً عن الحد، واستعار اللفظ اكتفاء بوقوع المشاركة بين المستعار له والمستعار منه في وصف واحد، وهو أن الفاعل سبب على الجملة، والسراج سبب الضوء، والشمس سبب النور، ولكن الفاعل لم يسم فاعلا صانعاً، لمجرد كونه سبباً، بل لكونه سبباً على وجه مخصوص، وهو وقوع الفعل منه على وجه الإرادة والاختيار، حتى لو قال قائل: الجدار ليس بفاعل، والحجر ليس بفاعل، والجماد ليس بفاعل، وإنما الفعل للحيوان، لم ينكر عليه في ذلك، ولم يكن قوله كاذباً، وللحجر فعل عندهم، وهو الهوى والثقل والميل إلى المركز، كما أن للنار فعلا وهو التسخين، وللحائط فعلا، وهو الميل إلى المركز ووقوع الظل، فإن كل ذلك صادر منه وهو محال. فإن قيل: كل موجود ليس واجب الوجود بذاته وإنما هو موجود بغيره، فإنا نسمى ذلك الشيء مفعولا، ونسمى سببه فاعلا ولا نبالي كان السبب فاعلا بالطبع، أم بالإرادة، كما أنكم لا تبالون أنه كان فاعلاً بآلة أم بغير آلة، بل الفعل جنس، وينقسم إلى ما يقع بآلة، وإلى ما يقع بغير آلة، فكذلك هو جنس، وينقسم إلى ما يقع بالطبع وإلى ما يقع بالاختيار، بدليل أنا إذا قلنا فعل بالطبع، لم يكن قولنا بالطبع ضدا لقولنا فعل ولا دفعاً ونقضاً له، بل كان بياناً لنوع الفعل، كما أنا إذا قلنا فعل مباشرة من غير آلة، لم يكن نقضاً، بل كان تنويعاً وبياناً، وإذا قلنا فعل بالاختيار، لم يكن تكراراً مثل قولنا: حيوان إنسان، بل كان بياناً لنوع الفعل، كقولنا فعل بآلة، ولو كان قولنا فعل يتضمن الإرادة، وكانت الإرادة ذاتية للفعل، من حيث إنه فعل، لكان قولنا فعل بالطبع، متناقضاً، كقولنا فعل وما فعل. قلنا: هذه التسمية فاسدة، ولا يجوز أن يسمى كل سبب بأي وجه كان فاعلا، ولا كل مسبب مفعولا، ولو كان كذلك، لما صح أن يقال: الجماد لا فعل له، وإنما الفعل للحيوان، وهذه من الكليات المشهورة الصادقة فإن سمى الجماد فاعلا، فبالاستعارة، كما قد يسمى طالباً مريداً على سبيل المجاز، يسمى إذ يقال: الحجر يهوى، لأنه يريد المركز ويطلبه، والطلب والإرادة حقيقة لا يتصوران إلا مع العلم بالمراد المطلوب، ولا يتصوران إلا من الحيوان. وأما قولكم: إن قولنا: فعل عام، وينقسم إلى ما هو بالطبع، وإلى ما هو بالإرادة، فهو غير مسلم، وهو كقول القائل: قولنا: أراد، عام، وينقسم فاسد، إلى من يريد مع العلم بالمراد، وإلى من يريد، ولا يعلم ما يريد؛ وهو إذ الإرادة تتضمن العلم بالضرورة، فكذلك الفعل يتضمن الإرادة بالضرورة. وأما قولكم: إن قولنا: فعل بالطبع ليس بنقض للأول، فليس كذلك فإنه نقض له من حيث الحقيقة، ولكن لا يسبق إلى الفهم التناقض، ولا يشتد نفور الطبع عنه، لأنه يبقى مجازاً، فإنه لما كان سبباً ما، والفاعل أيضاً بوجه سبب، سمي فعلا مجازاً. وإذا قيل: فعل بالاختيار فهو تكرير على التحقيق، كقوله: أراد وهو عالم بما أراده، إلا أنه لما تصور أن يقال: فعل وهو مجاز، ويقال: فعل وهو حقيقة، لم تنفر النفس عن قوله فعل بالاختيار وكان معناه فعل فعلا حقيقيا لا مجازياً، كقول القائل: تكلم بلسانه، ونظر بعينه، فإنه لما جاز أن يستعمل النظر في القلب مجازاً، والكلام في تحريك الرأس واليد، حتى يقال: قال برأسه أي نعم؛ لم يستقبح أن يقال: قال بلسانه.
Regarding the first [point] concerning the cause, we say: The agent is an expression for one from whom the act issues with will for the act by way of choice, and with knowledge of the intended [object]. According to you, the world from God Most High is like the effect that follows necessarily, it not being conceivable that God Most High repels it, like the following of the shadow from the person and the light from the sun. This is not from the act in any way. Rather, whoever says that the lamp does the light, and the person does the shadow, has used metaphor (tajawwaza) and exceeded in metaphor to an extent beyond the limit, and he borrowed the term being satisfied with the occurrence of commonality between the thing borrowed for and the thing borrowed from in one description, namely that the agent is a cause in general, and the lamp is a cause of light, and the sun is a cause of light. But the agent is not called an agent and a maker merely because it is a cause, but because it is a cause in a specific manner, which is the occurrence of the act from it by way of will and choice. Even if someone were to say: The wall is not an agent, the stone is not an agent, the inanimate is not an agent, and the act is only for the living being, he would not be denied in that, nor would his statement be false. According to them, the stone has an act, which is falling, heaviness, and inclination toward the center, just as fire has an act, which is heating, and the wall has an act, which is inclination toward the center and the occurrence of shadow. For all that issues from it and it is impossible [to be an agent in reality]. If it is said: Every existent that is not necessary of existence by itself but rather exists by another—we call that thing an effect (maf'ul) and we call its cause an agent (fa'il), and we do not care whether the cause is an agent by nature (bi al-tab') or by will, just as you do not care whether it is an agent with an instrument or without an instrument. Rather, act is a genus that divides into what occurs with an instrument and what occurs without an instrument. So similarly it is a genus that divides into what occurs by nature and what occurs by choice. The evidence is that when we say 'act by nature,' our saying 'by nature' is not contrary to our saying 'act,' nor a repudiation and negation of it, but rather it is an explanation of the type of act. Just as when we say 'act directly without an instrument,' it is not a negation, but rather a specification and explanation. And when we say 'act by choice,' it is not a repetition like our saying 'living being, human,' but rather an explanation of the type of act, like our saying 'act with an instrument.' And if our saying 'act' entailed will, and will were essential to the act insofar as it is an act, then our saying 'act by nature' would be contradictory, like our saying 'act and not act.' We say: This naming is invalid. It is not permissible to call every cause, in whatever manner, an agent, nor every effect an effect. If that were so, it would not be correct to say that the inanimate has no act, and the act is only for the living being. And these are among well-known true universals. If the inanimate is called an agent, it is by metaphor (isti'ara), just as it is sometimes called a seeker and a willer figuratively, when it is said: the stone falls because it wants the center and seeks it. But seeking and willing in reality are not conceivable except with knowledge of the intended and sought object, and are not conceivable except from a living being. As for your statement that our saying ‘act’ is general and divides into what is by nature and what is by will, it is not granted. It is like someone saying: our saying ‘he willed’ is general and divides invalidly into one who wills with knowledge of the intended and one who wills without knowing what he wills. For will entails knowledge necessarily, and similarly act entails will necessarily. As for your statement that our saying ‘act by nature’ is not a negation of the first, it is not so. Rather, it is a negation of it in reality, but the contradiction does not occur to the understanding, nor does the nature strongly recoil from it, because it remains a metaphor. Since it is some kind of cause, and the agent is also in a way a cause, it is called an act metaphorically. And when it is said: 'act by choice,' it is a repetition in reality, like saying: he willed while knowing what he willed. However, because it is conceivable that one says 'act' as metaphor and one says 'act' as reality, the soul does not recoil from the statement 'act by choice,' and its meaning is 'to do a real act, not a metaphorical one,' like someone saying: he spoke with his tongue and he looked with his eye. For when it is permissible to use 'looking' for the heart metaphorically, and 'speaking' for moving the head and hand, to the point that it is said: 'he said with his head, i.e., yes,' it is not deemed objectionable to say: 'he said with his tongue.'
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation. Ghazali begins by defining 'agent' (al-fāʿil) in a specific sense: it is one from whom an act issues with will (irāda) for the act by way of choice (ikhtiyār) and with knowledge (ʿilm) of the intended object. He contrasts this with the philosophers' view that God's creation of the world is like a necessary effect following a cause—e.g., shadow from a person or light from the sun—which is not a voluntary act. He argues that calling a lamp a 'doer' of light or a person a 'doer' of shadow is a metaphor (tajāwuz) that goes beyond proper limits. The commonality is that both are causes in some sense, but the term 'agent' is only properly used for a cause that acts by will and choice. This is supported by ordinary language: we do not call a wall, a stone, or an inanimate object an 'agent,' and if someone says they are not agents, we accept it as true. Inanimate things do have effects (e.g., stone's falling, fire's heating), but these are not called 'acts' in the strict sense.

Then Ghazali presents an objection from the philosophers (fāʾin qīl): they argue that every contingent is an effect (mafʿūl) caused by something else, and they call that cause an 'agent' regardless of whether it acts by nature or by will. They compare this to the distinction between acting with or without an instrument, which does not negate the term 'act.' They say 'act' is a genus that divides into natural and voluntary, and 'by nature' does not contradict 'act' but specifies its type, just as 'by instrument' specifies. If 'act' entailed will, then 'act by nature' would be contradictory.

Ghazali's response (qulnā) rejects this naming as invalid. He insists that not every cause can be called an agent, and not every effect can be called an effect (mafʿūl) in the same sense. If the philosophers' view were correct, the common knowledge that 'the inanimate has no act, and act is only for the living' would be false. Calling an inanimate object an agent is a metaphor (istiʿāra), like saying a stone 'seeks' the center—a figurative expression. In reality, seeking and willing require knowledge of the intended object and are exclusive to living beings.

He then deconstructs the philosophers' analogy. The claim that 'act' is a general term dividing into natural and voluntary is not granted. It would be like saying 'he willed' divides into willing with knowledge and willing without knowledge—which is invalid because will necessarily entails knowledge. Similarly, 'act' necessarily entails will. So 'act by nature' would be a contradiction in reality, though it does not immediately strike the listener as contradictory because the term is used metaphorically. Finally, he explains that 'act by choice' is actually a repetition (takrīr) in reality, since real act already implies choice. Yet it is acceptable because it clarifies that the act is real (ḥaqīqa), not metaphorical (majāz), just as 'he spoke with his tongue' clarifies the real mode of speaking when 'speaking' can be used metaphorically for gestures.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is necessary for something to be a true agent (al-fāʿil)?
A) Being a cause of something
B) Acting by will and choice with knowledge of the intended object
C) Being a necessary cause like a natural cause
D) Producing an effect without any instrument

Key Takeaway
A true agent acts by will and choice, not by necessity—mere causality is not enough.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes think of God's actions as automatic, like a natural force? How does this lesson challenge you to see God as a personal Will behind every event? Will you consciously remind yourself of this when you observe nature today?
?
When you cause harm or good, do you act with intention and choice, or are you just reacting? What would change if you lived every moment as a true agent, responsible before God for your choices?
?
If inanimate things are not agents, what does that say about the responsibility you bear for your actions? Are you living as a mere cause or as a true agent accountable to the Creator?

Causality and Created Things
beginner
5 min
The Philosophers' Objection and Ghazali's Rebuttal
Ghazali transforms the debate by showing that act is not a neutral genus but entails will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that 'act' is a genus divided into natural and voluntary, and Ghazali's counter-argument that 'act' necessarily entails will.

Core Idea
Act implies will by nature—'act by nature' is a contradiction in terms.

The philosophers present a challenge: if 'act' is a general term that can be specified by 'by nature' or 'by choice,' then natural causes can be called agents. Ghazali responds by insisting that the very concept of 'act' includes will, making 'act by nature' as absurd as 'volition without knowledge.' This lesson explores this pivotal exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The philosophers argue that 'act' is a genus that divides into types, just like action with or without instrument. Understand their reasoning.
They use the analogy of instrument to argue that 'by nature' is a valid specification.
2
Identify the key analogy
Notice how they compare 'by nature' to 'by instrument' as a specification, not a negation.
3
Ghazali's rebuttal: the naming is invalid
Ghazali says you cannot call every cause an agent; ordinary language rejects it. This is the first part of his response.
4
The 'will' entailment
Ghazali argues that act entails will necessarily, just as will entails knowledge. This makes 'act by nature' contradictory.
This is the core logical move: act cannot be conceived without will.

Source Text

أما الأول العلة، فنقول: الفاعل عبارة عمن يصدر عنه الفعل مع الإرادة للفعل على سبيل الاختيار، ومع العلم بالمراد، وعندكم أن العالم من الله تعالى كالمعلول من يلزم لزوماً ضرورياً، لا يتصور من الله تعالى دفعه، كلزوم الظل من الشخص، والنور من الشمس وليس هذا من الفعل في شيء، بل من قال: إن السراج يفعل الضوء، والشخص يفعل الظل، فقد تجوز، وتوسع في التجوز توسعاً خارجاً عن الحد، واستعار اللفظ اكتفاء بوقوع المشاركة بين المستعار له والمستعار منه في وصف واحد، وهو أن الفاعل سبب على الجملة، والسراج سبب الضوء، والشمس سبب النور، ولكن الفاعل لم يسم فاعلا صانعاً، لمجرد كونه سبباً، بل لكونه سبباً على وجه مخصوص، وهو وقوع الفعل منه على وجه الإرادة والاختيار، حتى لو قال قائل: الجدار ليس بفاعل، والحجر ليس بفاعل، والجماد ليس بفاعل، وإنما الفعل للحيوان، لم ينكر عليه في ذلك، ولم يكن قوله كاذباً، وللحجر فعل عندهم، وهو الهوى والثقل والميل إلى المركز، كما أن للنار فعلا وهو التسخين، وللحائط فعلا، وهو الميل إلى المركز ووقوع الظل، فإن كل ذلك صادر منه وهو محال. فإن قيل: كل موجود ليس واجب الوجود بذاته وإنما هو موجود بغيره، فإنا نسمى ذلك الشيء مفعولا، ونسمى سببه فاعلا ولا نبالي كان السبب فاعلا بالطبع، أم بالإرادة، كما أنكم لا تبالون أنه كان فاعلاً بآلة أم بغير آلة، بل الفعل جنس، وينقسم إلى ما يقع بآلة، وإلى ما يقع بغير آلة، فكذلك هو جنس، وينقسم إلى ما يقع بالطبع وإلى ما يقع بالاختيار، بدليل أنا إذا قلنا فعل بالطبع، لم يكن قولنا بالطبع ضدا لقولنا فعل ولا دفعاً ونقضاً له، بل كان بياناً لنوع الفعل، كما أنا إذا قلنا فعل مباشرة من غير آلة، لم يكن نقضاً، بل كان تنويعاً وبياناً، وإذا قلنا فعل بالاختيار، لم يكن تكراراً مثل قولنا: حيوان إنسان، بل كان بياناً لنوع الفعل، كقولنا فعل بآلة، ولو كان قولنا فعل يتضمن الإرادة، وكانت الإرادة ذاتية للفعل، من حيث إنه فعل، لكان قولنا فعل بالطبع، متناقضاً، كقولنا فعل وما فعل. قلنا: هذه التسمية فاسدة، ولا يجوز أن يسمى كل سبب بأي وجه كان فاعلا، ولا كل مسبب مفعولا، ولو كان كذلك، لما صح أن يقال: الجماد لا فعل له، وإنما الفعل للحيوان، وهذه من الكليات المشهورة الصادقة فإن سمى الجماد فاعلا، فبالاستعارة، كما قد يسمى طالباً مريداً على سبيل المجاز، يسمى إذ يقال: الحجر يهوى، لأنه يريد المركز ويطلبه، والطلب والإرادة حقيقة لا يتصوران إلا مع العلم بالمراد المطلوب، ولا يتصوران إلا من الحيوان. وأما قولكم: إن قولنا: فعل عام، وينقسم إلى ما هو بالطبع، وإلى ما هو بالإرادة، فهو غير مسلم، وهو كقول القائل: قولنا: أراد، عام، وينقسم فاسد، إلى من يريد مع العلم بالمراد، وإلى من يريد، ولا يعلم ما يريد؛ وهو إذ الإرادة تتضمن العلم بالضرورة، فكذلك الفعل يتضمن الإرادة بالضرورة. وأما قولكم: إن قولنا: فعل بالطبع ليس بنقض للأول، فليس كذلك فإنه نقض له من حيث الحقيقة، ولكن لا يسبق إلى الفهم التناقض، ولا يشتد نفور الطبع عنه، لأنه يبقى مجازاً، فإنه لما كان سبباً ما، والفاعل أيضاً بوجه سبب، سمي فعلا مجازاً. وإذا قيل: فعل بالاختيار فهو تكرير على التحقيق، كقوله: أراد وهو عالم بما أراده، إلا أنه لما تصور أن يقال: فعل وهو مجاز، ويقال: فعل وهو حقيقة، لم تنفر النفس عن قوله فعل بالاختيار وكان معناه فعل فعلا حقيقيا لا مجازياً، كقول القائل: تكلم بلسانه، ونظر بعينه، فإنه لما جاز أن يستعمل النظر في القلب مجازاً، والكلام في تحريك الرأس واليد، حتى يقال: قال برأسه أي نعم؛ لم يستقبح أن يقال: قال بلسانه.
Regarding the first [point] concerning the cause, we say: The agent is an expression for one from whom the act issues with will for the act by way of choice, and with knowledge of the intended [object]. According to you, the world from God Most High is like the effect that follows necessarily, it not being conceivable that God Most High repels it, like the following of the shadow from the person and the light from the sun. This is not from the act in any way. Rather, whoever says that the lamp does the light, and the person does the shadow, has used metaphor (tajawwaza) and exceeded in metaphor to an extent beyond the limit, and he borrowed the term being satisfied with the occurrence of commonality between the thing borrowed for and the thing borrowed from in one description, namely that the agent is a cause in general, and the lamp is a cause of light, and the sun is a cause of light. But the agent is not called an agent and a maker merely because it is a cause, but because it is a cause in a specific manner, which is the occurrence of the act from it by way of will and choice. Even if someone were to say: The wall is not an agent, the stone is not an agent, the inanimate is not an agent, and the act is only for the living being, he would not be denied in that, nor would his statement be false. According to them, the stone has an act, which is falling, heaviness, and inclination toward the center, just as fire has an act, which is heating, and the wall has an act, which is inclination toward the center and the occurrence of shadow. For all that issues from it and it is impossible [to be an agent in reality]. If it is said: Every existent that is not necessary of existence by itself but rather exists by another—we call that thing an effect (maf'ul) and we call its cause an agent (fa'il), and we do not care whether the cause is an agent by nature (bi al-tab') or by will, just as you do not care whether it is an agent with an instrument or without an instrument. Rather, act is a genus that divides into what occurs with an instrument and what occurs without an instrument. So similarly it is a genus that divides into what occurs by nature and what occurs by choice. The evidence is that when we say 'act by nature,' our saying 'by nature' is not contrary to our saying 'act,' nor a repudiation and negation of it, but rather it is an explanation of the type of act. Just as when we say 'act directly without an instrument,' it is not a negation, but rather a specification and explanation. And when we say 'act by choice,' it is not a repetition like our saying 'living being, human,' but rather an explanation of the type of act, like our saying 'act with an instrument.' And if our saying 'act' entailed will, and will were essential to the act insofar as it is an act, then our saying 'act by nature' would be contradictory, like our saying 'act and not act.' We say: This naming is invalid. It is not permissible to call every cause, in whatever manner, an agent, nor every effect an effect. If that were so, it would not be correct to say that the inanimate has no act, and the act is only for the living being. And these are among well-known true universals. If the inanimate is called an agent, it is by metaphor (isti'ara), just as it is sometimes called a seeker and a willer figuratively, when it is said: the stone falls because it wants the center and seeks it. But seeking and willing in reality are not conceivable except with knowledge of the intended and sought object, and are not conceivable except from a living being. As for your statement that our saying ‘act’ is general and divides into what is by nature and what is by will, it is not granted. It is like someone saying: our saying ‘he willed’ is general and divides invalidly into one who wills with knowledge of the intended and one who wills without knowing what he wills. For will entails knowledge necessarily, and similarly act entails will necessarily. As for your statement that our saying ‘act by nature’ is not a negation of the first, it is not so. Rather, it is a negation of it in reality, but the contradiction does not occur to the understanding, nor does the nature strongly recoil from it, because it remains a metaphor. Since it is some kind of cause, and the agent is also in a way a cause, it is called an act metaphorically. And when it is said: 'act by choice,' it is a repetition in reality, like saying: he willed while knowing what he willed. However, because it is conceivable that one says 'act' as metaphor and one says 'act' as reality, the soul does not recoil from the statement 'act by choice,' and its meaning is 'to do a real act, not a metaphorical one,' like someone saying: he spoke with his tongue and he looked with his eye. For when it is permissible to use 'looking' for the heart metaphorically, and 'speaking' for moving the head and hand, to the point that it is said: 'he said with his head, i.e., yes,' it is not deemed objectionable to say: 'he said with his tongue.'
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation. Ghazali begins by defining 'agent' (al-fāʿil) in a specific sense: it is one from whom an act issues with will (irāda) for the act by way of choice (ikhtiyār) and with knowledge (ʿilm) of the intended object. He contrasts this with the philosophers' view that God's creation of the world is like a necessary effect following a cause—e.g., shadow from a person or light from the sun—which is not a voluntary act. He argues that calling a lamp a 'doer' of light or a person a 'doer' of shadow is a metaphor (tajāwuz) that goes beyond proper limits. The commonality is that both are causes in some sense, but the term 'agent' is only properly used for a cause that acts by will and choice. This is supported by ordinary language: we do not call a wall, a stone, or an inanimate object an 'agent,' and if someone says they are not agents, we accept it as true. Inanimate things do have effects (e.g., stone's falling, fire's heating), but these are not called 'acts' in the strict sense.

Then Ghazali presents an objection from the philosophers (fāʾin qīl): they argue that every contingent is an effect (mafʿūl) caused by something else, and they call that cause an 'agent' regardless of whether it acts by nature or by will. They compare this to the distinction between acting with or without an instrument, which does not negate the term 'act.' They say 'act' is a genus that divides into natural and voluntary, and 'by nature' does not contradict 'act' but specifies its type, just as 'by instrument' specifies. If 'act' entailed will, then 'act by nature' would be contradictory.

Ghazali's response (qulnā) rejects this naming as invalid. He insists that not every cause can be called an agent, and not every effect can be called an effect (mafʿūl) in the same sense. If the philosophers' view were correct, the common knowledge that 'the inanimate has no act, and act is only for the living' would be false. Calling an inanimate object an agent is a metaphor (istiʿāra), like saying a stone 'seeks' the center—a figurative expression. In reality, seeking and willing require knowledge of the intended object and are exclusive to living beings.

He then deconstructs the philosophers' analogy. The claim that 'act' is a general term dividing into natural and voluntary is not granted. It would be like saying 'he willed' divides into willing with knowledge and willing without knowledge—which is invalid because will necessarily entails knowledge. Similarly, 'act' necessarily entails will. So 'act by nature' would be a contradiction in reality, though it does not immediately strike the listener as contradictory because the term is used metaphorically. Finally, he explains that 'act by choice' is actually a repetition (takrīr) in reality, since real act already implies choice. Yet it is acceptable because it clarifies that the act is real (ḥaqīqa), not metaphorical (majāz), just as 'he spoke with his tongue' clarifies the real mode of speaking when 'speaking' can be used metaphorically for gestures.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali denies all causal relationships.
Correction: Ghazali only rejects applying the term 'agent' to natural causes; he acknowledges that inanimate things produce effects, but they are not agents in the true sense.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is the philosophers' division of 'act' into natural and voluntary invalid?
A) Because act is not a genus
B) Because act necessarily entails will, making 'act by nature' contradictory
C) Because natural causes do not produce effects
D) Because the philosophers cannot prove it

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that 'act' inherently involves will, so 'act by nature' is a contradiction; only voluntary action is true action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you use the word 'act' for anything—from the sun rising to your own choices—do you instinctively recognize will behind it? Or do you slip into thinking of actions as automatic? How does this lesson challenge you to change your speech and thoughts about causality?
?
You believe God is the true agent behind everything. But when something good happens to you, do you attribute it to luck, nature, or your own effort first? What would it mean for your gratitude if you truly saw every 'natural' event as God's voluntary act right now?
?
Ghazali says only living beings with will and knowledge are true agents. Look at your own actions today: how many were done with full awareness and intention? How many were mechanical? If act entails will, what does that say about those mechanical actions? What will you do to bring more deliberate will into your daily acts?

Causality and Created Things

Metaphor and Reality in the Concept of Act
Ghazali clarifies the metaphorical nature of 'act by nature' and the clarifying function of 'act by choice'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's explanation of why 'act by nature' is tolerable as a metaphor but not as a literal description, and why 'act by choice' is a repetition that clarifies real action.

Core Idea
Calling natural causation 'act' is metaphor; real action is always by choice.

Ghazali continues his refutation by addressing the claim that 'act by nature' is not a negation. He explains that it is a negation in reality but accepted as metaphor. He also clarifies that 'act by choice' is not a redundant qualifier but a way to emphatically assert that the action is real, not metaphorical. This lesson dissects the final layers of his argument.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the metaphor argument
Ghazali says 'act by nature' is metaphorically acceptable, but it negates real agency. It is a denial of true action.
The contradiction is not immediately felt because the term 'act' is used loosely.
2
Understand 'act by choice' as repetition
He argues that 'act by choice' is a repetition in reality, but it serves to distinguish real from metaphorical acts.
It is like saying 'he willed with knowledge' – a tautology that clarifies.
3
The tongue and eye analogy
Study the analogy of 'speaking with the tongue' to see how qualifiers can clarify real vs. metaphorical usage.
Just as 'looked with his eye' clarifies literal seeing, 'act by choice' clarifies literal action.

أما الأول العلة، فنقول: الفاعل عبارة عمن يصدر عنه الفعل مع الإرادة للفعل على سبيل الاختيار، ومع العلم بالمراد، وعندكم أن العالم من الله تعالى كالمعلول من يلزم لزوماً ضرورياً، لا يتصور من الله تعالى دفعه، كلزوم الظل من الشخص، والنور من الشمس وليس هذا من الفعل في شيء، بل من قال: إن السراج يفعل الضوء، والشخص يفعل الظل، فقد تجوز، وتوسع في التجوز توسعاً خارجاً عن الحد، واستعار اللفظ اكتفاء بوقوع المشاركة بين المستعار له والمستعار منه في وصف واحد، وهو أن الفاعل سبب على الجملة، والسراج سبب الضوء، والشمس سبب النور، ولكن الفاعل لم يسم فاعلا صانعاً، لمجرد كونه سبباً، بل لكونه سبباً على وجه مخصوص، وهو وقوع الفعل منه على وجه الإرادة والاختيار، حتى لو قال قائل: الجدار ليس بفاعل، والحجر ليس بفاعل، والجماد ليس بفاعل، وإنما الفعل للحيوان، لم ينكر عليه في ذلك، ولم يكن قوله كاذباً، وللحجر فعل عندهم، وهو الهوى والثقل والميل إلى المركز، كما أن للنار فعلا وهو التسخين، وللحائط فعلا، وهو الميل إلى المركز ووقوع الظل، فإن كل ذلك صادر منه وهو محال. فإن قيل: كل موجود ليس واجب الوجود بذاته وإنما هو موجود بغيره، فإنا نسمى ذلك الشيء مفعولا، ونسمى سببه فاعلا ولا نبالي كان السبب فاعلا بالطبع، أم بالإرادة، كما أنكم لا تبالون أنه كان فاعلاً بآلة أم بغير آلة، بل الفعل جنس، وينقسم إلى ما يقع بآلة، وإلى ما يقع بغير آلة، فكذلك هو جنس، وينقسم إلى ما يقع بالطبع وإلى ما يقع بالاختيار، بدليل أنا إذا قلنا فعل بالطبع، لم يكن قولنا بالطبع ضدا لقولنا فعل ولا دفعاً ونقضاً له، بل كان بياناً لنوع الفعل، كما أنا إذا قلنا فعل مباشرة من غير آلة، لم يكن نقضاً، بل كان تنويعاً وبياناً، وإذا قلنا فعل بالاختيار، لم يكن تكراراً مثل قولنا: حيوان إنسان، بل كان بياناً لنوع الفعل، كقولنا فعل بآلة، ولو كان قولنا فعل يتضمن الإرادة، وكانت الإرادة ذاتية للفعل، من حيث إنه فعل، لكان قولنا فعل بالطبع، متناقضاً، كقولنا فعل وما فعل. قلنا: هذه التسمية فاسدة، ولا يجوز أن يسمى كل سبب بأي وجه كان فاعلا، ولا كل مسبب مفعولا، ولو كان كذلك، لما صح أن يقال: الجماد لا فعل له، وإنما الفعل للحيوان، وهذه من الكليات المشهورة الصادقة فإن سمى الجماد فاعلا، فبالاستعارة، كما قد يسمى طالباً مريداً على سبيل المجاز، يسمى إذ يقال: الحجر يهوى، لأنه يريد المركز ويطلبه، والطلب والإرادة حقيقة لا يتصوران إلا مع العلم بالمراد المطلوب، ولا يتصوران إلا من الحيوان. وأما قولكم: إن قولنا: فعل عام، وينقسم إلى ما هو بالطبع، وإلى ما هو بالإرادة، فهو غير مسلم، وهو كقول القائل: قولنا: أراد، عام، وينقسم فاسد، إلى من يريد مع العلم بالمراد، وإلى من يريد، ولا يعلم ما يريد؛ وهو إذ الإرادة تتضمن العلم بالضرورة، فكذلك الفعل يتضمن الإرادة بالضرورة. وأما قولكم: إن قولنا: فعل بالطبع ليس بنقض للأول، فليس كذلك فإنه نقض له من حيث الحقيقة، ولكن لا يسبق إلى الفهم التناقض، ولا يشتد نفور الطبع عنه، لأنه يبقى مجازاً، فإنه لما كان سبباً ما، والفاعل أيضاً بوجه سبب، سمي فعلا مجازاً. وإذا قيل: فعل بالاختيار فهو تكرير على التحقيق، كقوله: أراد وهو عالم بما أراده، إلا أنه لما تصور أن يقال: فعل وهو مجاز، ويقال: فعل وهو حقيقة، لم تنفر النفس عن قوله فعل بالاختيار وكان معناه فعل فعلا حقيقيا لا مجازياً، كقول القائل: تكلم بلسانه، ونظر بعينه، فإنه لما جاز أن يستعمل النظر في القلب مجازاً، والكلام في تحريك الرأس واليد، حتى يقال: قال برأسه أي نعم؛ لم يستقبح أن يقال: قال بلسانه.
Regarding the first [point] concerning the cause, we say: The agent is an expression for one from whom the act issues with will for the act by way of choice, and with knowledge of the intended [object]. According to you, the world from God Most High is like the effect that follows necessarily, it not being conceivable that God Most High repels it, like the following of the shadow from the person and the light from the sun. This is not from the act in any way. Rather, whoever says that the lamp does the light, and the person does the shadow, has used metaphor (tajawwaza) and exceeded in metaphor to an extent beyond the limit, and he borrowed the term being satisfied with the occurrence of commonality between the thing borrowed for and the thing borrowed from in one description, namely that the agent is a cause in general, and the lamp is a cause of light, and the sun is a cause of light. But the agent is not called an agent and a maker merely because it is a cause, but because it is a cause in a specific manner, which is the occurrence of the act from it by way of will and choice. Even if someone were to say: The wall is not an agent, the stone is not an agent, the inanimate is not an agent, and the act is only for the living being, he would not be denied in that, nor would his statement be false. According to them, the stone has an act, which is falling, heaviness, and inclination toward the center, just as fire has an act, which is heating, and the wall has an act, which is inclination toward the center and the occurrence of shadow. For all that issues from it and it is impossible [to be an agent in reality]. If it is said: Every existent that is not necessary of existence by itself but rather exists by another—we call that thing an effect (maf'ul) and we call its cause an agent (fa'il), and we do not care whether the cause is an agent by nature (bi al-tab') or by will, just as you do not care whether it is an agent with an instrument or without an instrument. Rather, act is a genus that divides into what occurs with an instrument and what occurs without an instrument. So similarly it is a genus that divides into what occurs by nature and what occurs by choice. The evidence is that when we say 'act by nature,' our saying 'by nature' is not contrary to our saying 'act,' nor a repudiation and negation of it, but rather it is an explanation of the type of act. Just as when we say 'act directly without an instrument,' it is not a negation, but rather a specification and explanation. And when we say 'act by choice,' it is not a repetition like our saying 'living being, human,' but rather an explanation of the type of act, like our saying 'act with an instrument.' And if our saying 'act' entailed will, and will were essential to the act insofar as it is an act, then our saying 'act by nature' would be contradictory, like our saying 'act and not act.' We say: This naming is invalid. It is not permissible to call every cause, in whatever manner, an agent, nor every effect an effect. If that were so, it would not be correct to say that the inanimate has no act, and the act is only for the living being. And these are among well-known true universals. If the inanimate is called an agent, it is by metaphor (isti'ara), just as it is sometimes called a seeker and a willer figuratively, when it is said: the stone falls because it wants the center and seeks it. But seeking and willing in reality are not conceivable except with knowledge of the intended and sought object, and are not conceivable except from a living being. As for your statement that our saying ‘act’ is general and divides into what is by nature and what is by will, it is not granted. It is like someone saying: our saying ‘he willed’ is general and divides invalidly into one who wills with knowledge of the intended and one who wills without knowing what he wills. For will entails knowledge necessarily, and similarly act entails will necessarily. As for your statement that our saying ‘act by nature’ is not a negation of the first, it is not so. Rather, it is a negation of it in reality, but the contradiction does not occur to the understanding, nor does the nature strongly recoil from it, because it remains a metaphor. Since it is some kind of cause, and the agent is also in a way a cause, it is called an act metaphorically. And when it is said: 'act by choice,' it is a repetition in reality, like saying: he willed while knowing what he willed. However, because it is conceivable that one says 'act' as metaphor and one says 'act' as reality, the soul does not recoil from the statement 'act by choice,' and its meaning is 'to do a real act, not a metaphorical one,' like someone saying: he spoke with his tongue and he looked with his eye. For when it is permissible to use 'looking' for the heart metaphorically, and 'speaking' for moving the head and hand, to the point that it is said: 'he said with his head, i.e., yes,' it is not deemed objectionable to say: 'he said with his tongue.'

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation. Ghazali begins by defining 'agent' (al-fāʿil) in a specific sense: it is one from whom an act issues with will (irāda) for the act by way of choice (ikhtiyār) and with knowledge (ʿilm) of the intended object. He contrasts this with the philosophers' view that God's creation of the world is like a necessary effect following a cause—e.g., shadow from a person or light from the sun—which is not a voluntary act. He argues that calling a lamp a 'doer' of light or a person a 'doer' of shadow is a metaphor (tajāwuz) that goes beyond proper limits. The commonality is that both are causes in some sense, but the term 'agent' is only properly used for a cause that acts by will and choice. This is supported by ordinary language: we do not call a wall, a stone, or an inanimate object an 'agent,' and if someone says they are not agents, we accept it as true. Inanimate things do have effects (e.g., stone's falling, fire's heating), but these are not called 'acts' in the strict sense.

Then Ghazali presents an objection from the philosophers (fāʾin qīl): they argue that every contingent is an effect (mafʿūl) caused by something else, and they call that cause an 'agent' regardless of whether it acts by nature or by will. They compare this to the distinction between acting with or without an instrument, which does not negate the term 'act.' They say 'act' is a genus that divides into natural and voluntary, and 'by nature' does not contradict 'act' but specifies its type, just as 'by instrument' specifies. If 'act' entailed will, then 'act by nature' would be contradictory.

Ghazali's response (qulnā) rejects this naming as invalid. He insists that not every cause can be called an agent, and not every effect can be called an effect (mafʿūl) in the same sense. If the philosophers' view were correct, the common knowledge that 'the inanimate has no act, and act is only for the living' would be false. Calling an inanimate object an agent is a metaphor (istiʿāra), like saying a stone 'seeks' the center—a figurative expression. In reality, seeking and willing require knowledge of the intended object and are exclusive to living beings.

He then deconstructs the philosophers' analogy. The claim that 'act' is a general term dividing into natural and voluntary is not granted. It would be like saying 'he willed' divides into willing with knowledge and willing without knowledge—which is invalid because will necessarily entails knowledge. Similarly, 'act' necessarily entails will. So 'act by nature' would be a contradiction in reality, though it does not immediately strike the listener as contradictory because the term is used metaphorically. Finally, he explains that 'act by choice' is actually a repetition (takrīr) in reality, since real act already implies choice. Yet it is acceptable because it clarifies that the act is real (ḥaqīqa), not metaphorical (majāz), just as 'he spoke with his tongue' clarifies the real mode of speaking when 'speaking' can be used metaphorically for gestures.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The True Meaning of 'Agent': Voluntary vs. Involuntary Action
Ghazali refutes natural causality as true agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that true agency requires will, and why natural causes are only metaphorical agents.

Core Idea
True agency is only from will; natural causes are metaphorical agents.

Ghazali confronts a common objection: everyday language calls fire a 'burner' and a sword a 'cutter.' Does this mean natural objects are genuine agents? He argues no—true action requires will. This passage establishes that without will, divine or human, there is no real agency.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection from language
Read the objection: Arabs say 'fire burns', 'sword cuts', etc. Does this prove natural causes are true agents?
Notice that the objection relies on common linguistic usage.
2
Examine Ghazali's answer: metaphor vs. reality
Ghazali replies that such expressions are metaphorical (مجاز); true action (فعل حقيقي) is only by will.
3
Analyze the proof using the fire-thrower example
Consider the example: a man throws another into a fire causing death. Who is called the killer? The thrower, not the fire, even though fire is proximate cause. This shows agency is attributed to the willing cause.
This is the key evidence for Ghazali's position.
4
Apply the conclusion to God's attributes
If God is not willing (as philosophers claim), then He is only a metaphorical agent, not a true one.
This connects the linguistic argument to theology.

ونظر بعينه ، ويكون معناه نفى احتمال المجاز ، فهذه مزلة القدم ، فليتنبه لمحل الخداع هؤلاء الأغبياء . فإن قيل : تسمية الفاعل فاعلا ، إنما تعرف من اللغة ، وإلا فقد ظهر في العقل أن ما يكون سبباً للشيء ، ينقسم إلى ما يكون مريداً ؛ وإلى ما ما لا يكون ؛ ووقع النزاع في أن اسم الفاعل على كلا القسمين حقيقة أم لا ؟ ! ولا سبيل إلى إنكاره ، إذ العرب تقول : النار تحرق ، والسيف يقطع . والثلج يبرد ، والسقمونيا تسهل ، والخبز يشبع ، والماء يروى . وقولنا : يضرب ، معناه يفعل الضرب ، وقولنا تحرق ، معناه تفعل الإحراق ، وقولنا : يقطع معناه يفعل القطع ، فإن قلتم ، إن كل ذلك مجاز ، كنتم متحكمين فيه من غیر مستند . والجواب : أن كل ذلك بطريق المجاز ، وإنما الفعل الحقيقى ما يكون بالإرادة . والدليل عليه أنا لو فرضنا حادثاً توقف في حصوله على أمرين أحدهما إرادى والآخر غير إرادى ، أضاف العقل الفعل إلى الإرادى ، وكذا اللغة ، فإن من ألقى إنساناً في النار فمات ، يقال : هو القاتل ، دون النار ، حتى إذا قيل ما قتله إلا فلان، صدق قائله ، فإن كان اسم الفاعل على المريد وعلى غير المريد ، على وجه واحد ، لا بطريق كون أحدهما أصلا وكون الآخر مستعاراً منه ، فلم يضاف القتل إلى المريد لغة وعرفاً وعقلا ؟ ! أن النار هي العلة القريبة فى القتل ، وكأن الملقى لم يتعاط إلا الجمع بينه وبين النار ، ولكن لما كان الجمع بينه وبين النار بالإرادة ، وتأثير النار بغير إرادة ، لم تسم النار قاتلة ، إلا بنوع من الاستعارة ، فدل أن الفاعل من يصدر منه الفعل عن إرادته ، فإذا لم يكن الله تعالى مريداً عندهم ولا مختاراً لفعل العالم ، لم يكن صانعاً ولا فاعلا إلا مجازاً .
And one looks with his eye, and its meaning is the denial of the possibility of metaphor, so this is a slippery slope, so let these fools pay attention to the place of deception. For if it is said: The naming of an agent as 'agent' is only known from language, otherwise it has become clear in reason that that which is a cause for something divides into that which is willing and that which is not; and dispute occurred as to whether the name 'agent' is applied to both divisions truly or not? There is no way to deny it, for the Arabs say: 'The fire burns,' 'The sword cuts,' 'The snow cools,' 'Scammony purges,' 'Bread satiates,' 'Water quenches.' And our saying 'he strikes' means he does the striking, and 'it burns' means it does the burning, and 'it cuts' means it does the cutting. If you say that all of this is metaphorical, you are being arbitrary without basis. The answer: All of that is by way of metaphor, and the true action is only that which is by will. The proof of that is that if we suppose an event whose occurrence depends on two matters, one voluntary and the other non-voluntary, reason attributes the action to the voluntary one, and likewise language. For whoever throws a person into a fire and he dies, it is said: He is the killer, not the fire, so much so that if it is said 'No one killed him but so-and-so', the speaker is truthful. If the name 'agent' were applied to both the willing and the non-willing in one way, not by one being original and the other being borrowed from it, then why is killing attributed to the willing one linguistically, customarily, and rationally? The fire is the proximate cause in the killing, and it is as if the thrower only undertook the combination between him and the fire. But since the combination between him and the fire was by will, and the fire's effect is without will, the fire is not called a killer except by a kind of metaphor. So it indicates that the agent is one from whom the act proceeds by his will. So if God is not willing according to them, nor choosing for the act of the world, He is not a maker nor an agent except metaphorically.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that natural causes are agents in the same sense as voluntary agents. Ghazali begins with a warning: if one interprets the term 'agent' in a way that denies the possibility of metaphorical usage, that is a slippery slope; the foolish should heed where deception lies. He then presents an objection from linguistic usage: the term 'agent' (فاعل) is known from language, and reason shows that a cause can be either voluntary or non-voluntary; the dispute is whether the name applies truly to both. The objector cites Arabic expressions like 'fire burns', 'sword cuts', etc., implying that these are genuine actions. Ghazali answers that all such expressions are metaphorical (مجاز); true action (الفعل الحقيقي) is only that which proceeds from will (إرادة). He provides a proof: if an event depends on two causes—one voluntary and one non-voluntary—reason and language attribute the action to the voluntary one. For example, if a man throws someone into a fire and he dies, the thrower is called the killer, not the fire, even though the fire is the proximate cause of death. If 'agent' applied equally to both, why would the attribution go to the willing one? The fire's effect is without will, so it is only metaphorically called a killer. This demonstrates that the agent is one from whom the act proceeds by his will. Ghazali concludes that if the philosophers deny will and choice to God, then He is not a maker or agent except metaphorically.

مجاز
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That using action verbs for natural objects in everyday language proves they are genuine agents in the same sense as voluntary agents.
Correction: Ghazali shows that such expressions are metaphorical; true agency requires will, as demonstrated by the example of the thrower and the fire.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's key example to prove that true agency requires will?
A) The fire burning wood
B) A man throwing another into a fire, where the thrower is called the killer
C) The sword cutting cloth
D) Water quenching thirst

Key Takeaway
True agency requires will; natural causes are agents only metaphorically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you next say 'the sun rose' or 'the medicine healed me,' will you consciously remember that these are mere metaphors, and that only Allah's will truly acts? How will you train your tongue to attribute all effects to the Creator?
?
If you were thrown into a fire, would your heart's last cry be to the fire's heat or to the One who commanded it? What does your instinctual fear of physical causes reveal about the depth of your tawheed?
?
You likely credit natural causes daily—your food for satiety, your alarm for waking. But have you ever paused mid-thought to expose this as a deception? Will you commit to a week of verbal correction, saying 'Allah gave me satiety' instead of 'the food filled me'? What resistance do you feel?

The Real Meaning of 'Maker': Refuting the Redefinition of God's Agency
Ghazali rejects the philosophers' redefinition of 'agent' as mere cause, calling it a verbal deception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali insists that true agency (فعل) must proceed from will, and why redefining it as mere causality is a verbal deception that undermines the Islamic concept of divine action.

Core Idea
Agency without will is not agency—it's empty words.

In this passage, Ghazali confronts the philosophers who try to preserve the language of God as 'maker' while stripping it of its essential meaning. He shows that using the word 'agent' to mean nothing more than 'cause' is a deception that undermines the Islamic understanding of divine action. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the philosophers' redefinition of 'agent' as cause, and their analogy of the sun and light.
Notice how they claim that if the opponent refuses the term, there's no dispute after the meaning is clear.
2
Identify Ghazali's response
Focus on Ghazali's counter: true action and making require will. He rejects the redefinition outright.
3
Note the accusation of deception
Ghazali accuses the philosophers of using the word 'agent' while negating its reality, which is a pretense (تجملاً) to appear Islamic.
He calls this verbal deception (تلبيس).
4
Grasp the stakes
Ghazali demands that philosophers openly say God has no action, so that the contradiction with Islam is clear. The whole aim is to expose this deception.
This shows the seriousness of the issue for Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : نحن نعنى بكون الله تعالى فاعلا ، أنه سبب لوجود كل موجود سواه ، وأن العالم قوامه به ، ولولا وجود البارى تعالى لما تصور وجود العالم ولو قدر عدم البارى سبحانه لانعدم العالم ، كما لو قدر عدم الشمس لانعدم الضوء ، فهذا ما نعنيه بكونه تعالى فاعلا ، فإن كان الخصم يأبى أن يسمى المعنى فعلاً ، فلا مشاحة فى الأسامي ، بعد ظهور المعنى . قلنا : غرضنا أن نبين أن هذا المعنى لا يسمى فعلاً وصنعاً ، وإنما المعنى بالفعل والصنع ما يصدر عن الإرادة حقيقة ، وقد نفيتم حقيقة معنى الفعل ونطقتم بلفظه تجملاً بالإسلاميين ، ولا يتم الدين بإطلاق الألفاظ الفارغة عن المعانى ، فصرحوا بأن الله سبحانه وتعالى لا فعل له ، حتى يتضح أن معتقدكم مخالف لدين المسلمين ، ولا تلبسوا بأن الله تعالى صانع العالم ، وأن العالم صنعه ، فإن هذه لفظة أطلقتموها ، ونفيتم حقيقتها ، والمقصود من هذه المسألة الكشف عن هذا التلبيس فقط .
For if it is said: We mean by God being an agent that He is the cause of the existence of every existent other than Himself, and that the world subsists by Him, and were it not for the existence of the Creator (al-Bārī), the existence of the world would not be conceivable, and if the non-existence of the Creator were supposed, the world would cease to exist, just as if the non-existence of the sun were supposed, light would cease—this is what we mean by His being an agent. If the opponent refuses to call this meaning 'action', there is no dispute over names after the meaning is clear. We say: Our aim is to show that this meaning is not called 'action' and 'making'; rather, the meaning of 'action' and 'making' is that which proceeds from will in truth. You have negated the true meaning of action and uttered its word as a pretense to the Muslims. Religion is not accomplished by uttering words empty of meaning. So declare clearly that God has no action, so that it becomes clear that your belief is contrary to the religion of Muslims. And do not deceive by saying that God is the maker of the world and that the world is His work, for this is a word you have uttered but negated its reality. The aim of this discussion is merely to expose this deception.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The philosophers (the 'opponent') object by redefining what they mean by God being an 'agent' (فاعل): they mean that God is simply the cause (سبب) of the existence of everything other than Himself, and that the world depends on Him. They give an analogy: just as light depends on the sun, the world depends on God. If the opponent (Ghazali) refuses to call this meaning 'action', then there is no dispute over terms after the meaning is clear. Ghazali responds: his purpose is to show that this meaning is not rightly called 'action' (فعل) or 'making' (صنع). The true meaning of these terms is that which proceeds from will (إرادة). He accuses the philosophers of negating the true meaning of 'action' while using the word as a pretense (تجملاً) to appear in agreement with Muslims. Religion is not upheld by uttering empty words. He demands that they explicitly state that God has no action, so that their belief is clearly seen to contradict the religion of Muslims. He warns them not to deceive by saying that God is the maker of the world and that the world is His work, because they have uttered the word but negated its reality. The whole aim, Ghazali says, is to expose this deception (تلبيس).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Redefining 'agent' to mean merely 'cause' preserves the Islamic concept of God as creator.
Correction: Ghazali insists that true agency requires will; without it, the term is empty and deceives.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali reject the philosophers' definition of God as an 'agent' meaning simply 'cause'?
A) Because God cannot be a cause
B) Because the word 'agent' should be reserved for voluntary action, and they have negated its true meaning
C) Because they deny the existence of the world
D) Because they use the term without understanding it

Key Takeaway
Redefining 'agent' to mean mere cause empties the term of its Islamic meaning; true agency requires will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used Islamic terminology—like 'Allah is the Creator'—while in your heart you think of Him as merely a first cause, without active will? This lesson demands that your words match the true meaning. Will you examine your own understanding of God's agency and repent from any empty words?
?
The philosophers pretended to agree with Muslims by using the word 'agent' while redefining it. Where in your life do you use religious language to appear faithful, but your actions or beliefs contradict its true meaning? What specific change will you make today to align your speech with reality?
?
Ghazali says religion is not upheld by uttering words empty of meaning. When you say 'God is the Maker of the world,' do you truly believe He created with will and choice, or do you merely repeat the phrase? What would it cost you to live as if God's agency is real and personal?

Exposing the Empty Use of 'Act' by the Philosophers
Ghazali accuses philosophers of using 'act' verbally while denying its true meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the philosophers' claim that God is an agent is merely verbal, without the reality of action, and why he demands they admit their true position.

Core Idea
Using a term while denying its meaning is deception, not theology.

In this passage, Ghazali concludes his critique of the philosophers' concept of divine action. He has already established that true action requires will. Now he confronts the philosophers directly: you use the word 'act' but you reject its reality. He warns that such empty terminology cannot uphold the religion and demands they openly state their contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Ghazali frames his accusation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify key terms
Look for the terms 'act' (فعل), 'maker' (صانع), 'will' (إرادة), and 'deception' (تلبيس). Understand how Ghazali uses them.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Trace the argument's flow
Ghazali first restates his definition of act (issuing from will). Then he addresses the opponent: if you refuse to call that 'act', no quarrel. But his real point is that they deny the reality of act while using the term. He demands they explicitly say God has no act.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ghazali states the purpose is to 'uncover this deception'. Consider why he considers this important for the integrity of Islamic belief.

هذا الضوء ، فهذا ما نعنيه بكونه تعالى فاعلا ، فإن كان الخصم يأبى أن يسمى المعنى فعلاً ، فلا مشاحة فى الأسامي ، بعد ظهور المعنى قلنا : غرضنا أن نبين أن هذا المعنى لا يسمى فعلاً وصنعاً ، وإنما المعنى بالفعل والصنع ما يصدر عن الإرادة حقيقة ، وقد نفيتم حقيقة معنى الفعل ونطقتم بلفظه تجملاً بالإسلاميين ، ولا يتم الدين بإطلاق الألفاظ الفارغة عن المعانى ، فصرحوا بأن الله سبحانه وتعالى لا فعل له ، حتى يتضح حتى يتضح أن معتقدكم مخالف لدين المسلمين ، ولا تلبسوا بأن الله تعالى صانع العالم ، وأن العالم صنعه ، فإن هذه لفظة أطلقتموها ، ونفيتم حقيقتها ، والمقصود من هذه المسألة الكشف عن هذا التلبيس فقط
This light, so this is what we mean by His being, exalted, an agent. So if the opponent refuses to call the meaning 'act', then no quarrel over names after the meaning is clear. We said: our purpose is to show that this meaning is not called 'act' and 'making'. Rather, the meaning of 'act' and 'making' is what issues from will truly. And you have denied the reality of the meaning of act and uttered its term only to flatter the Muslims. And religion is not perfected by uttering words empty of meanings. So declare that Allah, glorified and exalted, has no act, so that it becomes clear that your belief contradicts the religion of Muslims. And do not deceive [by saying] that Allah, exalted, is the Maker of the world and that the world is His making, for this is a term you have uttered while denying its reality. And the purpose of this discussion is only to uncover this deception.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a refutational argument targeting the philosophers who claim God is an agent but deny the true meaning of action. Ghazali begins by stating that he has already clarified what he means by God being an agent—namely, that the meaning of 'act' is what proceeds from will. If the opponent refuses to call that meaning 'act', there is no dispute over names once the meaning is clear. However, Ghazali's point is that the philosophers actually deny the very reality of the meaning of 'act', yet they use the term 'act' and 'maker' to flatter the Muslims. He insists that religion is not upheld by empty words, so they must explicitly state that God has no act, thereby revealing the contradiction between their belief and Islam. He warns against the deception of calling God the 'Maker' while denying the true sense of making, concluding that the whole aim of this issue is simply to expose this disguise.

Why an Eternal World Cannot Be God's Act
Ghazali presents the second argument: the world is eternal, so cannot be an act requiring origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that an eternal world cannot be considered an act of God, based on the definition of 'act' as origination.

Core Idea
An act requires bringing something from non-existence to existence; an eternal world fails that condition.

This lesson presents Ghazali's second argument against the philosophers who claim the world is God's act while also asserting its eternity. Ghazali exposes a contradiction by unpacking the very meaning of 'act': to act is to originate, to bring forth from nothing. If the world has always existed, it cannot have been originated, so it cannot be an act. Let's examine this logical knockout punch.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: condition, definition, application, conclusion.
The key term is 'إحداث' (origination).
2
Identify the condition for an act
According to Ghazali, what is the essential condition for something to be called an act?
It must be an act of bringing into existence.
3
Apply the condition to the world
The philosophers say the world is eternal (qadīm). Does an eternal thing meet the condition of being originated?
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Why does Ghazali end with exalting God above their saying? What does this reveal about his view of the philosophers' claim?
This shows the seriousness of the error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في إبطال كون العالم فعلا الله تعالى على أصلهم ، لانعدام الشرط في الفعل ، وهو أن الفعل عبارة عن الإحداث ، والعالم عندهم قديم ، وليس بحادث ، ومعنى الفعل إخراج الشيء من العدم إلى الوجود بإحداثه ، وذلك لا يتصور في القديم ، إذ الموجود لا يمكن إيجاده ، فإذن شرط الفعل أن يكون حادثاً ، والعالم قديم عندهم ، فكيف يكون فعلا الله ، تعالى عن قولهم علواً كبيراً .
The second way in invalidating the world's being an act of Allah, exalted, according to their principle, due to the absence of the condition for an act. And that is that 'act' is a term for bringing into existence (al-iḥdāth). And the world, according to them, is eternal (qadīm), not originated (ḥādith). And the meaning of 'act' is bringing something out of non-existence into existence by originating it. And that is not conceivable for the eternal, because what exists cannot be brought into existence. So then the condition for an act is that it be originated. And the world is eternal according to them. So how could it be an act of Allah? Exalted be He above their saying, a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a second argument refuting the philosophers' claim that the world is God's act. Ghazali states that the condition for an act is that it be an act of origination (iḥdāth), meaning bringing something from non-existence to existence. However, according to the philosophers' own principle, the world is eternal (qadīm), not originated (ḥādith). Since what already exists cannot be originated, the world cannot be an act of God. Therefore, the philosophers contradict themselves: they call the world God's act while denying the very condition of being an act. Ghazali concludes by exalting God above their false assertion.

إحداث
قديم
حادث
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'act' can mean something other than bringing into existence from non-existence.
Correction: Here Ghazali defines 'act' strictly as origination (iḥdāth), so an eternal thing cannot be an act.

Checkpoint
What is the condition for something to be called an 'act' according to Ghazali in this passage?
A) It must be eternal.
B) It must be originated, i.e., brought from non-existence to existence.
C) It must be willed by God.
D) It must be material.

Key Takeaway
An act is defined as bringing something from non-existence to existence; an eternal world, never having been non-existent, cannot be an act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God created the world,' do you truly mean He brought it from absolute nothingness, or do you sometimes imagine Him working with pre-existing materials? Examine your deepest assumptions: has this lesson exposed any hidden belief that something exists eternally alongside God?
?
If you truly believe that every action of God is an act of origination from nothing, how does that affect the way you view your own existence? Are you living as someone who was brought from non-existence into existence, and could be returned to non-existence in an instant?
?
The philosophers claimed the world is eternal, thus not a true act of God. Have you ever, by how you live, treated the world as if it exists on its own, independent of God's constant creative act? What would change today if you saw every moment as a fresh act of creation?

The Philosopher's Objection: What Does the Agent Bring About?
The objector presents a dilemma regarding the agent's relation to existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosopher's argument that the agent's act relates only to existence, and why this would imply an eternal act if existence is eternal.

Core Idea
The philosopher argues that the agent's relation is solely to existence, which if eternal, makes the act eternal.

This passage presents a philosophical objection against the necessity of temporal origination. The objector begins by defining 'originated' and then examines what exactly issues from the agent—existence, knowledge, or both. He eliminates alternatives to conclude that only existence relates to the agent, setting the stage for his challenge: if existence is eternal, the relation is eternal, implying an eternal act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from the definition to the conclusion
Read the Arabic text from 'و فإن قيل' to 'لم يتعلق العدم بالفاعل بحال'. This covers the objection. Then read the translation.
Notice how the objector uses logical elimination.
2
Identify the three alternatives
The objector considers three possibilities: existence, pure knowledge, or both. Note why he rejects the second and third.
The rejection of 'both' relies on the fact that non-existence is not related to the agent.
3
Understand the implication of perpetual existence
If existence is eternal, the relation is eternal, and the agent would be perpetually active. Why is this problematic for the claim of temporal origination?
This leads to the conclusion that origination requires something more than mere existence.

و فإن قيل : معنى الحادث « الموجود بعد عدم » ، فلنبحث أن الفاعل إذا أحدث ، أكان الصادر منه المتعلق به ، الوجود ، أو العلم المجرد ، أو كليهما ؟ ! ، وباطل أن يقال : إن المتعلق به العدم السابق إذ لا تأثير للفاعل فى العدم، وباطل أن يقال : كلاهما ، إذ بان أن العدم لا يتعلق به أصلا، وأن العدم في كونه عدماً لا يحتاج إلى فاعل البتة ، فبقى أنه متعلق به من حيث إنه موجود ، وأن الصادر منه مجرد الوجود ، وأنه لا نسبة إليه إلا الوجود ، فإن فرض الوجود دائماً فرضت النسبة دائمة ، وإذا دامت هذه النسبة ، كان (۲) المنسوب إليه أفعل (۱) وأدوم تأثيراً ، لأنه لم يتعلق العدم بالفاعل بحال . بقى ) أن يقال : إنه متعلق به ، من حيث إنه حادث ، ولا معنى لكونه حادثاً ، إلا أنه موجود بعد عدم ، والعدم لم يتعلق به ، فإن جعل سبق العدم وصفاً للوجود ، وقيل المتعلق به وجود مخصوص ، لا كل وجود ، وهو وجود مسبوق بالعدم ؛ فيقال : كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم ليس من فعل الفاعل ، وصنع الصانع ؛ فإن هذا الوجود لا يتصور صدوره من الفاعل إلا والعدم سابق عليه ، وسبق العدم ليس (۱) وفى نسخة « أفضل » (۲) لعله اعتراض أجراه الفيلسوف على لسانه ضد نفسه ليدفعه فيصفو له المقام ، وفى العبارة ركة لا تخفى
And if it is said: The meaning of 'originated' (al-ḥādith) is 'the existent after non-existence'. Let us investigate: when the agent brings into existence, is that which issues from him and to which [the act] relates existence, or pure knowledge, or both? It is false to say that [the act] relates to prior non-existence, because the agent has no effect on non-existence. And it is false to say both, because it is clear that non-existence is not related to [the act] at all, and that non-existence, in its being non-existence, does not need an agent at all. So it remains that [the act] relates to [the effect] insofar as it exists, and that which issues from him is mere existence, and that there is no relation to him except existence. If existence is assumed perpetual, the relation is assumed perpetual. And when this relation is perpetual, what is related to him is more active and more enduring in effect, because non-existence has never been related to the agent. It remains to be said: It relates to him insofar as it is originated, and the meaning of its being originated is only that it exists after non-existence. And non-existence has not been related to him. Then if the priority of non-existence is made a description of existence, and it is said that what relates to him is a specific existence, not all existence, and it is an existence preceded by non-existence; then it is said: Its being preceded by non-existence is not from the act of the agent and the making of the maker. For this existence is not conceived as issuing from the agent unless non-existence is prior to it. And the priority of non-existence is not [from the agent] (1) And in one copy: 'afḍal' (better). (2) Perhaps it is an objection that the philosopher advances on his own tongue against himself in order to refute it and thus clear his position, and in the expression there is an obscurity not hidden.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer dialectical unit. The objector (likely the philosopher) begins by defining 'originated' as existent after non-existence. Then he investigates what exactly issues from the agent: is it existence, knowledge, or both? He argues that non-existence is not affected by the agent, and existence is the only thing related. If existence is eternal, the relation is eternal, and the agent is perpetually active. The answer (marked by 'yuqāl' or 'fa-yuqāl') then refines the response: the agent's act relates to existence, but specifically to an existence that is preceded by non-existence. However, the priority of non-existence itself is not from the agent; rather, the agent brings into existence only after non-existence. Thus, the condition of origination remains essential. The passage ends with a marginal note suggesting the objection is a self-refutation by the philosopher, and noting an obscure expression.

Checkpoint
Why does the objector reject the possibility that the agent's act relates to both existence and non-existence?
A) Because non-existence is not affected by the agent.
B) Because the agent cannot create both at the same time.
C) Because existence and non-existence are opposites.
D) Because the agent only creates knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The philosopher's objection reduces the agent's relation to existence alone, threatening the need for temporal origination if existence is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the agent's act relates only to existence, what does that imply about your own actions? Do you truly 'create' anything, or do you only rearrange what already exists? How does this affect your understanding of God as the sole Creator?
?
The philosopher argues that if existence is eternal, the agent must be perpetually active. Do you live as if God is perpetually active in your life, or do you fall into the illusion of a distant, inactive Creator? What would change if you truly believed every moment of your existence is directly sustained by Him?
?
Non-existence requires no agent. When you consider your own death—a return to non-existence if God wills—does this challenge your sense of self-sufficiency? How does the fact that only existence depends on God reshape your daily reliance on Him?

Ghazali's Answer: Origination Requires Priority of Non-Existence
Ghazali gives the answer, specifying that the agent's act relates to existence insofar as it is originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's response that the agent brings about existence preceded by non-existence, and that the priority of non-existence is not itself caused by the agent.

Core Idea
The agent brings about existence after non-existence, but the 'after' is not an act of the agent.

In this part, Ghazali provides the answer to the previous objection. He refines the relation: the agent relates to existence not in an absolute sense, but specifically to an existence that is preceded by non-existence. He then argues that the priority of non-existence itself is not from the agent, but is a condition of the act. This preserves the necessity of temporal origination without attributing non-existence to the agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic from 'بقى ) أن يقال' to the end. Read the translation carefully.
Look for the phrase 'بقى ) أن يقال' which introduces the answer.
2
Identify the key refinement
The answer says the agent relates to existence 'min ḥaythu innahu ḥādith' – insofar as it is originated. What does this add?
It qualifies existence as being preceded by non-existence.
3
Understand the status of the priority of non-existence
The answer clarifies that 'being preceded by non-existence' is not an act of the agent. Why is this important?
It prevents the need to posit an agent for non-existence.
