The Condition of an Act: The Eternal Cannot Be an Act of God
Ghazali states the condition of an act and refutes the philosophers' claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that an eternal world cannot be considered an act of God, based on the definition of 'act' as bringing into existence.

Core Idea
An act means bringing into existence; the eternal already exists and cannot be 'brought into existence'.

Ghazali begins his second refutation by stating a fundamental condition: for something to be an act, it must be brought into existence—it must be originated. The philosophers, however, claim the world is eternal and pre-existing. In this lesson, we examine Ghazali's logical argument that the eternal world cannot, by definition, be an act of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Ghazali defines 'act' (fiʿl) and 'originated' (ḥādith).
This is a short, dense argument. Pay attention to the logical steps.
2
Identify the condition
Identify the condition Ghazali sets for an act: that it be originated, meaning brought from non-existence to existence. See how he contrasts this with the philosophers' view of the world as eternal.
3
Understand the implication
Reflect on why the eternal cannot satisfy this condition: because what already exists cannot be 'brought into existence' again. This makes the philosophers' claim self-contradictory.
Ghazali concludes with a doxological exclamation to emphasize the gravity of the error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في إبطال كون العالم فعلا الله تعالى على أصلهم، لانعدام الشرط في الفعل، وهو أن الفعل عبارة عن الإحداث، والعالم عندهم قديم، وليس بحادث، ومعنى الفعل إخراج الشيء من العدم إلى الوجود بإحداثه، وذلك لا يتصور في القديم، إذ الموجود لا يمكن إيجاده، فإذن شرط الفعل أن يكون حادثاً، والعالم قديم عندهم، فكيف يكون فعلا الله، تعالى عن قولهم علواً كبيراً.

فإن قيل: معنى الحادث «الموجود بعد عدم»، فلنبحث أن الفاعل إذا أحدث، أكان الصادر منه المتعلق به، الوجود، أو العلم المجرد، أو كليهما؟!، وباطل أن يقال: إن المتعلق به العدم السابق إذ لا تأثير للفاعل في العدم، وباطل أن يقال: كلاهما، إذ بان أن العدم لا يتعلق به أصلا، وأن العدم في كونه عدماً لا يحتاج إلى فاعل البتة، فبقى أنه متعلق به من حيث إنه موجود، وأن الصادر منه مجرد الوجود، وأنه لا نسبة إليه إلا الوجود، فإن فرض الوجود دائماً فرضت النسبة دائمة، وإذا دامت هذه النسبة، كان المنسوب إليه أفعل وأدوم تأثيراً، لأنه لم يتعلق العدم بالفاعل بحال. بقى أن يقال: إنه متعلق به، من حيث إنه حادث، ولا معنى لكونه حادثاً، إلا أنه موجود بعد عدم، والعدم لم يتعلق به، فإن جعل سبق العدم وصفاً للوجود، وقيل المتعلق به وجود مخصوص، لا كل وجود، وهو وجود مسبوق بالعدم؛ فيقال: كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم ليس من فعل الفاعل، وصنع الصانع؛ فإن هذا الوجود لا يتصور صدوره من الفاعل إلا والعدم سابق عليه، وسبق العدم ليس بفعل الفاعل، فكونه مسبوقاً بعدم ليس بفعل الفاعل فلا تعلق له به، فاشتراطه في كونه فعلا، اشتراط ما لا تأثير للفاعل فيه بحال.
The second aspect: Regarding abolishing [the claim] that the world is an act of God, according to their principle, due to the absence of the condition in the act, which is that 'act' is a term for bringing into existence (al-iḥdāth), and the world according to them is eternal (qadīm), and is not originated (ḥādith). And the meaning of 'act' is bringing a thing out from non-existence into existence by initiating it (bi-iḥdāthihi), and that is inconceivable in the eternal, because the existent cannot be brought into existence (lā yumkin ījāduhu). So then the condition of an act is that it be originated (ḥādithan), and the world is eternal according to them, so how can it be an act of God? Exalted is God above their saying, a great exaltation.

If it is said: The meaning of 'originated' is 'that which exists after non-existence'. Let us investigate: when the agent brings into existence, what proceeds from him that is related to him? Is it existence, or mere knowledge, or both? It is false to say that the related thing is the prior non-existence, for the agent has no effect on non-existence. And it is false to say both, because it has become clear that non-existence is not related to him at all, and that non-existence as non-existence does not need an agent at all. So it remains that it is related to him insofar as it is existent, and that which proceeds from him is mere existence, and that there is no relation to him except existence. So if existence is assumed to be perpetual, the relation is assumed perpetual. And when this relation is perpetual, that to which it is related is more active and more permanently effective, because non-existence has not been related to the agent at any time. It remains to be said: It is related to him insofar as it is originated, and there is no meaning to its being originated except that it is existent after non-existence, and non-existence has not been related to him. So if priority of non-existence is made a description of existence, and it is said: the related thing is a particular existence, not every existence, and it is an existence preceded by non-existence; then it is said: its being preceded by non-existence is not from the act of the agent and the making of the Maker, for this existence cannot be conceived to proceed from the agent unless non-existence precedes it, and the priority of non-existence is not an act of the agent. So its being preceded by non-existence is not an act of the agent, so there is no relation to him with it. So stipulating it in its being an act is stipulating something in which the agent has no effect at all.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (ibṭāl) of the philosophers' claim that the world is an act of God while also being eternal. It begins by stating the condition: an act (fiʿl) means bringing into existence (iḥdāth), which requires the thing to be originated (ḥādith) — coming from non-existence to existence. The eternal cannot be brought into existence because it already exists. Therefore, an eternal world cannot be God's act. Then Ghazali presents an objection from the philosophers: they argue that an act relates only to existence, not to non-existence. If existence is perpetual, the relation is perpetual, so the world can be an eternal act. Ghazali's answer is that the philosophers' argument fails because the relation of an act to its agent requires that the existence be preceded by non-existence. This priority of non-existence is not an act of the agent but is a necessary condition for something to be an act. Without this condition, the concept of 'act' loses its meaning. Thus, stipulating prior non-existence is not adding a condition that the agent effects, but recognizing a logical requirement for the relation of act to agent.

Key Terms
فعل (fiʿl)
إحداث (iḥdāth)
حادث (ḥādith)
قديم (qadīm)
عدم (ʿadam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think an act can be eternal if God is eternally active.
Correction: Ghazali argues that an act by definition involves bringing into existence; an eternal thing has never not existed, so it cannot be an act.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the essential condition for something to be considered an act (fiʿl)?
A) That it is eternal (qadīm)
B) That it is originated (ḥādith)
C) That it exists perpetually
D) That it is related to the agent's will

Key Takeaway
An eternal world cannot be an act of God because an act requires origination, but the eternal is unoriginated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that every created thing, including yourself, was brought into existence from nothing by God's act, how does that change the way you see your own life and its purpose? Are you living as if you are self-made, or as a constant recipient of God's creative act?
?
When you consider that the world is an act of God, not a self-existing reality, does that make you feel more dependent on Him? In what areas of your life are you still acting as if something is eternal besides God, like your status, your health, or your relationships?
?
Ghazali insists that an eternal world cannot be an act. How does this challenge your own tendency to think of your habits, sins, or successes as permanent? Are there things in your life you treat as 'eternal' that need to be recognized as contingent and reliant on God's continuous act of sustaining?

Answering the Philosophers' Objection: The Role of Prior Non-Existence
Ghazali answers the objection by clarifying the role of prior non-existence in the concept of act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's response to the philosophers' objection that an act relates only to existence, and why prior non-existence is a necessary condition for an act.

Core Idea
Prior non-existence is not an effect of the agent but a necessary condition for an act.

The philosophers object that an act relates only to existence, so if existence is perpetual, the relation is perpetual. Ghazali's response is subtle: he argues that for something to be an act, the existence must be preceded by non-existence. This priority of non-existence is not something the agent produces, but it is a logical prerequisite. Without it, the concept of 'act' collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
First, read the philosophers' objection: they argue that the agent's act relates only to existence, not to non-existence, so if existence is eternal, the relation is eternal, and the world can be an eternal act.
Note how they reduce the act to a relation (nisba) to existence.
2
Trace Ghazali's answer
Ghazali agrees that non-existence is not related to the agent. But he then asks: what is the relation? It is to existence. However, he insists that the existence must be 'originated' (ḥādith), meaning preceded by non-existence.
He distinguishes between the existence itself and its being preceded by non-existence.
3
Understand the key point
The priority of non-existence (sabq al-ʿadam) is not an effect of the agent; it is a necessary condition for the existence to be an act. Stipulating it is not adding an extra condition on the agent but recognizing the logical structure of act.
This answers the objection without attributing any effect on non-existence to the agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في إبطال كون العالم فعلا الله تعالى على أصلهم، لانعدام الشرط في الفعل، وهو أن الفعل عبارة عن الإحداث، والعالم عندهم قديم، وليس بحادث، ومعنى الفعل إخراج الشيء من العدم إلى الوجود بإحداثه، وذلك لا يتصور في القديم، إذ الموجود لا يمكن إيجاده، فإذن شرط الفعل أن يكون حادثاً، والعالم قديم عندهم، فكيف يكون فعلا الله، تعالى عن قولهم علواً كبيراً.

فإن قيل: معنى الحادث «الموجود بعد عدم»، فلنبحث أن الفاعل إذا أحدث، أكان الصادر منه المتعلق به، الوجود، أو العلم المجرد، أو كليهما؟!، وباطل أن يقال: إن المتعلق به العدم السابق إذ لا تأثير للفاعل في العدم، وباطل أن يقال: كلاهما، إذ بان أن العدم لا يتعلق به أصلا، وأن العدم في كونه عدماً لا يحتاج إلى فاعل البتة، فبقى أنه متعلق به من حيث إنه موجود، وأن الصادر منه مجرد الوجود، وأنه لا نسبة إليه إلا الوجود، فإن فرض الوجود دائماً فرضت النسبة دائمة، وإذا دامت هذه النسبة، كان المنسوب إليه أفعل وأدوم تأثيراً، لأنه لم يتعلق العدم بالفاعل بحال. بقى أن يقال: إنه متعلق به، من حيث إنه حادث، ولا معنى لكونه حادثاً، إلا أنه موجود بعد عدم، والعدم لم يتعلق به، فإن جعل سبق العدم وصفاً للوجود، وقيل المتعلق به وجود مخصوص، لا كل وجود، وهو وجود مسبوق بالعدم؛ فيقال: كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم ليس من فعل الفاعل، وصنع الصانع؛ فإن هذا الوجود لا يتصور صدوره من الفاعل إلا والعدم سابق عليه، وسبق العدم ليس بفعل الفاعل، فكونه مسبوقاً بعدم ليس بفعل الفاعل فلا تعلق له به، فاشتراطه في كونه فعلا، اشتراط ما لا تأثير للفاعل فيه بحال.
The second aspect: Regarding abolishing [the claim] that the world is an act of God, according to their principle, due to the absence of the condition in the act, which is that 'act' is a term for bringing into existence (al-iḥdāth), and the world according to them is eternal (qadīm), and is not originated (ḥādith). And the meaning of 'act' is bringing a thing out from non-existence into existence by initiating it (bi-iḥdāthihi), and that is inconceivable in the eternal, because the existent cannot be brought into existence (lā yumkin ījāduhu). So then the condition of an act is that it be originated (ḥādithan), and the world is eternal according to them, so how can it be an act of God? Exalted is God above their saying, a great exaltation.

If it is said: The meaning of 'originated' is 'that which exists after non-existence'. Let us investigate: when the agent brings into existence, what proceeds from him that is related to him? Is it existence, or mere knowledge, or both? It is false to say that the related thing is the prior non-existence, for the agent has no effect on non-existence. And it is false to say both, because it has become clear that non-existence is not related to him at all, and that non-existence as non-existence does not need an agent at all. So it remains that it is related to him insofar as it is existent, and that which proceeds from him is mere existence, and that there is no relation to him except existence. So if existence is assumed to be perpetual, the relation is assumed perpetual. And when this relation is perpetual, that to which it is related is more active and more permanently effective, because non-existence has not been related to the agent at any time. It remains to be said: It is related to him insofar as it is originated, and there is no meaning to its being originated except that it is existent after non-existence, and non-existence has not been related to him. So if priority of non-existence is made a description of existence, and it is said: the related thing is a particular existence, not every existence, and it is an existence preceded by non-existence; then it is said: its being preceded by non-existence is not from the act of the agent and the making of the Maker, for this existence cannot be conceived to proceed from the agent unless non-existence precedes it, and the priority of non-existence is not an act of the agent. So its being preceded by non-existence is not an act of the agent, so there is no relation to him with it. So stipulating it in its being an act is stipulating something in which the agent has no effect at all.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (ibṭāl) of the philosophers' claim that the world is an act of God while also being eternal. It begins by stating the condition: an act (fiʿl) means bringing into existence (iḥdāth), which requires the thing to be originated (ḥādith) — coming from non-existence to existence. The eternal cannot be brought into existence because it already exists. Therefore, an eternal world cannot be God's act. Then Ghazali presents an objection from the philosophers: they argue that an act relates only to existence, not to non-existence. If existence is perpetual, the relation is perpetual, so the world can be an eternal act. Ghazali's answer is that the philosophers' argument fails because the relation of an act to its agent requires that the existence be preceded by non-existence. This priority of non-existence is not an act of the agent but is a necessary condition for something to be an act. Without this condition, the concept of 'act' loses its meaning. Thus, stipulating prior non-existence is not adding a condition that the agent effects, but recognizing a logical requirement for the relation of act to agent.

Key Terms
نسبة (nisba)
وجود (wujūd)
عدم (ʿadam)
حادث (ḥādith)
سبق العدم (sabq al-ʿadam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an act relates only to existence, then an eternal existence can be an act without temporal prior non-existence.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the concept of 'act' logically requires prior non-existence as a condition, even though non-existence is not produced by the agent.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's response, why does stipulating prior non-existence not imply that the agent has an effect on non-existence?
A) Because the agent can control non-existence
B) Because prior non-existence is a necessary condition for the act, not an effect of the agent
C) Because non-existence does not exist
D) Because the agent is not involved in the world

Key Takeaway
Prior non-existence is not an effect of the agent but a necessary logical condition for something to be an act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's act of creation, do you truly feel that everything that came into being after nothing is a direct act of His will? How does that awareness change the way you see the world around you right now?
?
The philosophers thought an eternal act was more perfect. In your own life, do you ever value things that are constant and unchanging over things that have a clear beginning? What does that preference reveal about your understanding of God's power and creativity?
?
Ghazali insists that prior non-existence is not an effect of the agent. How does this apply to your own actions? When you create something, do you ever take credit for the 'absence' that preceded it? What would change if you saw your creative acts as merely bringing existence, while the condition of prior non-existence is beyond your control?

Clarifying the Meaning of Ījād: The Relation of Existentiation
Ghazali clarifies the meaning of 'bringing into existence' and defines ījād as the relation between agent and existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'bringing into existence' (ījād) is the concurrent relation between the agent and the existing thing, not an act directed at non-being.

Core Idea
Ījād is the relation of the existentiator to the existent, not an act upon non-existence.

Before addressing the builder analogy, Ghazali first clarifies what the philosophers mean by 'the existent cannot be brought into existence.' He distinguishes two possible interpretations: one about the renewal of existence after non-existence, and another about the dependence of the existent on an existentiator. This lesson unpacks that clarification and the crucial concept of ījād as a relation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and notice the two interpretations
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the two meanings of the philosophers' statement that Ghazali distinguishes.
Look for 'إن عنيتم به...' for the two interpretations.
2
Identify key terms: mawjūd, mūjid, ījād, nisba
These terms are central to Ghazali's clarification. Understand them in context.
Their definitions are given in the passage.
3
Understand why ījād is concurrent with existence
Ghazali argues that the agent is an existentiator only when the effect exists. The relation (nisba) is simultaneous.
4
Reflect on the importance of the relation for theology
This clarification prevents misunderstanding God's creative act as something done in non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولكم: إن الموجود لا يمكن إيجاده؛ إن عنيتم به أنه لا يستأنف له وجود بعد عدم، فصحيح، وإن عنيتم به أنه في حال كونه موجوداً، لا يكون موجوداً بموجد، فقد بينا أنه يكون موجوداً في حال كونه موجوداً، لا في حال كونه معدوماً، فإنه إنما يكون الشيء موجوداً، إذا كان الفاعل له موجداً، ولا يكون الفاعل موجداً في حال العدم، بل في حال وجود الشيء منه، والإيجاد مقارن لكون الفاعل موجداً، وكون المفعول موجوداً، لأنه عبارة عن نسبة الموجد إلى الموجود، وكل ذلك مع الوجود لا قبله، فإذن لا إيجاد إلا لموجود، إن كان المراد بالإيجاد النسبة التي بها يكون الفاعل موجداً، والمفعول موجوداً. قالوا: ولهذا قضينا بأن العالم فعل الله تعالى أزلاً وأبداً، وما من حال إلا وهو تعالى فاعل له، لأن المرتبط بالفاعل الوجود فإن دام الارتباط دام الوجود، وإن انقطع، انقطع، لا كما تخيلتموه من أن البارئ تعالى لو قدر عدمه، لبقى العالم، إذ ظننتم كالبناء مع البناء، فإنه ينعدم البناء، وبقى البناء، فإن بقاء البناء ليس بالباني، بل هو باليبوسة الممسكة لتركيبه، إذ لو لم تكن فيه قوة ممسكة كالماء مثلاً، لم يتصور بقاء الشكل الحادث بفعل الفاعل فيه. والجواب: أن الفعل يتعلق بالفاعل من حيث حدوثه، لا من حيث عدمه السابق ولا من حيث كونه موجوداً فقط، فإنه لا يتعلق به في ثاني حال الوجود عندنا. وهو موجود – بل يتعلق به في حال حدوثه، من حيث إنه حدوث وخروج من العدم إلى الوجود، فإن نفى عنه معنى الحدوث، لم يعقل كونه فعلا، ولا عقل تعلقه بالفاعل. وقولكم إن كونه حادثاً، يرجع إلى كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم، وكونه مسبوقاً بالعدم، ليس من فعل الفاعل وجعل الجاعل، فهو كذلك، لكنه شرط في كون الوجود فعل الفاعل - أعني كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم.
And as for your statement: 'The existent cannot be brought into existence.' If you mean by it that existence is not renewed for it after non-existence, then it is correct. And if you mean by it that while it is existent, it is not existent by an existentiator (mūjid), then we have shown that it becomes existent while it is existent, not while it is non-existent. For a thing only becomes existent when the agent is an existentiator for it, and the agent is not an existentiator in the state of non-existence, but rather in the state of the thing's existence from him. And bringing into existence (ījād) is concurrent with the agent's being an existentiator and the effect's being existent, because it is an expression of the relation of the existentiator to the existent. And all of that is with existence, not before it. So then [there is] no bringing into existence except for an existent, if what is meant by bringing into existence is the relation by which the agent is an existentiator and the effect is existent.

They said: And for this we have judged that the world is an act of God eternally (azalan) and always, and there is no state except that He, exalted, is an agent for it, because what is connected to the agent is existence. So if the connection persists, existence persists, and if it is severed, it is severed, not as you imagined that the Creator, exalted, if He willed its non-existence, the world would remain, for you assumed [it] like the builder with the building: the builder ceases to exist while the building remains, but the building's remaining is not due to the builder, but due to the dryness that holds its composition together. For if there were no holding force in it, like water for example, the permanence of the originated shape by the act of the agent could not be conceived.

And the answer: That the act is related to the agent in terms of its origination (ḥudūth), not in terms of its prior non-existence nor in terms of its being existent only, for it is not related to him in the second state of existence according to us – while it is existent – but it is related to him in the state of its origination, in terms of its being origination and coming out from non-existence to existence. For if the meaning of origination is denied from it, its being an act is not understood, nor is its relation to the agent understood. And your statement that its being originated reduces to its being preceded by non-existence, and its being preceded by non-existence is not from the act of the agent and the making of the Maker, is as you say, but it is a condition in the existence's being an act of the agent – I mean its being preceded by non-existence.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation by addressing another philosophical objection: the claim that 'the existent cannot be brought into existence' (al-mawjūd lā yumkin ījāduhu). Ghazali first clarifies the meaning: if they mean that existence is not renewed after non-existence, that is correct; but if they mean that while existent it does not depend on an existentiator (mūjid), that is false. In fact, existence is concurrent with the agent's act of causing existence; the relation (nisba) of existentiator to existent is what 'bringing into existence' (ījād) means. Therefore, ījād is always for an existent, not for a non-existent. The philosophers then argue: since the connection between agent and existence is eternal, the world is eternally God's act, and they give the analogy of a builder: when the builder ceases, the building remains due to internal cohesion, so the world could remain even if God ceased to will its existence. Ghazali's answer is that the act relates to the agent specifically in terms of origination (ḥudūth), not in terms of mere existence or prior non-existence. The act is the coming-into-existence from non-existence. If we remove origination, the concept of 'act' becomes unintelligible. The philosophers are correct that being preceded by non-existence is not itself an act of the agent, but it is a necessary condition (sharṭ) for something to be an act. Thus, the world cannot be an eternal act because origination is essential to the meaning of act.

Key Terms
موجود (mawjūd)
إيجاد (ījād)
موجد (mūjid)
نسبة (nisba)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that ījād is an act performed on non-existence, like creating from nothing.
Correction: Ghazali shows that ījād is a relation with the existent, not an act on non-being.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is ījād an expression of?
A) The act of creating from nothing
B) The relation of the existentiator to the existent
C) The renewal of existence after non-existence
D) The agent's power over the nonexistent

Key Takeaway
Ījād is the concurrent relation between the agent and the existent, not an act performed on non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence at this very moment is sustained by a concurrent relation from God (ījād), what does that demand from you in terms of gratitude and obedience right now? Are you living as if your existence is a past event or a present gift?
?
The passage teaches that ījād is not an act on non-existence but a relation with the existent. How does that change your understanding of God's closeness to you? Do you feel that closeness, and if not, what is blocking it?
?
If God's act of creation is a relation that exists only when you exist, then your continued existence depends on that relation every moment. What would it mean for you to consciously rely on God for your existence each morning? Will you commit to a prayer or dhikr that reminds you of this truth today?

The Philosophers' Builder Analogy and Ghazali's Answer on Ḥudūth
Ghazali presents and refutes the philosophers' argument that the world is an eternal act, using the builder analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the act of creation requires origination (ḥudūth) and why the builder analogy fails to support an eternal act.

Core Idea
The act relates to the agent through origination, not mere existence or prior non-existence.

After clarifying the meaning of ījād, Ghazali presents the philosophers' argument: since the connection between agent and existence is eternal, the world is an eternal act. They illustrate with a builder whose building remains after he ceases. Ghazali's answer cuts to the heart: an act is essentially a coming-into-existence; without origination, the concept of 'act' is unintelligible. This lesson follows that crucial exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument and their builder analogy
Read the passage starting from 'They said: And for this we have judged...' up to the analogy. Understand how they use the builder to argue for an eternal act.
The analogy compares God to a builder whose work persists after he stops.
2
Note how they argue for an eternal act
Because the connection between agent and existence is persistent, they conclude the world is eternally an act of God.
3
Read Ghazali's answer carefully
Focus on the shift: the act relates to the agent in terms of ḥudūth (origination), not existence alone. This is the key refutation.
Look for 'من حيث حدوثه'.
4
Understand the distinction between act and condition
Ghazali concedes that being preceded by non-existence is not the act itself, but a necessary condition (sharṭ) for an act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولكم: إن الموجود لا يمكن إيجاده؛ إن عنيتم به أنه لا يستأنف له وجود بعد عدم، فصحيح، وإن عنيتم به أنه في حال كونه موجوداً، لا يكون موجوداً بموجد، فقد بينا أنه يكون موجوداً في حال كونه موجوداً، لا في حال كونه معدوماً، فإنه إنما يكون الشيء موجوداً، إذا كان الفاعل له موجداً، ولا يكون الفاعل موجداً في حال العدم، بل في حال وجود الشيء منه، والإيجاد مقارن لكون الفاعل موجداً، وكون المفعول موجوداً، لأنه عبارة عن نسبة الموجد إلى الموجود، وكل ذلك مع الوجود لا قبله، فإذن لا إيجاد إلا لموجود، إن كان المراد بالإيجاد النسبة التي بها يكون الفاعل موجداً، والمفعول موجوداً. قالوا: ولهذا قضينا بأن العالم فعل الله تعالى أزلاً وأبداً، وما من حال إلا وهو تعالى فاعل له، لأن المرتبط بالفاعل الوجود فإن دام الارتباط دام الوجود، وإن انقطع، انقطع، لا كما تخيلتموه من أن البارئ تعالى لو قدر عدمه، لبقى العالم، إذ ظننتم كالبناء مع البناء، فإنه ينعدم البناء، وبقى البناء، فإن بقاء البناء ليس بالباني، بل هو باليبوسة الممسكة لتركيبه، إذ لو لم تكن فيه قوة ممسكة كالماء مثلاً، لم يتصور بقاء الشكل الحادث بفعل الفاعل فيه. والجواب: أن الفعل يتعلق بالفاعل من حيث حدوثه، لا من حيث عدمه السابق ولا من حيث كونه موجوداً فقط، فإنه لا يتعلق به في ثاني حال الوجود عندنا. وهو موجود – بل يتعلق به في حال حدوثه، من حيث إنه حدوث وخروج من العدم إلى الوجود، فإن نفى عنه معنى الحدوث، لم يعقل كونه فعلا، ولا عقل تعلقه بالفاعل. وقولكم إن كونه حادثاً، يرجع إلى كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم، وكونه مسبوقاً بالعدم، ليس من فعل الفاعل وجعل الجاعل، فهو كذلك، لكنه شرط في كون الوجود فعل الفاعل - أعني كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم.
And as for your statement: 'The existent cannot be brought into existence.' If you mean by it that existence is not renewed for it after non-existence, then it is correct. And if you mean by it that while it is existent, it is not existent by an existentiator (mūjid), then we have shown that it becomes existent while it is existent, not while it is non-existent. For a thing only becomes existent when the agent is an existentiator for it, and the agent is not an existentiator in the state of non-existence, but rather in the state of the thing's existence from him. And bringing into existence (ījād) is concurrent with the agent's being an existentiator and the effect's being existent, because it is an expression of the relation of the existentiator to the existent. And all of that is with existence, not before it. So then [there is] no bringing into existence except for an existent, if what is meant by bringing into existence is the relation by which the agent is an existentiator and the effect is existent.

They said: And for this we have judged that the world is an act of God eternally (azalan) and always, and there is no state except that He, exalted, is an agent for it, because what is connected to the agent is existence. So if the connection persists, existence persists, and if it is severed, it is severed, not as you imagined that the Creator, exalted, if He willed its non-existence, the world would remain, for you assumed [it] like the builder with the building: the builder ceases to exist while the building remains, but the building's remaining is not due to the builder, but due to the dryness that holds its composition together. For if there were no holding force in it, like water for example, the permanence of the originated shape by the act of the agent could not be conceived.

For if the meaning of origination is denied from it, its being an act is not understood, nor is its relation to the agent understood. And your statement that its being originated reduces to its being preceded by non-existence, and its being preceded by non-existence is not from the act of the agent and the making of the Maker, is as you say, but it is a condition in the existence's being an act of the agent – I mean its being preceded by non-existence.
Source pages p. 140

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an effect persists after the agent ceases, the act is eternal.

Checkpoint

A) Its existence
B) Its origination (ḥudūth)
C) Its permanence
D) Its being preceded by non-existence

Key Takeaway
An act is defined by origination; without ḥudūth, no relation between act and agent can be understood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you experience it as a fresh moment of existence coming from nothing, or as a mere continuation of habit? How would viewing each act as a new origination change your awareness of God as the constant Creator?
?
Ghazali insists that an act is only truly an act at the moment of its coming into being. What would it mean for your relationship with God if you realized that your very existence is being ‘act-ualized’ by Him at every instant? Are you living in that moment-by-moment dependence?
?
If being preceded by non-existence is a condition for something to be an act, then your entire life is a series of acts that could not have been without that prior nothingness. Does your daily gratitude reflect the weight of this? What would you do differently today to own your contingency?

Act and the Condition of Non-Existence
Ghazali argues that an act must be preceded by non-existence as a condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali insists that an act must be preceded by non-existence, and why this condition is not itself produced by the agent.

Core Idea
An act is defined by its origination from non-existence; the condition of prior non-existence is not an act of the agent.

This passage from the Tahafut addresses a fundamental dispute: can the world be an eternal act of God? The philosophers argue yes, but Ghazali counters that an act, by definition, must have a beginning. He carefully distinguishes between the condition of being preceded by non-existence and the act itself, showing that the condition is not something the agent produces.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let Ghazali's own argumentative rhythm come through.
Notice the back-and-forth with the philosophers.
2
Identify Ghazali's main claim
Find where Ghazali states that an act (fiʿl) is essentially tied to origination (ḥudūth). Highlight the sentence: 'The act is related to the agent in terms of its origination.'
3
Understand the refutation of the builder analogy
The philosophers compare God to a builder whose building remains after he is gone. Ghazali explains that the building's persistence is due to material properties, not the builder's act.
This analogy fails because God's act is not like a builder's.
4
Grasp the key distinction: condition vs. effect
Ghazali argues that being preceded by non-existence is a condition (sharṭ) for being an act, not an effect of the agent. Similarly, the agent's own attributes are conditions.
Not every necessary condition is produced by the agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بفعل الفاعل ، فكونه مسبوقاً بعدم ليس بفعل الفاعل فلا تعلق له به ، فاشتراطه في كونه فعلا ، اشتراط ما لا تأثير للفاعل فيه بحال . وأما قولكم : إن الموجود لا يمكن إيجاده ؛ إن عنيتم به أنه لا يستأنف له وجود بعد عدم، فصحيح، وإن عنيتم به أنه في حال كونه موجوداً ، لا يكون موجوداً بموجد ، فقد بينا أنه يكون موجوداً في حال كونه موجوداً ، لا في حال كونه معدوماً ، فإنه إنما يكون الشيء موجوداً ، إذا كان الفاعل له موجداً ، ولا يكون الفاعل موجداً في حال العدم ، بل في حال وجود الشيء منه ، والإيجاد مقارن لكون الفاعل موجداً ، وكون المفعول موجوداً ، لأنه عبارة عن نسبة الموجد إلى الموجود ، وكل ذلك مع الوجود لا قبله ، فإذن لا إيجاد إلا لموجود ، إن كان المراد بالإيجاد النسبة التي بها يكون الفاعل موجداً ، والمفعول موجوداً . قالوا : ولهذا قضينا بأن العالم فعل الله تعالى أزلاً وأبداً ، وما من حال إلا وهو تعالى فاعل له، لأن المرتبط بالفاعل الوجود فإن دام الارتباط دام الوجود ، وإن انقطع ، انقطع ، لا كما تخيلتموه من أن الباري تعالى لو قدر عدمه ، لبقى العالم ، إذ ظننتم كالبناء مع البناء ، فإنه ينعدم البناء ، وبقى البناء ، فإن بقاء البناء ليس بالباني ، بل هو باليبوسة الممسكة لتركيبه ، إذ لو لم تكن فيه قوة ممسكة كالماء مثلاً ، لم يتصور بقاء الشكل الحادث بفعل الفاعل فيه . والجواب : أن الفعل يتعلق بالفاعل من حيث حدوثه ، لا من حيث عدمه السابق ولا من حيث كونه موجوداً فقط ، فإنه لا يتعلق به في ثاني حال الوجود عندنا وهو موجود – بل يتعلق به في حال حدوثه ، من حيث إنه حدوث وخروج من العدم إلى الوجود ، فإن نفي عنه معنى الحدوث ، لم يعقل كونه فعلاً ، ولا عقل تعلقه بالفاعل . وقولكم إن كونه حادثاً ، يرجع إلى كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم ، وكونه مسبوقاً بالعدم، ليس من فعل الفاعل وجعل الجاعل ، فهو كذلك ، لكنه شرط في كون الوجود فعل الفاعل -- أعني كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم - فالوجود الذي ليس مسبوقاً بعدم ، بل هو دائم ، لا يصلح أن يكون فعلاً للفاعل ، وليس كل ما يشترط في كون الفعل فعلاً ينبغي أن يكون بفعل الفاعل ، فإن ذات الفاعل وعلمه وإرادته وقدرته ، شرط في كونه فاعلاً، وليس ذلك من أثر الفاعل ، ولكن لا يعقل فعل إلا من موجود ، فكان وجود الفاعل وعلمه وإرادته وقدرته شرطاً ، ليكون فاعلاً ، و إن لم يكن من أثر الفاعل .
(The act is) by the act of the agent. So its being preceded by non-existence is not from the act of the agent, so there is no attachment of it to him. Thus stipulating it for its being an act is stipulating something in which the agent has no effect at all. And as for your statement: 'The existent cannot be brought into existence (ijād).' If you mean by it that it cannot have existence renewed after non-existence, that is correct. But if you mean by it that while it is existing, it does not exist by an existentiator (mūjid), we have shown that it exists while it is existing, not while it is non-existent. For a thing only exists if the agent is its existentiator. And the agent is not an existentiator in the state of non-existence, but rather in the state of the existence of the thing from him. And bringing-into-existence (ījād) is simultaneous with the agent's being an existentiator and the effect's being existent, because it is an expression of the relation of the existentiator to the existent. And all of that is with existence, not before it. Thus there is no bringing-into-existence except of an existent, if by 'bringing-into-existence' is meant the relation by which the agent is an existentiator and the effect is existent. They said: For this reason we judged that the world is the act (fiʿl) of Allah, exalted, from eternity and forever, and there is no state except that He, exalted, is its agent, because what is related to the agent is existence. If the relation continues, existence continues; and if it is cut off, it is cut off. Not as you imagined, that if the Creator, exalted, were to cease to exist, the world would remain, as you thought like the builder with the building, for the builder ceases to exist and the building remains. For the remaining of the building is not from the builder, but rather from the dryness that holds its structure together; because if there were not in it a holding force, like water for example, the remaining of the form produced by the act of the agent in it would not be conceivable. The answer: The act is related to the agent in terms of its origination (ḥudūth), not in terms of its prior non-existence nor in terms of its being merely existent. For it is not related to it in the second moment of existence, according to us, while it is existent. Rather, it is related to it in the moment of its origination, in that it is an origination and a going out from non-existence to existence. If the meaning of origination is negated from it, its being an act is not intelligible, nor is its relation to the agent intelligible. And your statement that its being originated reduces to its being preceded by non-existence, and its being preceded by non-existence is not from the act of the agent or the making of the maker – that is so. But it is a condition for the existence to be the act of the agent – I mean its being preceded by non-existence. So existence which is not preceded by non-existence, but is perpetual, is not fit to be an act of the agent. And not everything that is stipulated for the act to be an act must be by the act of the agent. For the essence of the agent, His knowledge, will, and power are conditions for His being an agent, and that is not from the effect of the agent. But an act is not intelligible except from an existent. So the existence of the agent, His knowledge, will, and power are conditions for His being an agent, even if they are not from the effect of the agent.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that the world is an eternal act of God. Ghazali first responds to their argument that an existent cannot be brought into existence, clarifying that bringing into existence is a relation simultaneous with existence, not prior to it. He then addresses their analogy of the builder and the building, arguing that the persistence of the building is not due to the builder but to the material's properties. The core of Ghazali's argument is that an act (fiʿl) is defined by its origination (ḥudūth) – it must be preceded by non-existence. The state of being preceded by non-existence is not itself produced by the agent; it is a condition for something to be considered an act. Therefore, an eternal entity (not preceded by non-existence) cannot be an act. Ghazali concludes by noting that the agent's own existence, knowledge, will, and power are also conditions for agency, yet they are not produced by the agent – similarly, the condition of being preceded by non-existence is not produced by the agent but is necessary for the act.

Key Terms
فاعل (agent)
فعل (act)
حدوث (origination)
عدم (non-existence)
إيجاد (bringing-into-existence)
شرط (condition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the condition of being preceded by non-existence must itself be an act of the agent.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that it is only a condition (sharṭ), not an effect. The agent's own existence and attributes are also conditions, not effects.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the key condition for something to be considered an act (fiʿl)?
A) It must be eternal.
B) It must be preceded by non-existence.
C) It must be simultaneous with the agent.
D) It must be produced by the agent's essence.

Key Takeaway
For an act to be an act, it must be originated from non-existence; the condition of prior non-existence is not something the agent produces but is necessary for the act to be intelligible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life have you been acting as if your existence is self-sustaining, forgetting that you are brought from non-existence to existence every moment by God's act? What will you do today to consciously acknowledge your contingency?
?
Ghazali argues that it is a condition—not an effect—that an act is preceded by non-existence. Do you ever feel that your past mistakes are 'caused' by God because He created the condition of your weakness? How does this distinction free you from blaming God while still relying entirely on His will?
?
If an eternal entity cannot be an act, then your own soul—which is not eternal—is a true act of God. Does this thought humble you? When you consider that your very existence is a continual act of divine origination, how should that change your gratitude and your sense of purpose today?

Ghazali's Refutation of an Eternal Act
Ghazali responds to the objection that an act can be simultaneous and eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that an eternal act is impossible, and the distinction between a true act (originated from non-existence) and a mere eternal effect.

Core Idea
True acts come from non-existence; eternal effects are not acts in reality.

In this dense passage from the Tahafut, Ghazali responds to the philosophers' objection that if an act can be simultaneous with its agent, then an eternal agent can have an eternal act. Ghazali does not deny simultaneity, but he insists that a true act must be originated from non-existence. This distinction is crucial for understanding the creator-creature relationship.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Identify the philosophers' argument using the hand-water analogy: an act can be simultaneous with the agent, so an eternal agent could have an eternal act.
The philosophers try to force Ghazali to admit an eternal act based on his own concession.
2
Grasp Ghazali's distinction
Notice how Ghazali separates 'act' (fiʿl) from 'effect' (maʿlūl). He concedes simultaneity but denies that an eternal effect qualifies as a true act.
The key is the condition that a true act must be from non-existence.
3
Understand the condition for an act
Ghazali states that what is not from non-existence is only called an act metaphorically. This is the core of his refutation.
An eternal entity, by definition, has no beginning, so it cannot be 'from non-existence'.
4
Examine the rebuttal
See how Ghazali dismantles the hand-water analogy: the finger has no act; the agent is the person. Even if eternal, the finger's movement would consist of originated parts.
This shows that the analogy does not support an eternal act without originated parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : اعترفتم بجواز كون الفعل مع الفاعل غير متأخر عنه ، فيلزم منه أن يكون الفعل حادثاً ، إن كان الفاعل حادثاً ، وقديماً إن كان قديماً ، وإن اشترطتم أن يتأخر الفعل عن الفاعل بالزمان فهذا محال ، إذ من حرك اليد في ماء ، تحرك الماء مع حركة اليد ، لا قبلها ولا بعدها ، إذ لو تحرك بعدها ، لكانت اليد مع الماء قبل تنحيه في حيز واحد ، ولو تحرك قبلها ، لا نفصل عن اليد اليد . وهو مع كونه معه معلولها ، وفعل من جهتها ، فإن فرضنا اليد قديمة في الماء متحركة ، كانت حركة الماء أيضاً دائمة ، وهى مع دوامها معلولة ومفعولة ، ولا يمتنع ذلك بفرض الدوام ، فكذلك نسبة العالم إلى الله تعالى . قلنا : لا نحيل أن يكون الفعل مع الفاعل ، بعد كون الفعل حادثاً ، كحركة الماء ، فإنها حادثة عن عدم، فجاز أن يكون فعلاً ، ثم سواء كان متأخراً عن ذات الفاعل ، أو مقارناً له . وإنما نحيل الفعل القديم ، فإن ما ليس عن عدم ، فتسميته فعلاً مجاز ، مجرد لا حقيقة له . وأما المعلول مع العلة أن يكونا حادثين ، وأن يكونا قديمين ، كما يقال : إن العلم القديم علة لكون القديم سبحانه عالماً ، ولا كلام فيه ، وإنما الكلام فيما يسمى فعلاً ومعلول العلة لا يسمى فعل العلة إلا مجازاً ، بل ما يسمى فعلاً ، فشرطه أن يكون عن عدم ، فإن تجوز متجوز بتسمية القديم الدائم الوجود فعلاً لغيره ، كان متجوزاً في الاستعارة . وقولكم : لو قدرنا حركة الماء مع الأصبع قديمة دائمة ، لم تخرج حركة الماء عن كونها فعلاً ، تلبيس" ، لأن الأصبع لا فعل لها فيه ، وإنما الفاعل ذو الأصبع وهو المريد ، ولو قدرناه قديماً لكانت حركة الأصبع فعلاً له ، من حيث إن كل جزء من الحركة حادث عن عدم ، فبهذا
For it is said: You have conceded the permissibility of the act being with the agent not posterior to it, so it follows that the act is originated (ḥādith) if the agent is originated, and eternal (qadīm) if the agent is eternal. And if you stipulate that the act be temporally posterior to the agent, that is impossible, because whoever moves a hand in water, the water moves with the movement of the hand, neither before it nor after it. For if it moved after it, the hand would be with the water before its displacement in one space; and if it moved before it, it would not separate from the hand. And it (the water movement), while being simultaneous with it (the hand movement), is its effect (maʿlūl) and act (fiʿl) from it (the hand). So if we suppose the hand to be eternal in the water moving, the water's movement would also be perpetual, and while being perpetual, it is an effect (maʿlūl) and acted-upon (mafʿūl), and that is not impossible by supposing perpetuity. Similarly is the relation of the world to Allah, exalted. We say: We do not deem impossible that the act be with the agent, after the act is originated, like the movement of water, for it is originated from non-existence, so it is permissible for it to be an act. Then, whether it is posterior to the essence of the agent or simultaneous with it, (that is possible). But we deem impossible the eternal act (al-fiʿl al-qadīm). For what is not from non-existence, its being called an act is metaphorical (majāz), a mere metaphor without reality (ḥaqīqa). As for the effect (maʿlūl) with the cause (ʿilla), they may both be originated or both be eternal, as it is said: 'The eternal knowledge is a cause for the Eternal, exalted, being knowing.' There is no dispute about that. The dispute is only about what is called an act. And the effect of the cause is not called an act of the cause except metaphorically. Rather, what is called an act – its condition is that it be from non-existence. So if someone metaphorically applies the term act to the eternal perpetual existent in relation to another, he is being metaphorical in his metaphorical usage. And your statement: 'If we suppose the movement of water with the finger to be eternal and perpetual, the water's movement does not cease to be an act' – that is obfuscation (talbīs), because the finger has no act in it. Rather, the agent is the possessor of the finger, who is the willer. And if we supposed him eternal, then the movement of the finger would be an act of his, in that each part of the movement is originated from non-existence. So by this
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage consists of an objection from the philosophers and Ghazali's response. The objection argues that if an act can be simultaneous with the agent, as Ghazali conceded, then an eternal agent could have an eternal act, using the example of a hand moving water: the water moves simultaneously with the hand, and if the hand were eternal, the water's movement would be eternal yet still an act. Ghazali responds by distinguishing between a true act and a mere effect (maʿlūl). A true act (fiʿl) must be originated from non-existence. The water movement is originated from non-existence (it had a beginning), so it can be an act even if simultaneous with the agent. But an eternal act is impossible because the term 'act' is only properly applied to what comes from non-existence. An eternal effect, like the relation of knowledge to God, is not called an act except metaphorically. Ghazali also points out that the analogy of the finger is flawed: the finger itself has no act; the agent is the person with intention. The finger's movement, if eternal, would still be composed of originated parts, each from non-existence, so it could be an act. Thus, the philosophers' attempt to prove an eternal act fails.

Key Terms
فعل (act)
معلول (effect)
علة (cause)
قديم (eternal)
حادث (originated)
مجاز (metaphorical)
حقيقة (literal truth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That an eternal act is possible because effects can be eternal and simultaneous with their cause.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that 'act' strictly means originating from non-existence; an eternal entity, even if an effect, is not a true act.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is an eternal act (al-fiʿl al-qadīm) impossible?
A) Because it would require the agent to be temporal.
B) Because what is not from non-existence is only called an act metaphorically.
C) Because an act must be posterior to the agent.
D) Because God cannot act eternally.

Key Takeaway
For Ghazali, a true act must be originated from non-existence; an eternal entity, even if an effect, is not an act except metaphorically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
In what areas of your life do you treat something that is merely an effect (maʿlūl) as if it were a true act (fiʿl) – perhaps attributing independent power to habits or people? How can you realign your perspective to see all originated events as true acts from God?
?
The philosophers used an analogy (hand-water) to argue for an eternal act. Are there analogies you rely on in your own thinking about God that might be unintentionally misleading? What would it cost you to replace them with more precise concepts?

Definition of Act and Its Condition of Origination
Ghazali defines 'act' as necessarily preceded by non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's definition of an act (fi'l) as necessarily preceded by non-existence, and the distinction between conditions for agency and the act itself.

Core Idea
An act is only real if it emerges from non-existence.

Ghazali begins his critique of the philosophers by establishing a precise definition of 'act'. He argues that the term cannot apply to eternal realities, only to originated events. In this passage, he sets the conceptual stage for the entire discussion of divine causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the literal meaning, then read the translation. Note Ghazali's direct style.
The Arabic uses 'بالعدم' (by non-existence) to immediately state the condition.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the sentence that states what makes something an act. It asserts that perpetual existence cannot be an act.
Key phrase: 'الوجود الذي ليس مسبوقاً بعدم ، بل هو دائم ، لا يصلح أن يكون فعلا'
3
Distinguish conditions of agency from the act
Notice how Ghazali separates the agent's necessary attributes (knowledge, will, power) from the act itself. They are prerequisites, not effects.
This prevents misunderstanding that God's attributes are created acts.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this definition matters for the debate about the world's creation. If the world were eternal, it would not be an act of God.
This definition directly challenges the philosophers' claim of a pre-eternal world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالعدم - فالوجود الذي ليس مسبوقاً بعدم ، بل هو دائم ، لا يصلح أن يكون فعلا للفاعل ، وليس كل ما يشترط فى كون الفعل فعلا ينبغي أن يكون بفعل الفاعل ، فإن ذات الفاعل وعلمه وإرادته وقدرته ، شرط في كونه فاعلا، وليس ذلك من أثر الفاعل ، ولكن لا يعقل فعل إلا من موجود ، فكان وجود الفاعل وعلمه وإرادته وقدرته شرطاً ، ليكون فاعلا ، و إن لم يكن من أثر الفاعل .
By non-existence – so existence which is not preceded by non-existence, but is perpetual, is not suitable to be an act of the agent. And not everything that is a condition for an act being an act should be by the act of the agent. For the essence of the agent, His knowledge, will, and power are conditions for His being an agent, and that is not from the effect of the agent. But an act is only conceivable from an existent; so the existence of the agent, His knowledge, will, and power are conditions for Him to be an agent, even if they are not from the effect of the agent.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'act' (fi'l) and its essential condition. Ghazali begins by stating that any existence that is not preceded by non-existence (al-`adam), but is perpetual (dā'im), cannot be considered an act in the real sense. He then distinguishes between the conditions for being an agent (fā'il) and the act itself. The agent's essence (dhāt), knowledge (`ilm), will (irāda), and power (qudra) are prerequisites for agency, but these attributes are not themselves products of the agent's act. Acts proceed only from an existent agent, so these attributes are necessary conditions for agency, not effects of it. The argument establishes that an act must be originated (hādith) from non-existence, and that the eternal attributes of God are not acts but conditions of His agency.

Key Terms
فعل (act)
فاعل (agent)
عدم (non-existence)
دائم (perpetual)
شرط (condition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The conditions of agency (knowledge, will, power) are themselves acts of the agent.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies they are prerequisites for agency, not effects of the agent's act.

Checkpoint

A) It must be produced by an intelligent agent.
B) It must be preceded by non-existence.
C) It must be simultaneous with the agent.
D) It must be eternal and perpetual.

Key Takeaway
An act must be preceded by non-existence; perpetual existence cannot be an act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every act must be preceded by non-existence, what does that say about your own daily actions? Do you live as if your deeds emerge from nothing each moment, or do you take them for granted as if they are perpetual extensions of yourself? How can you cultivate a constant awareness of your dependence on the Creator for every single action?
?
Ghazali insists that God's knowledge and power are conditions of His agency, not effects of His act. Reflect: Have you ever treated your own abilities (knowledge, will, strength) as your own independent possessions rather than as gifts loaned to you by God? What would change if you saw them as conditions for your agency that are not themselves your acts?
?
Consider the difference between an act that comes from non-existence and a perpetual existence. Are there areas of your life where you assume your character or habits are 'perpetual'—fixed and unchanging? How does this lesson challenge you to see even your personality and inclinations as acts of God that He could originate anew? What would it mean to ask God to re-create your heart?

Refutation of the Eternity of Act via Hand-Water Example
Ghazali answers the objection that an act could be eternal using simultaneity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali insists that an act must be originated from non-existence to be truly an act, and why an eternal act is impossible.

Core Idea
An act is defined by origination from non-existence, not by temporal relationship with the agent.

This lesson addresses a key objection raised by the philosophers: if an act can be simultaneous with its agent, why cannot the world be an eternal act of God? Ghazali answers by drawing a crucial distinction between physical causality and true divine action, using the example of a hand moving in water. We will follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The philosophers argue that simultaneity of act and agent implies the act shares the agent's temporal attribute. Understand their reasoning: if a originated agent produces an originated act, an eternal agent should produce an eternal act.
Notice the logical symmetry they propose.
2
Examine Ghazali's initial concession
Ghazali concedes that act and agent can be simultaneous, but only when the act is originated. The water's movement is simultaneous yet originated.
Simultaneity does not automatically make the act eternal.
3
Understand the condition of origination
Ghazali asserts that what is not from non-existence cannot be called an act except metaphorically. An act must come into being after not being.
This is the core of his argument.
4
Distinguish between cause and agent
Ghazali distinguishes between a cause (`illa) and an agent (fā'il). The water's movement is an effect of a cause, but not an act of the hand in the true sense; it is an act of God because it is originated.
The finger's movement is an act because each part is originated.
5
Reflect on the obfuscation charge
Ghazali labels the philosopher's example as 'talbīs' (obfuscation), because they ignore the role of the willer and treat the water's movement as if it were the hand's act.
The example misapplies the term 'act'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الماء فإن قيل : اعترفتم بجواز كون الفعل مع الفاعل غير متأخر عنه ، فيلزم منه أن يكون الفعل حادثاً ، إن كان الفاعل حادثاً ، وقديماً إن كان قديماً ، وإن اشترطتم أن يتأخر الفعل عن الفاعل بالزمان فهذا محال ، إذ من حرك اليد في ماء ، تحرك الماء مع حركة اليد ، لا قبلها ولا بعدها ، إذ لو تحرك بعدها ، لكانت اليد مع الماء قبل تنحيه فى حيز واحد ، ولو تحرك قبلها ، لا نفصل عن اليد اليد . وهو مع كونه معه معلولها ، وفعل من جهتها ، فإن فرضنا اليد قديمة فى الماء متحركة ، كانت حركة الماء أيضاً دائمة ، وهى مع دوامها معلولة ومفعولة ، ولا يمتنع ذلك بفرض الدوام ، فكذلك نسبة العالم إلى الله تعالى . قلنا : لا نحيل أن يكون الفعل مع الفاعل ، بعد كون الفعل حادثاً ، كحركة الماء ، فإنها حادثة عن عدم، فجاز أن يكون فعلا ، ثم سواء كان متأخراً عن ذات الفاعل ، أو مقارناً له . وإنما نحيل الفعل القديم ، فإن ما ليس عن عدم ، فتسميته فعلا مجاز ، مجرد لا حقيقة له . وأما المعلول مع العلة أن يكونا حادثين ، وأن يكونا قديمين ، كما يقال : إن العلم القديم علة لكون القديم سبحانه عالماً ، ولا كلام فيه ، وإنما الكلام فيما يسمى فعلا ومعلول العلة لا يسمى فعل العلة إلا مجازاً ، بل ما يسمى فعلا ، فشرطه أن يكون عن عدم ، فإن تجوز متجوز بتسمية القديم الدائم الوجود فعلا لغيره ، كان متجوزاً فى الاستعارة . وقولكم : لو قدرنا حركة الماء مع الأصبع قديمة دائمة ، لم تخرج حركة الماء عن كونها فعلا ، تلبيس" ، لأن الأصبع لا فعل لها فيه ، وإنما الفاعل ذو الأصبع وهو المريد ، ولو قدرناه قديماً لكانت حركة الأصبع فعلا له ، من حيث إن كل جزء من الحركة حادث عن عدم ، فبهذا الاعتبار كان فعلاً ، وأما حركة الماء ، فقد لا نقول : إنها من فعله ، بل هي من فعل الله سبحانه وعلى أى وجه كانه فكون فعلا ، من حيث إنه حادث ، إلا أنه دائم الحدوث ، وهو فعل من حيث إنه حادث .
Water. For it is said: You have admitted the permissibility of the act being with the agent, not delayed from it. So it follows that the act is originated (hādith) if the agent is originated, and eternal (qadīm) if the agent is eternal. And if you stipulate that the act must be temporally delayed from the agent, that is impossible, since whoever moves his hand in water, the water moves with the movement of the hand, neither before it nor after it. For if it moved after it, the hand and water would be in one place before the water's displacement; and if it moved before it, the hand would be separate from the hand. And it, while being with it, is its effect (ma'lūl) and act from its direction. If we suppose the hand is eternal in water moving, then the water's movement would also be perpetual, and while perpetual it is an effect (ma'lūla) and a made thing (maf'ūla), and that is not impossible by supposing perpetuity. So likewise is the relation of the world to God Most High. We say: We do not deem impossible that the act be with the agent, given that the act is originated, like the movement of water, for it is originated from non-existence, so it is permissible for it to be an act. Then, whether it is delayed from the essence of the agent or simultaneous with it. We only deem impossible the eternal act; for what is not from non-existence, its being called an act is a metaphor, purely figurative with no reality. As for the effect (ma'lūl) with the cause (`illa), they may both be originated or both be eternal, as it is said: eternal knowledge is the cause for the Eternal being knowing – there is no dispute about that. The contention is only about what is called an act (fi'l); and the effect of the cause is not called the act of the cause except metaphorically. Rather, what is called an act has the condition that it be from non-existence. If someone takes a license by calling the eternal perpetual existence an act for another, he is taking a license in metaphor. And your statement: if we suppose the movement of water with the finger to be eternal and perpetual, the water's movement would not cease to be an act – that is obfuscation (talbīs), because the finger has no act in it; rather, the agent is the possessor of the finger, who is the willer. And if we suppose him eternal, then the movement of the finger would be his act, insofar as every part of the movement is originated from non-existence; by this consideration it is an act. As for the movement of water, we may not say it is from his act; rather, it is from the act of God Most High. And in whatever way it is, it is an act insofar as it is originated, except that it is perpetually originated, and it is an act insofar as it is originated.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that if an act can be simultaneous with its agent, then the act's temporal status should follow the agent's: if the agent is originated, the act is originated; if the agent is eternal, the act is eternal. He gives the example of a hand moving in water: the water moves simultaneously with the hand, not before or after. If the hand were eternal and moving, the water's movement would also be eternal, yet it would still be an effect and an act. Similarly, the world could be an eternal act of God. Ghazali answers by conceding that act and agent can be simultaneous, but only if the act is originated (hādith). The water's movement is originated from non-existence, even if simulatenous. He insists that an eternal act (al-fi'l al-qadīm) is impossible; calling it an act is merely metaphorical (majāz). He distinguishes between a cause (`illa) and an agent (fā'il). An effect of a cause (ma'lūl al-`illa) is not called an act of the cause except metaphorically. The true act must proceed from non-existence. He then refines the example: the finger (as part of the agent) produces motion where each part is originated, so that motion is truly an act. But the water's movement, though caused by the hand, is not the act of the hand but of God. Thus, even if the water's movement were perpetually originated, it would still be an act because it is originated, not because it is eternal. The key point is that eternity itself does not make something an act; origination does.

Key Terms
حادث
قديم
معلول
علة
مجاز
تلبيس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People often think that if an act is simultaneous with its agent, then the act must share the agent's temporal attribute—so an eternal agent would produce an eternal act that is genuinely an act.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the true criterion for an act is origination from non-existence. An eternal act is a contradiction because an 'eternal originated' cannot exist. Thus, even if agent and act are simultaneous, the act must be originated to be considered a real act.

Checkpoint
In the hand-water example, why does Ghazali consider the water's movement an act?
A) Because it is simultaneous with the hand.
B) Because it is originated from non-existence.
C) Because it is caused by the finger.
D) Because it is perpetual.

Key Takeaway
The decisive criterion for an act is origination from non-existence, not temporal simultaneity with the agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of your own actions—like speaking or moving—do you truly see them as originated from non-existence by your will, or do you take them for granted as automatic? How would your sense of responsibility change if you realized each moment of action is a fresh creation from non-existence?
?
Ghazali says calling an eternal existence an act is mere metaphor. Is there a 'long-standing habit' or 'permanent trait' you attribute to yourself that might actually be a metaphor? What would it mean to honestly admit that it is not truly your act but an enduring state you have no real power to originate?
?
If only what comes from non-existence is a true act, then every good deed you do is an act of God's creation through you. Does this humble you, or does it threaten your sense of self? Will you consciously affirm today that your every act is originated from non-existence by the true Agent?

Ghazali's Conclusion: Philosophers' 'Act' for God Is Merely Metaphorical
Ghazali concludes that philosophers' usage of 'act' is metaphorical, not real.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the philosophers' use of the term 'act' for God is metaphorical and does not signify real agency.

Core Idea
For Ghazali, calling God an 'agent' without real causal efficacy is mere metaphor, not truth.

In this final exchange of the discussion, Ghazali addresses an objection that tries to reduce the dispute to a matter of terminology. He firmly rejects this and asserts that the philosophers' language masks a deeper denial of real divine agency. The passage reveals that the debate is not about words but about the reality of God's causal power.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's concession: if the world is a perpetual effect of God, it is acceptable to call it an act; names are trivial after meanings are clear.
The objector tries to sidestep the disagreement by minimizing terminology.
2
Examine Ghazali's response
Ghazali insists that the whole point is to show the philosophers' use of 'act' is without verification (tahqīq) and merely metaphorical.
He states directly: 'God Most High is not a real agent according to you, nor is the world His real act.'
3
Reflect on the stakes
Understand why Ghazali considers this more than a semantic issue—it concerns the reality of divine causation and the proper attribution of agency to God.
The disagreement is about whether God truly acts or whether 'act' is just a borrowed term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فإذا اعترفتم بأن نسبة الفعل إلى الفاعل من حيث إنه موجود معه ، كنسبة المعلول إلى العلة ، ثم سلمتم تصور الدوام في نسبة العلة فنحن لا نعني بكون العالم فعلا ، إلا كونه معلولا دائم النسبة إلى الله تعالى ، فإن لم تسموا هذا فعلا فلا مضايقة فى التسميات بعد ظهور المعاني . قلنا : ولا غرض لنا من هذه المسألة إلا بيان أنكم تتجملون بهذه الأسماء من غير تحقيق ، وأن الله تعالى عندكم ليس فاعلا تحقيقاً ، ولا العالم فعله تحقيقاً ، وأن إطلاق هذا الاسم مجاز منكم لا تحقيق له ، وقد ظهر هذا
For it is said: If you admit that the relation of the act to the agent, insofar as it exists with him, is like the relation of the effect (ma'lūl) to the cause (`illa), and then you concede the conceivability of perpetuity in the causal relation – then we mean by the world being an act nothing other than its being an effect perpetually related to God Most High. If you do not call this an act, there is no quarrel over names after the meanings have become clear. We say: We have no purpose in this discussion except to show that you adorn yourselves with these names without verification, and that God Most High is not a real agent according to you, nor is the world His real act, and that the application of this name is a metaphor from you with no reality. And this has become clear.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage contains a final objection and Ghazali's conclusive response. The objector concedes that the relation of act to agent is like that of effect to cause, and admits that perpetuity in causality is conceivable. Therefore, he argues, calling the world an 'act' of God simply means it is a perpetual effect of God. If Ghazali disagrees with the name, the meanings are clear, so terminology is trivial. Ghazali responds sharply: the whole point of the discussion is to demonstrate that the philosophers use these terms (fā'il, fi'l) without real verification (tahqīq). According to them, God is not truly an agent, nor is the world truly His act. Their use of these names is merely metaphorical (majāz), not real. Ghazali asserts that this has been made evident. The passage underscores that the debate is not about mere words but about the reality of divine agency.

Key Terms
تحقيق (verification)
مجاز (metaphor)
فاعل تحقيقاً (real agent)
معلول دائم النسبة (perpetually related effect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers and Ghazali only disagree on terminology; the underlying meaning is the same.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the philosophers deny real agency, so their use of 'act' is without verification, making it metaphorical. The disagreement is about the reality of divine causal power, not just names.

Checkpoint

B) God is not a real agent; the term is metaphorical.
C) God is an agent only in the sense of being a cause.
D) God's agency is unknown.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali establishes that the philosophers' description of God as an 'agent' is merely metaphorical because they deny real causal efficacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is the agent of my life,' do you mean it as a comfortable metaphor or as a reality that God actually, causally intervenes in your smallest decisions? Examine one specific decision you made today—did you truly rely on Him as a real agent, or did you casually assume your own independence while using religious language?
?
If you really believed that God is a 'real agent' (فاعل تحقيقاً) in every event—from your heartbeat to the sunrise—what would you need to change in how you pray, plan, or worry? Be honest: are there areas of your life where you act as if God's action is merely a metaphor, not a fact?
?
Ghazali says the philosophers 'adorn themselves with names without verification'—they use the word 'agent' but deny its reality. In your relationship with God, are you ever guilty of the same: using pious language about God's control and power while living as if it's just a figure of speech? What would it cost you to let your life be governed by the full, literal reality of His agency?

Ghazali Refutes the Philosophers' Concept of Divine Agency
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' argument that 'act' means any effect of a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the philosophers' use of 'act' for the world is merely metaphorical, not a real description of divine agency.

Core Idea
If God is just a cause, His 'act' is just a metaphor.

In this passage from the Tahafut, Ghazali engages in a dialectical exchange with the philosophers. They claim that 'act' simply means any effect of a cause, so the world can be called God's act. Ghazali responds that this reduces divine agency to mere causality, making the term 'act' a hollow metaphor. Let's see how he dismantles their argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the philosophers' objection carefully. They argue that since the act-agent relation is like effect-cause, and perpetuity is acceptable, then calling the world an act is fine.
Notice they claim the dispute is only about names after meanings are clear.
2
Identify Ghazali's rebuttal
Ghazali says the philosophers merely adorn themselves with words without verification; God is not a real agent in their system.
3
Understand the key distinction
Focus on the terms majāz (metaphor) vs taḥqīq (verification). Ghazali insists the issue is not merely terminological but about the reality of divine agency.
This is the core of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتبار كان فعلاً ، وأما حركة الماء ، فقد لا نقول : إنها من فعله ، بل هي من فعل الله سبحانه وعلى أى وجه كانه فكون فعلا ، من حيث إنه حادث ، إلا أنه دائم الحدوث ، وهو فعل من حيث إنه حادث .
فإن قيل : فإذا اعترفتم بأن نسبة الفعل إلى الفاعل من حيث إنه موجود معه ، كنسبة المعلول إلى العلة ، ثم سلمتم تصور الدوام في نسبة العلة فنحن لا نعنى بكون العالم فعلا ، إلا كونه معلولا دائم النسبة إلى الله تعالى ، فإن لم تسموا هذا فعلا فلا مضايقة فى التسميات بعد ظهور المعاني

Consideration was that it is an act. As for the movement of water, we may not say that it is from his act, but rather it is from the act of God, exalted. And in whatever way it is, it is an act insofar as it is originated, except that it is perpetual in origination, and it is an act insofar as it is originated. If it is said: Since you admit that the relation of the act to the agent insofar as it exists with him is like the relation of the effect to the cause, and then you accept conceiving perpetuity in the relation of the cause, then we do not mean by the world being an act except that it is an effect perpetually related to God, exalted. So if you do not call this an act, there is no dispute over names after the meanings are clear. We said: We have no purpose in this discussion except to show that you adorn yourselves with these names without verification, and that God, exalted, in your view is not an agent in reality, nor is the world His act in reality, and that the application of this name is a metaphor from you with no reality, and this has become clear.

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer exchange. First, Ghazali states the philosophers' position: they consider something an act (fiʿl) merely insofar as it is originated (ḥādith), even if perpetually so. Then the objector (presumably a philosopher) argues: since you (Muslim theologians) admit that the relation of act to agent is like that of effect to cause, and you accept perpetuity in that relation, then we simply mean by 'act' a perpetual effect—so there is no dispute over naming once meanings are clear. Ghazali replies that his only aim is to expose that the philosophers merely adorn themselves with the term 'agent' (fāʿil) without reality: God in their system is not a true agent, and the world is not truly His act; their usage is metaphorical (majāz), not veridical (taḥqīq). This underscores Ghazali's critique that the philosophers' concept of God as efficient cause is insufficient for genuine divine action.
