The Philosophers' Principle: From the One Only One Proceeds
Ghazali presents the philosophers' principle and their emanation solution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' principle that from the One only one proceeds, and how they use emanation to explain the world's multiplicity.

Core Idea
The philosophers' own rule makes the world impossible as God's direct act.

Ghazali begins by stating the philosophers' foundational principle: from a simple, single cause only one effect can directly issue. Since God is absolutely one and the world is diverse, how could the world be His act? The philosophers answer through a chain of intermediaries, starting with a single intellect (the 'angel'). This lesson examines that claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Ghazali presents the philosophers' view neutrally here.
2
Identify the philosophers' principle
Find the statement 'لا يصدر من الواحد إلا شيء واحد' and understand its meaning. Note that it is a condition common to agent and act.
3
Understand the implication for the world
The world is composite and diverse, so it cannot be a direct act of the absolutely One. This creates a problem for the philosophers.
4
Examine the emanation solution
The philosophers propose that the world originates through intermediaries: a first separate intellect (angel), then another, and so on. This allows multiplicity without violating the principle.
Note that they identify the first intellect with the angel in religious language.
5
Prepare for the refutation
Recognize that Ghazali is setting up the philosophers' position to refute it later. Keep this in mind as you proceed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث
في استحالة كون العالم فعلا لله تعالى على أصلهم ، لشرط مشترك بين الفاعل والفعل ، وهو أنهم قالوا : لا يصدر من الواحد إلا شيء واحد ؛ والمبدأ الأول واحد من كل وجه ، والعالم مركب من مختلفات ، فلا يتصور أن يكون فعلا الله تعالى بموجب أصلهم
فإن قالوا : العالم بجملته ليس صادراً من الله تعالى بغير واسطة ، بل الصادر عنه موجود واحد ، هو أول المخلوقات ، وهو عقل مجرد ، أي هو جوهر مجرد ، قائم بنفسه ، غير متحيز ، يعرف نفسه ، ويعرف مبدأه ، ويعبر عنه في لسان الشرع بالملك ، ثم يصدر منه ثالث ، ومن الثالث رابع ، وتكثر الموجودات بالتوسط
·
فإن اختلاف الفعل وكثرته :
إما أن يكون لاختلاف القوى الفاعلة كما أنا نفعل بقوة الشهوة خلاف ما نفعله بقوة الغضب
و إما أن يكون لاختلاف المواد ، كما أن الشمس تبيض الثوب المغسول ،
وتسود وجه الإنسان ، وتذيب بعض الجواهر ، وتصلب بعضها وإما لاختلاف الآلات ، كالنجار الواحد ، ينشر بالمنشار ، وينحت ، ويثقب بالمثقب
بالقدوم
و إما أن تكون كثرة الفعل بالتوسط ، بأن يفعل فعلا واحداً ، ثم ذلك الفعل
يفعل غيره فيكثر الفعل
·
وهذه الأقسام كلها محالة ، فى المبدأ الأول ، إذ ليس في ذاته اختلاف ،
6
ولا اثنينية ، ولا كثرة ، كما سيأتى فى أدلة التوحيد ، ولا ثمت اختلاف مواد : فإن الكلام فى المعلول الأول الذى هو المادة الأولى مثلاً ، ولا ثمت اختلاف
The Third Aspect: On the impossibility of the world being an act of God, exalted, according to their principle, due to a condition common between agent and act, which is that they said: From the one only one thing issues; and the First Principle is one from every aspect, and the world is composed of diverse things, so it cannot be conceived that it is an act of God, exalted, according to their principle. For they said: The world in its entirety does not issue from God, exalted, without an intermediary; rather, what issues from Him is one existent, which is the first of created things, and it is a separate intellect, i.e. a separate substance, subsisting by itself, non-spatial, knowing itself and knowing its principle, and it is expressed in the language of the Law as the angel (malak). Then from it issues a third, and from the third a fourth, and existents multiply through mediation. For the diversity and multiplicity of act: either it is due to diversity of efficient powers, as we do by the power of appetite contrary to what we do by the power of anger; or it is due to diversity of materials, as the sun whitens washed cloth, blackens a man's face, melts some jewels, and hardens others; or it is due to diversity of instruments, like one carpenter who saws with the saw, carves, and bores with the awl and the adze; or the multiplicity of act is through mediation, by his doing one act, then that act does another, and thus acts multiply. All these divisions are impossible in the First Principle, for in His essence there is no difference, no duality, no multiplicity—as will come in the proofs of tawḥīd—and there is no diversity of materials, for the discussion concerns the first effect which is the first matter, for example, and there is no diversity.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a complete argument and refutation. Ghazali first states the philosophers' own principle: from the one (al-wāḥid) only one thing can issue. Since the First Principle (al-mabdaʾ al-awwal) is absolutely one, and the world is composite and diverse, it follows on their own grounds that the world cannot be God's act. Then Ghazali presents the philosophers' rejoinder: the world does not issue directly from God but through a chain of intermediaries; first a single separate intellect (identified with the angel in religious language), then from it another, and so on, so that multiplicity arises through mediation (bi-t-tawassuṭ). Ghazali then counters by analyzing the possible sources of diversity in an act. He enumerates four causes: (1) difference in the agent's powers (e.g., appetite vs. anger), (2) difference in materials (e.g., sun's effect varies by object), (3) difference in instruments (e.g., carpenter's tools), and (4) mediation (one act causing another). He then argues that all these are impossible for the First Principle. The First Principle has no internal difference, duality, or multiplicity, so (1) is ruled out. There is no diversity of materials at the first effect, which is the first matter, so (2) is ruled out. Instruments imply composition, so (3) is ruled out. As for mediation, the chain itself starts from a single effect, so the initial diversity still cannot arise without some differentiating factor in the first cause. This leaves the philosophers' emanation scheme without a satisfactory explanation for the world's multiplicity. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear: the philosophers' own principles make it impossible for the world to be God's act.

مبدأ أول

Correction: Ghazali is presenting the philosophers' view to refute it; he does not accept it.

?
Ghazali presents the philosophers' view without comment, preparing to refute it. Have you ever entertained an idea that seemed intellectually satisfying but contradicted divine revelation? What intellectual idols have you unknowingly served? The lesson calls you to test every principle against the truth. Commit to examining one belief you hold this week—is it based on God's word or human reasoning?
?
The first intellect is described as 'knowing itself and knowing its Principle.' Does your self-knowledge lead you to deeper knowledge of God, or does it become an end in itself? Ghazali's Refutation: Four Causes of Diversity
Ghazali enumerates and refutes four causes of multiplicity in acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the emanation scheme by analyzing the possible sources of multiplicity in an act.

Core Idea
Every source of diversity fails for the First Principle, so emanation cannot explain the world.

Having presented the philosophers' emanation scheme, Ghazali now dismantles it. He analyzes four ways an act can be diverse: by differences in powers, materials, instruments, or through mediation. He then shows that none apply to the First Principle, leaving the philosophers without a coherent explanation for the world's multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four causes of diversity
Find the four causes Ghazali lists: difference in powers, materials, instruments, and mediation. Understand each with his examples.
2
Examine why each fails for God
For each cause, Ghazali argues why it cannot apply to the First Principle. Note his reasoning: no internal composition, no pre-existing materials, no instruments, and mediation requires a prior source of multiplicity.
3
Understand the final point
The passage ends with an incomplete sentence, but the argument is clear: the philosophers' emanation still cannot account for the first diversity.
This is a refutation of the entire emanation scheme.
4
Reflect on the strength of the argument
Consider how Ghazali uses the philosophers' own principle against them. The multiplicity of the world must come from somewhere, but no source is viable for a simple, non-material First Principle.

The Philosophers' Emanationist Response
Ghazali reports the philosophers' emanationist account of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that the world arises from God through a chain of intermediate abstract intellects, rather than directly.

Core Idea
Multiplicity from unity requires mediation through intellects.

After presenting the problem of how multiplicity can arise from the One, Ghazali now reports the philosophers' solution. They argue that God's direct creation is only a single entity—an abstract intellect—which then serves as the source for further existents. This chain of emanations introduces multiplicity gradually. This passage thus outlines the emanationist cosmology that Ghazali will critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the structure: 'فإن قالوا' introduces the philosophers' response.
2
Identify the first effect
The first effect is described as 'عقل مجرد' – an abstract intellect. Also called a substance, self-subsistent, non-spatial, knowing itself and its principle.
Religious language calls this an angel (الملك).
3
Trace the chain of mediation
From that first intellect proceeds a third, from the third a fourth, and so on. Multiplicity arises through successive steps, not directly from God.
4
Consider the philosophical rationale
This model solves the problem of how the perfect, simple One can produce a diverse world. It preserves God's simplicity by making the immediate effect also simple.
The philosophers rely on the principle 'from one, only one can proceed directly.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: العالم بجملته ليس صادراً من الله تعالى بغير واسطة، بل الصادر عنه موجود واحد، هو أول المخلوقات، وهو عقل مجرد، أي هو جوهر مجرد، قائم بنفسه، غير متحيز، يعرف نفسه، ويعرف مبدأه، ويعبر عنه في لسان الشرع بالملك، ثم يصدر منه ثالث، ومن الثالث رابع، وتكثر الموجودات بالتوسط
For if they say: The world in its entirety does not proceed from God, the Exalted, without an intermediary; rather, what proceeds from Him is a single existent, which is the first of created things, and it is an abstract intellect, i.e. an abstract substance, subsisting by itself, not occupying space, knowing itself and knowing its principle, and it is expressed in the language of the Law as 'the angel'; then from it proceeds a third, and from the third a fourth, and existents multiply through mediation.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' counter-argument to the objection raised by Ghazali. They acknowledge the problem (how can many come from one?) and offer their solution: the First Principle does not directly produce the world in all its multiplicity. Instead, it produces a single first effect, which is an abstract intellect—a self-subsistent, non-spatial substance that knows itself and its source. In religious language, this is called an angel. This first intellect then produces a second effect, and that second produces a third, and so on, in a descending chain. Thus multiplicity arises not directly from the One but through successive mediations. This is their emanationist cosmology. Ghazali here reports this view before proceeding to refute it.

جوهر مجرد
الملك

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers believe the world was created directly by God without intermediaries.
Correction: They believe the world arises through a chain of necessary emanations from the First Principle, with the first effect being an abstract intellect.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' emanationist model posits that God's direct creation is a single abstract intellect, from which all other existents proceed through a chain of mediations.

?

Four Possible Sources of Multiplicity in Divine Action
Ghazali enumerates four possible sources of multiplicity in a single agent's action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical ways multiplicity can arise from a single agent and why three of them are impossible for God.

Core Idea
Multiplicity in creation cannot come from God having parts, materials, or tools; only through a chain of effects.

Ghazali systematically analyzes how a single cause could produce multiple effects. He enumerates four possibilities and then tests each against the nature of the First Principle. This lesson unpacks his logical classification and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the four possibilities Ghazali lists.
The passage uses examples from everyday experience.
2
Identify the four causes
Find each of the four possible sources of multiplicity: difference of powers, materials, instruments, and mediation.
Each is introduced with 'إما أن يكون'.
3
Understand the impossibility arguments
For each cause except mediation, Ghazali gives a reason why it cannot apply to God. Work through each one.
The reasons involve God's simplicity, no pre-existing matter, and no co-existent beings.
4
Note the remaining possibility
Ghazali concludes that only mediation is left as a possibility in the philosophers' framework, but he will later refute it.
This sets up the next stage of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن اختلاف الفعل وكثرته: إما أن يكون لاختلاف القوى الفاعلة كما أنا نفعل بقوة الشهوة خلاف ما نفعله بقوة الغضب، وإما أن يكون لاختلاف المواد، كما أن الشمس تبيض الثوب المغسول، وتسود وجه الإنسان، وتذيب بعض الجواهر، وتصلب بعضها، وإما لاختلاف الآلات، كالنجار الواحد ينشر بالمنشار وينحت ويثقب بالمثقب بالقدوم، وإما أن تكون كثرة الفعل بالتوسط، بأن يفعل فعلا واحداً ثم ذلك الفعل يفعل غيره فتكثر الفعل. وهذه الأقسام كلها محالة في المبدأ الأول، إذ ليس في ذاته اختلاف ولا اثنينية ولا كثرة، كما سيأتي في أدلة التوحيد، ولا ثمت اختلاف مواد: فإن الكلام في المعلول الأول الذي هو المادة الأولى مثلاً، ولا ثمت اختلاف آلات إذ لا موجود مع الله تعالى في رتبته، فالكلام في حدوث الآلة الأولى، فلم يبق إلا أن تكون الكثرة في العالم صادرة عن الله تعالى بطريق التوسط كما سبق.
For the difference and multiplicity of the act: either it is due to difference of active powers, as we do with the power of appetite something different from what we do with the power of anger; or it is due to difference of materials, as the sun whitens the washed garment, blackens the face of man, melts some jewels and hardens others; or due to difference of instruments, like a single carpenter who saws with the saw, carves, and drills with the drill and plane?; or the multiplicity of the act is due to mediation, that he does a single act then that act does another, so the act multiplies. And all these divisions are impossible for the First Principle, for there is in His essence no difference, no duality, no multiplicity, as will come in the proofs of tawheed; nor is there difference of materials: for the discourse is about the first effect which is, for example, the first matter; nor is there difference of instruments since there is no existent with God, the Exalted, in His rank, so the discourse is about the occurrence of the first instrument; so nothing remains except that the multiplicity in the world proceeds from God, the Exalted, by way of mediation, as mentioned before.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a systematic logical analysis of how multiplicity could arise from a single agent. Ghazali identifies four possible sources: (1) the agent has multiple faculties or powers (e.g., appetite and anger producing different acts); (2) the material on which the agent acts varies (e.g., the sun affects different materials differently); (3) the agent uses different tools or instruments; (4) the act is multiplied through a chain of intermediaries where each effect becomes a cause of further effects. Ghazali then argues that all four are impossible for the First Principle. First, God has no composition or multiplicity in His essence, so no multiple powers. Second, there is no pre-existing material distinct from Him when considering the first creation. Third, there are no other coexisting beings with Him at His level that could serve as instruments. The only remaining possibility is the fourth: mediation through a chain of effects. This is exactly what the philosophers propose. But Ghazali will then go on to refute even that possibility, as seen in the next lesson.

Key Terms
اختلاف القوى
اختلاف المواد
اختلاف الآلات
بالتوسط
المبدأ الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali accepts mediation as a valid explanation for how God creates multiplicity.
Correction: Ghazali only says that mediation is the only possibility left within the philosophers' framework; he will later refute it.

Checkpoint
Which of the four possible sources of multiplicity does Ghazali say is the only one that could apply to the First Principle?
A) Difference of powers
B) Difference of materials
C) Difference of instruments
D) Multiplicity through mediation

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's analysis shows that a simple God cannot produce multiplicity through multiple powers, materials, or instruments; only a chain of causation remains as a possibility, which he later challenges.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the immense diversity in creation—from galaxies to ants—do you unconsciously imagine God as having 'multiple faculties' or 'preferences' like a human being? How does Ghazali’s insistence on God’s absolute simplicity challenge that tendency, and what would it mean to resolutely believe that all of this flows from a perfectly simple will?
?
Which of the four possible sources of multiplicity do you most naturally assume in your daily understanding of how events happen? For instance, do you think that God's actions are constrained by the 'nature' of things (like the sun example) or that He uses 'instruments' like angels in a semi-autonomous way? How might this assumption subtly weaken your reliance on God alone?
?
If you truly internalized that no material, tool, or intermediate cause ultimately explains why things are the way they are—that everything goes back to a single, indivisible divine act—would you worry less about secondary causes (like people, circumstances, or your own efforts) and trust God more directly? What specific situation in your life right now would be most affected by such a shift?

Causality and Created Things

The Reality of Composites in Creation
Ghazali lists composite beings to show reality contradicts the simple chain model.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ghazali uses the existence of composites—bodies, humans, and celestial spheres—to disprove the philosophers' assumption of a simple, linear chain of causes.

Core Idea
Composites like body and soul cannot arise from a simple linear chain.

Ghazali begins his refutation by challenging the very picture of reality assumed by the philosophers. He points out that if the world were truly a chain of simple, independently existing units, then every being would be a simple atom. But observation and reason show that bodies, humans, and even the heavens are composites. This sets the stage for the dilemma that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional statement
Understand Ghazali's 'if...then' construction: if no composites, then all would be linear simples.
This is a reductio ad absurdum structure.
2
Identify the composites
Note the three examples: body (form+matter), human (body+soul), celestial sphere (body+rational soul).
These are the philosophers' own positions.
3
Notice the causal independence
In each composite, the parts do not produce each other; they come from a separate cause.
This undermines the idea of a single cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا أن لا يكون في العالم شيء واحد مركب من أفراد، قلنا: فيلزم من ذلك أن تكون الموجودات كلها أحادا، وكل واحد معلولا لواحد آخر فوقه، وعلة لآخر تحته، إلى أن ينتهي إلى معلول لا معلول له، كما انتهى في جهة التصاعد إلى علة لا علة لها، وليس كذلك، فإن الجسم عندهم مركب من صورة وهيولى، وقد صار باجتماعهما شيئاً واحداً، والإنسان مركب من جسم ونفس، وليس أحدهما من الآخر، بل وجودهما جميعاً من علة أخرى، والفلك عندهم كذلك، فإنه جرم ذو نفس، لم تحدث النفس بالجرم، ولا الجرم بالنفس، بل كلاهما صادران من علة سواهما. فكيف وجدت هذه المركبات، أمن علة واحدة؟! فيبطل قولهم: لا يصدر من الواحد إلا واحد، أو من علة مركبة؟! فيتوجه السؤال في تركيب العلة، إلى أن يلتقي بالضرورة مركب بسيط، فإن المبدأ بسيط، وفي الآخر تركيب، ولا يتصور ذلك إلا بالالتقاء، وحيث التقاء يبطل قولهم: إن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد.
If there were no single thing in the world composed of individuals, we say: then it would follow that all existents are single units, each one being an effect of one above it and a cause of one below it, until it ends with an effect that has no effect, just as it ended in the ascending direction with a cause that has no cause. But it is not so; for the body according to them is composed of form and matter, and together they become one thing. And man is composed of body and soul, neither of which is from the other; rather, their existence together is from another cause. And the sphere according to them is like that: it is a body with a soul, the soul not originated from the body, nor the body from the soul; rather, both proceed from a cause other than them. So how did these composites arise? From a single cause? That would falsify their statement: 'From the one only one proceeds.' Or from a composite cause? Then the question arises concerning the composition of the cause, until necessarily a simple composite is encountered. For the First Principle is simple, and at the other end there is composition, and that is inconceivable without meeting; and wherever meeting occurs, their statement that 'the one does not produce except one' is falsified.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is the decisive refutation of the philosophers' principle. Ghazali sets up a conditional: if there were no composites in the world (i.e., everything were a simple, independently existing entity linked in a linear chain), then the emanation scheme might work. But reality is full of composites: bodies are composed of form (صورة) and matter (هيولى); humans are composed of body and soul, which do not produce each other but come from a separate cause; the celestial spheres are also composites of body and rational soul. These composites cannot arise from a single simple cause, because they involve two distinct principles. If the philosophers say they come from a single cause, that contradicts their principle. If they say from a composite cause, then that cause itself requires explanation, leading to an infinite regress until we reach a simple cause that produces a composite effect—which is impossible without some 'meeting' (التقاء) point between simplicity and composition. That meeting would violate the principle. Thus the philosophers' axiom collapses.

Key Terms
مركب
بسيط
صورة
هيولى
نفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the philosophers' emanation scheme accommodates composites easily.
Correction: Ghazali shows that composites like body+soul require a cause external to both, which contradicts the idea that from one simple cause only one simple effect emerges.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the relationship between the body and soul in a human?
A) The soul is caused by the body.
B) The body is caused by the soul.
C) Both are caused by a separate cause.
D) They are the same thing.

Key Takeaway
The existence of composites like body, soul, and sphere disproves the notion that reality is a simple linear chain of causes.

?

?
If every composite in this world, including your own body-soul union, requires an external cause, what does that demand from you right now in terms of recognition and gratitude toward that Cause?
?
Ghazali shows that the linear model fails with composites. What linear model of success or relationship have you been relying on that may be blind to the true, composite nature of your life and its dependence on Allah?

The Dilemma of Composite Causes
Ghazali poses a dilemma and concludes that the philosophers' principle is false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's dilemma: a composite effect must come either from a single simple cause (contradicting the philosophers' principle) or from a composite cause (leading to an infinite regress), and the necessity of a 'meeting' between simplicity and composition falsifies the principle.

Core Idea
A composite effect cannot come from a simple cause without violating the 'only one' rule.

Having established that composites are real, Ghazali now poses a devastating dilemma. If composites arise from a single simple cause, that violates the philosophers' own axiom that from the one only one proceeds. If they arise from a composite cause, then that cause itself requires explanation, leading to a regress. Eventually a simple cause must produce a composite effect, which is impossible without some 'meeting' (التقاء) between the cause and the effect—a meeting that itself would be a violation of the principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma
First option: composite from a single cause refutes the axiom. Second: composite cause leads to regress.
Two horns of the dilemma.
2
Understand the regress
If the cause is composite, we must ask about its own composition, leading to infinite regress unless we reach a simple cause.
3
Grasp the 'meeting' (التقاء) argument
A simple cause producing a composite effect requires a meeting between simplicity and composition, which is impossible without violating the principle.
This is the decisive blow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Philosophers' Classification of Existents
The philosophers offer a detailed classification of existents to defend their position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hierarchy of existents as presented by the philosophers and how they use it to argue that composite existence is not a problem.

Core Idea
The philosophers divide all existents into a hierarchy to show the world's composition is orderly.

In this passage, Imam Ghazali presents the philosophers' own defense against his critique. They argue that once their full classification of existents is understood, the objection about composites dissolves. This lesson unpacks that classification and its underlying logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' defense
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure of the argument.
2
Trace the classification tree
Identify the first division: what is in a substrate vs. what is not. Then further subdivisions.
Pay attention to the terms 'substrate' (محال) and 'substance' (جوهر).
3
Understand accidents and circular motion
The philosophers explain that accidents are originated and trace back to circular motion as a perpetual principle.
This part is crucial for their causal account.
4
Identify the self-subsistent principles
The passage claims the real discussion is about three self-subsistent principles: bodies, abstract intellects, and souls (implied).
The passage ends mid-sentence, so this part is incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: إذا عرف مذهبنا، اندفع الإشكال، فإن الموجودات تنقسم: إلى ما هي في محال، كالأعراض والصور؛ وإلى ما ليست في محال؛ وهذه تنقسم: إلى ما هي محال لغيرها كالأجسام، وإلى ما ليست بمحال كالموجودات التي هي جواهر قائمة بأنفسها، وهي تنقسم إلى ما يؤثر في الأجسام ونسميها نفوساً، وإلى ما لا يؤثر في الأجسام، بل في النفوس، ونسميها عقولا مجردة. أما الموجودات التي تحل في المحال كالأعراض، فهي حادثة، ولها علل حادثة، وتنتهي إلى مبدأ هو حادث من وجه دائم من وجه، وهو الحركة الدورية. وليس الكلام فيها. وإنما الكلام في الأصول القائمة بأنفسها لا في محال، وهي ثلاثة: أجسام، وهي أخسها. وعقول مجردة، وهي التي لا تتعلق بالأجسام
For it is said: If our school is known, the difficulty is removed. For existents are divided into what is in a substrate, like accidents and forms; and what is not in a substrate. And these are divided into what is a substrate for others, like bodies; and what is not a substrate, like existents that are substances subsisting by themselves. And they are divided into what affects bodies, which we call souls; and what does not affect bodies but affects souls, which we call abstract intellects. As for existents that occur in substrates, like accidents, they are originated, and they have originated causes, and they end with a principle that is originated in one respect and perpetual in another, which is circular motion. And there is no discussion about them. Rather the discussion is about the principles subsisting by themselves not in a substrate, and they are three: bodies, which are the basest; and abstract intellects, which are those that do not relate to bodies
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a defense put in the mouth of the philosophers. They argue that once their full system is understood, the objection about composites dissolves. They provide a classification of all existents: (1) those that inhere in a substrate, i.e., accidents and forms—these are temporary and caused by other accidents, ultimately tracing back to circular motion; (2) those that do not inhere in a substrate. The latter are further subdivided: (a) those that serve as substrates for others, i.e., bodies; (b) those that are not substrates, i.e., self-subsistent substances. Among these, some affect bodies (souls) and some affect souls (abstract intellects). The philosophers claim the real discussion concerns these self-subsistent principles, and that the world's composition comes from the interaction of these types. However, the passage ends abruptly, and Ghazali presumably goes on to refute this classification. The incomplete nature of the OCR makes it difficult to see the full argument, but the philosophers are trying to show that the problem of composites is only apparent because different categories have different causal origins.

عرض
جوهر
نفس
عقل مجرد
حركة دورية

Refuting 'From One Only One' via Composition
Ghazali argues that composition in the world refutes the emanation principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the existence of composite beings to refute the philosophical principle that from one simple cause only one effect can proceed.

Core Idea
Composites in the world force a contradiction: the cause must be one or composite—both break the rule.

Ghazali challenges the emanationist principle by examining the world around us. If from the One only one proceeds, then everything should be a simple atom in a causal chain. But we see composites like bodies and humans, which require multiple principles. This observation sets up a dilemma that undermines the entire emanation scheme.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle being refuted
The philosophers' principle states: 'From one simple cause, only one effect can directly proceed.' This is the target of Ghazali's argument.
This principle is central to Neoplatonic emanation.
2
Follow Ghazali's reductio assumption
If the principle held strictly, all existents would be simple atoms, each caused by one above and causing one below, forming a linear chain.
No composites would exist in such a world.
3
Identify the composites Ghazali lists
Ghazali points to bodies (form + matter), humans (body + soul), and spheres (body + soul) as clear examples of composites found in reality.
These composites are not simple; they involve two principles.
4
Grasp the dilemma
Either these composites come from one cause (violates 'from one only one') or from a composite cause (requires explanation of that cause's composition). Both options refute the principle.
The argument forces a meeting of simple and composite, which is impossible under the emanation rule.
5
Conclude the refutation
Since composites exist and cannot be derived from a single simple cause without contradiction, the principle 'From one only one' is invalid.
This shows that multiplicity can arise from the One through means that the philosophers cannot account for.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آلات إذ لا موجود مع الله تعالى في رتبته ، الله تعالى في رتبته ، فالكلام في حدوث الآلة الأولى ، فلم يبق إلا أن تكون الكثرة في العالم صادرة عن الله تعالى بطريق التوسط كما سبق . وجود هذا أن لا يكون في العالم شيء واحد مركب من أفراد ، قلنا : فيلزم من بل تكون الموجودات كلها أحادا ، وكل واحد معلولا لواحد آخر فوقه ، وعلة لآخر تحته ، إلى أن ينتهى إلى معلول لا معلول له ، كما انتهى في جهة التصاعد إلى علة لا علة لها ، وليس كذلك ، فإن الجسم عندهم مركب من صورة وهيولى ، وقد صار باجتماعهما شيئاً واحداً ، والإنسان مركب من جسم ونفس ، وليس أحدهما من الآخر ، بل وجودهما جميعاً ، بل وجودهما جميعاً من علة أخرى ، والفلك عندهم كذلك ، فإنه جرم ذو نفس ، لم تحدث النفس بالجرم ، ولا الجرم بالنفس ، بل كلاهما صادران من علة سواهما . فكيف وجدت هذه المركبات ، أمن علة واحدة ؟ ! فيبطل قولهم : لا يصدر من الواحد إلا واحد ، أو من علة مركبة ؟ ! فيتوجه السؤال فى تركيب العلة ، إلى أن يلتقى – بالضرورة – مركب بسيط ، فإن المبدأ بسيط ، وفى الآخر تركيب ، ولا يتصور ذلك إلا بالالتقاء ، وحيث التقاء يبطل قولهم : إن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد يقع .
Instruments since there is no existent with God Almighty in His rank, God Almighty in His rank, so the discussion is about the origination of the first instrument. So there remains only that the multiplicity in the world emanates from God Almighty by way of mediation as previously. There is this: that there is no single thing in the world composed of individuals. We say: then it would necessitate that all existents are atoms, each one caused by another above it, and cause for another below it, until it reaches a caused with no cause, just as it ended in the ascending direction to a cause with no cause. But it is not so, for the body according to them is composed of form and matter, and by their conjunction it became one thing, and man is composed of body and soul, and neither is from the other, rather their existence both together, rather their existence both together from another cause. And the sphere according to them is likewise, for it is a body with a soul; the soul did not arise from the body, nor the body from the soul, but both emanate from a cause other than them. So how were these composites found? From one cause? Then their statement is invalidated: 'From the one only one proceeds', or from a composite cause? Then the question arises about the composition of the cause, until – necessarily – a composite and a simple meet. For the principle is simple, and at the end there is composition, and that cannot be conceived except by meeting. And where there is meeting, their statement is invalidated: 'The one does not proceed from it except one' falls.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by Ghazali of the philosophers' principle that from one simple cause only one effect can directly proceed. He begins by noting that if there is no other existent co-eternal with God, then all multiplicity comes through mediation. He then constructs a reductio: if the philosophers' principle were strictly applied, then all existents would be simple atoms, each caused by one above and causing one below, leading to an infinite chain of causes and effects without any composite. However, the world clearly contains composites: bodies composed of form and matter, humans composed of body and soul, spheres composed of body and soul. These composites arise from two distinct principles that are not causally related to each other in a linear chain. Ghazali then poses a dilemma: either these composites come from one cause (contradicting the principle 'from one only one') or from a composite cause, which would require an explanation of the cause's composition. Eventually, the argument forces a meeting between a simple principle and composite effects, which undermines the rule that from one only one can proceed.

Key Terms
الآلة الأولى (the first instrument)
الكثرة (multiplicity)
مركب (composite)
بسيط (simple)
علة (cause)
معلول (effect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers deny all composition in the world; they believe everything is simple.
Correction: The philosophers accept composition in the world (e.g., bodies, humans, spheres) but claim that the direct emanation from the First Principle is simple; they do not deny composition altogether.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali use to refute the principle 'From one only one'?
A) The existence of atoms
B) The existence of composites in the world
C) The infinite regress of causes
D) The nature of the soul

Key Takeaway
The existence of composites in the world forces a contradiction that disproves the philosophical principle 'From one only one'.

What specific steps can you take this week to remind yourself of God's boundless creativity?
?

How might this affect your trust in His ability to handle the complexities of your life—your struggles, relationships, and future?
?
The philosophers' principle reduces God's action to a mechanical necessity. Are there areas in your life where you treat God's guidance or provision as a predictable, mechanical process rather than the free act of a living, personal Lord? What would it mean for you to truly expect the unexpected from Him?

The First Intellect and Its Emanation
Introduction to emanation: First Intellect and its threefold result.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' view of the heavens as animate and the emanation of the First Intellect with its threefold result.

Core Idea
The First Intellect is the first emanated being, from which proceed an intellect, a soul, and a body.

This lesson introduces the emanation scheme of the philosophers. Ghazali begins by describing the ten bodies and the animate heavens, then presents the first emanation from the First Principle: the First Intellect, which is a self-subsistent intellect that knows itself and its principle. From this First Intellect, three things necessarily follow: a second intellect, the soul of the outermost sphere, and its body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the ten bodies
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Ghazali presents the heavens as animals with bodies and souls.
This sets the stage for emanation.
2
Identify the First Intellect
The text describes the First Intellect as a self-subsistent, non-bodily being that knows itself and its principle. Find the description and note its key attributes.
The First Intellect is the first effect from the First Principle.
3
List the three things that follow
The passage states that from the First Intellect follows three things: an intellect, the soul of the outermost sphere, and its body. Identify each.
This triad is the origin of the emanation chain.
4
Reflect on the starting point
Consider why the philosophers begin with an intellect that knows itself and its principle. How does this shape the rest of the scheme?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأجسام عشرة ، تسع سموات ، والعاشر المادة التي هي حشو مقعر فلك القمر ، والسموات التسع حيوانات لها أجرام ونفوس ، ولها ترتيب في الوجود كما نذكره . وهو أن المبدأ الأول فاض من وجوده العقل الأول ، وهو موجود قائم بنفسه ، ليس بجسم ولا منطبع في جسم ، يعرف نفسه ويعرف مبدأه ، وقد سميناه العقل الأول ، ولا مشاحة في الأسامى - يسمى ملكاً أو عقلا أو ما أريد - ويلزم عن ثلاثة أمور : عقل ، ونفس الفلك الأقصى - وهو السماء التاسعة – وجرم الفلك الأقصى ؛ ثم لزم من العقل الثانى : عقل ثالث ، ونفس فلك الكواكب ، وجرمه ؛ تم لزم من العقل الثالث : عقل رابع ، ونفس فلك زحل ، وجرمه ؛ ولزم من العقل الرابع : عقل خامس ، ونفس فلك المشترى ، وجرمه : وهكذا، حتى انتهى إلى العقل الذى لزم منه : عقل ، ونفسه فلك القمر ، وجرمه . والعقل الأخير هو الذي يسمى العقل الفعال ؛ ولزم حشو فلك القمر . - وهى المادة القابلة للكون والفساد - من العقل الفعال وطبائع الأفلاك – ثم إن المواد تمتزج بسبب حركات الكواكب، امتزاجات مختلفة ، تحصل منها المعادن والنبات والحيوانات . ولا يلزم أن يلزم من كل عقل عقل إلى غير نهاية ، لأن هذه العقول مختلفة الأنواع ، فمنذ ثبت لواحد لا يلزم الآخر فيخرج منه أن العقول بعد المبدأ الأول عشرة ، والأفلاك تسعة ، ومجموع هذه المبادئ الشريفة - بعد المبدأ الأول - تسعة عشر . وحصل منه : أن تحت كل عقل من العقول الأول (١) ثلاثة أشياء ؛ عقل ، ونفس فلك ، وجرمه ؛ فلابد أن يكون فى مبدئه تثليث لا محالة ، ولا تتصور كثرة فى المعلول الأول إلا (1) يريد بذلك إخراج العقل العاشر فقط
Then the bodies are ten: nine heavens, and the tenth is the matter which is the filling of the concave of the sphere of the moon. The nine heavens are animals having bodies and souls, and they have an order in existence as we will mention. It is that the First Principle overflowed from His existence the First Intellect, which is an existent subsisting by itself, not a body nor immanent in a body, knowing itself and knowing its principle. We have called it the First Intellect – and there is no quarrel about names; it may be called an angel, an intellect, or whatever is desired – and there follows from it three things: an intellect, the soul of the outermost sphere – which is the ninth heaven – and the body of the outermost sphere. Then from the second intellect follows a third intellect, the soul of the sphere of the fixed stars, and its body. Then from the third intellect follows a fourth intellect, the soul of the sphere of Saturn, and its body. Then from the fourth intellect follows a fifth intellect, the soul of the sphere of Jupiter, and its body. And so on, until it ended with the intellect from which follows: an intellect, the soul of the sphere of the moon, and its body. The last intellect is called the Active Intellect. And from the Active Intellect and the natures of the spheres follows the filling of the sphere of the moon – that is, the matter receptive of generation and corruption. Then the materials mix due to the movements of the planets in various mixtures, from which result minerals, plants, and animals. And it does not necessitate that from every intellect an intellect follows ad infinitum, because these intellects are of different types. Since it is established for one, the other does not follow, so it follows that the intellects after the First Principle are ten, and the spheres are nine, and the total of these noble principles – after the First Principle – is nineteen. And it results from this that under each of the first intellects (1) are three things: an intellect, the soul of a sphere, and its body. Therefore, there must be in its principle a trinity without fail, and multiplicity in the first effect cannot be conceived except (1) meaning thereby excluding only the tenth intellect.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage details the philosophers' emanation scheme. It begins by stating that bodies are ten: nine heavens and the sublunar matter. The nine heavens are animate beings with bodies and souls. Then it presents the emanation from the First Principle: the First Intellect emanates, which is a self-subsistent, non-bodily being that knows itself and its principle. From this First Intellect proceed three things: a second intellect, the soul of the outermost sphere (the ninth heaven), and its body. From the second intellect proceed a third intellect, the soul of the sphere of fixed stars, and its body. This pattern continues through the spheres of Saturn, Jupiter, and so on, until from the ninth intellect proceed the tenth intellect (Active Intellect), the soul of the moon's sphere, and its body. From the Active Intellect and the natures of the spheres comes the sublunar matter, which mixes to produce minerals, plants, and animals. The passage emphasizes that this series is finite because the intellects are of different types. The total number of intellects after the First Principle is ten, spheres nine, making nineteen noble principles. Each intellect (except possibly the last) gives rise to three things, implying a trinity in its principle.

Key Terms
العقل الأول
النفس
الجرم
الفلك الأقصى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The First Intellect is a material body.
Correction: The text explicitly states it is 'not a body nor immanent in a body'.

Checkpoint
How many things follow from the First Intellect?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
The First Intellect is the first emanated being, from which a triad of intellect, soul, and body necessarily proceeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The First Intellect knows itself and its Principle. Do you truly know yourself and your Creator, or are you distracted by the external world? What would it take to cultivate that direct knowledge today?
?
The nine heavens are said to be living beings with souls. If you saw the universe as alive and responsive to God, how would your prayers and awe change right now?
?
Emanation is a necessary outflow—yet your own faith is a choice. Do you live as if your faith is a mere effect of causes, or do you actively choose submission to the One from whom all existence flows?

The Chain of Intellects and Spheres
Emanation chain from second intellect to active intellect and sublunar matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will trace the emanation chain from the second intellect to the active intellect and understand how sublunar matter arises.

Core Idea
Nine intellects and nine spheres emanate in a descending chain, culminating in the Active Intellect governing the sublunar realm.

This lesson continues the emanation scheme. From the second intellect onward, a pattern repeats: each intellect gives rise to a lower intellect, the soul of a sphere, and its body. This chain proceeds through the spheres of the fixed stars, Saturn, Jupiter, and so on, until the tenth intellect (the Active Intellect) and the sphere of the moon. The sublunar matter, receptive of generation and corruption, then emanates from the Active Intellect and the natures of the spheres.

Guided Path
1
Read the chain passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'ثم لزم من العقل الثانى' to 'تحصل منها المعادن والنبات والحيوانات'. Follow the chain step by step.
The pattern is repetitive; notice the names of the spheres.
2
Identify the pattern
Each intellect gives rise to three things: a lower intellect (next in line), the soul of a sphere, and the body of that sphere. Verify this for at least two steps.
This is the trinity of emanation.
3
Locate the Active Intellect
The last intellect is called the Active Intellect. What does it govern? How does sublunar matter come about?
The Active Intellect is the tenth intellect.
4
Understand sublunar generation
The text says that from the Active Intellect and the natures of the spheres, the matter of the sublunar sphere arises. Then minerals, plants, and animals form from mixtures due to planetary motions.
This connects the celestial to the terrestrial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage describes an impersonal chain of necessary causes from intellect to intellect, culminating in the material world. Do you live your daily life aware of the ultimate Origin of all things, or do you get lost in intermediate causes? What would change if you constantly reminded yourself that even the Active Intellect is a created being dependent on the First?
?
In this scheme, the physical world—including your own body—arises from the mixing of materials due to planetary movements. Does this make your body seem distant from God, or does it fill you with awe at the intricate layers of causation He set up? How does this affect your gratitude and humility before Him?
?
Consider the last step: from the Active Intellect and natures of spheres, matter emerges, then mixing, then life. You are part of that life. Does this chain of causes make you feel like a product of necessity, or do you see it as a sign of God's wisdom and power? What can you do today to connect more directly with the Creator rather than the chain?

Why the Series Is Finite and the Trinity of Each Intellect
Explanation of finite series and trinity in each intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers assert that the intellect series is finite (ten intellects) and why each intellect (except the last) necessitates a trinity in its emanation.

Core Idea
The intellects are of different types, so the series cannot be infinite; each intellect gives rise to three things, implying a trinity at its source.

This lesson addresses two key principles of the emanation scheme: the finitude of the intellect series and the inherent trinity in each emanated intellect. Ghazali explains that because the intellects are of different types, the series cannot continue indefinitely. The total count after the First Principle is nineteen: ten intellects and nine spheres. Moreover, each of the first nine intellects (excluding the last?) produces three things, indicating a trinity in its principle. This is a crucial point for understanding the philosophers' metaphysical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on finitude
Read from 'ولا يلزم أن يلزم من كل عقل' to the end. Observe the argument against infinite regress.
The key phrase is 'لأن هذه العقول مختلفة الأنواع'.
2
Count the intellects and spheres
The passage gives the numbers: intellects after First Principle are ten, spheres are nine, total nineteen. Verify this by thinking about the chain.
This is the sum of noble principles.
3
Understand the trinity claim
The passage states that under each 'first intellect' (likely the nine intellects from the first to the ninth) there are three things: an intellect, a soul, and a body. This implies a trinity in its principle.
The footnote excludes the tenth intellect (Active Intellect) from this trinity.
4
Reflect on the philosophical implications
Why is it important that the series is finite? How does the trinity relate to the Neoplatonic concept of procession?
This connects to the learning objective.

How many intellects exist after the First Principle according to the philosophers?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson presents a logical system that reduces the order of creation to a neat chain of nineteen intellects and spheres. Do you find yourself tempted to rely on such systematic explanations to make God's actions predictable? How does that temptation distance you from the awe of His absolute freedom and transcendence? Will you commit today to intentionally pause in your worship and acknowledge that God's ways are beyond human comprehension, even when a system seems satisfying?
?
The philosophers here speak of a 'necessary trinity' in the first effect, implying that God's creative act must follow a certain pattern. Are you willing to accept that God's creation is not bound by any necessity, but is purely His free will? How does that affect your trust in His decrees, especially when events seem chaotic or illogical? Will you surrender your need for a tidy explanation and instead submit to His wisdom in uncertainty?
?
This passage highlights the finitude of the intellect series—there is an end to what human reasoning can uncover about the unseen. Are you comfortable with the limits of your own intellect? Or do you secretly believe that with enough effort you could fully understand God's plan? How can you cultivate a humble heart that finds peace in mystery rather than frustration? What concrete step will you take this week to embrace intellectual humility in your faith?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

The Emanation of the Ten Intellects and Nine Spheres
Ghazali describes the emanation chain from First Intellect to sublunary world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' cosmological scheme of emanation from the First Principle through ten intellects and nine spheres, as described by Ghazali.

Core Idea
Emanation fills the cosmos from the One through a cascade of intellects and spheres.

This passage presents the core of the philosophers' emanationist cosmology. Before Ghazali refutes it, he carefully lays out their system. Here you will see how they explain the existence of the entire universe as a necessary overflow from the First Principle, step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is detailed; take your time.
2
Identify the first intellect and its three effects
Notice that from the First Principle overflows the First Intellect, which immediately brings about three things: another intellect, the soul of the outermost sphere, and its body.
This pattern repeats for each subsequent intellect.
3
Trace the chain down to the Active Intellect
Follow the sequence from the second intellect to the tenth (Active Intellect), noting which sphere corresponds to each intellect.
The spheres are in order: outermost, fixed stars, Saturn, Jupiter, etc., down to the moon.
4
Note the total number of intellects and spheres
The passage concludes that after the First Principle there are ten intellects and nine spheres, totalling nineteen noble principles.

وهو أن المبدأ الأول فاض من وجوده العقل الأول، وهو موجود قائم بنفسه، ليس بجسم ولا منطبع في جسم، يعرف نفسه ويعرف مبدأه، وقد سميناه العقل الأول، ولا مشاحة في الأسامى - يسمى ملكاً أو عقلا أو ما أريد - ويلزم عن ثلاثة أمور : عقل، ونفس الفلك الأقصى - وهو السماء التاسعة – وجرم الفلك الأقصى؛ ثم لزم من العقل الثانى : عقل ثالث، ونفس فلك الكواكب، وجرمه؛ ثم لزم من العقل الثالث : عقل رابع، ونفس فلك زحل، وجرمه؛ ولزم من العقل الرابع : عقل خامس، ونفس فلك المشترى، وجرمه : وهكذا، حتى انتهى إلى العقل الذى لزم منه : عقل، ونفسه فلك القمر، وجرمه . والعقل الأخير هو الذي يسمى العقل الفعال؛ ولزم حشو فلك القمر . وهى المادة القابلة للكون والفساد - من العقل الفعال وطبائع الأفلاك – ثم إن المواد تمتزج بسبب حركات الكواكب، امتزاجات مختلفة، تحصل منها المعادن والنبات والحيوانات ولا يلزم أن يلزم من كل عقل عقل إلى غير نهاية، لأن هذه العقول مختلفة الأنواع، فمنذ ثبت لواحد لا يلزم الآخر فيخرج منه أن العقول بعد المبدأ الأول عشرة، والأفلاك تسعة، ومجموع هذه المبادئ الشريفة - بعد المبدأ الأول - تسعة عشر . وحصل منه : أن تحت كل عقل من العقول الأول ثلاثة أشياء؛ عقل، ونفس فلك، وجرمه؛ فلابد أن يكون فى مبدئه تثليث لا محالة، ولا تتصور كثرة فى المعلول الأول إلا من وجه واحد، وهو أنه يعقل مبدأه، ويعقل نفسه، وهو باعتبار ذاته ممكن الوجود، لأن وجوب وجوده بغيره، لا بنفسه، وهذه معان ثلاثة مختلفة، والأشرف من المعلولات الثلاثة ينبغى أن ينسب إلى الأشرف من هذه المعانى، فيصدر منه العقل، من حيث إنه يعقل مبدأه، وتصدر منه نفس الفلك، من حيث إنه يعقل نفسه، ويصدر منه جرم الفلك، من حيث إنه ممكن الوجود بذاته . فينبغى أن يقال : هذا التثليث من أين حصل في المعلول الأول ، ومبدؤه واحد ؟! فنقول : لم يصدر من المبدأ الأول إلا واحد، وهو ذات هذا العقل الذي به يعقل نفسه، ولزمه - ضرورة، لا من جهة المبدأ - أن عقل المبدأ وهو في ذاته ممكن الوجود، وليس له الإمكان من المبدأ الأول، بل هو ونحن لا نبعد أن يوجد من الواحد واحد ويلزم ذات المعلول – لا من جهة المبدأ بل من جهته - أمور ضرورية، إضافية أو غير إضافية، فتحصل بسببه كثرة، ويصير بذلك مبدأ لوجود الكثرة؛ فعلى هذا الوجه يمكن أن يلتقى المركب بالبسيط، إذ لابد من الالتقاء، ولا يمكن إلا كذلك، فهو الذي يجب الحكم به . فهذا هو القول في تفهيم مذهبهم
And it is that the First Principle overflowed from His existence the First Intellect, a self-subsisting existent, not a body nor imprinted in a body, knowing itself and knowing its principle, and we have called it the First Intellect, and there is no contention over names—it may be called an angel, an intellect, or whatever is desired. And from it follows necessarily from three matters: an intellect, and the soul of the outermost sphere—which is the ninth heaven—and the body of the outermost sphere. Then from the second intellect follows a third intellect, and the soul of the sphere of the fixed stars, and its body. Then from the third intellect follows a fourth intellect, and the soul of Saturn's sphere, and its body. And from the fourth intellect follows a fifth intellect, and the soul of Jupiter's sphere, and its body. And so on, until it reaches the intellect from which follows: an intellect, and the soul of the moon's sphere, and its body. And the last intellect is called the Active Intellect. And the filler of the moon's sphere—which is the matter receptive of generation and corruption—follows from the Active Intellect and the natures of the spheres. Then the matters mix due to the movements of the planets, various mixtures, from which come minerals, plants, and animals. And it is not necessary that from every intellect an intellect follows ad infinitum, because these intellects are of different species. So since it is established for one, the other does not necessarily follow; thus it follows that the intellects after the First Principle are ten, the spheres nine, and the total of these noble principles—after the First Principle—are nineteen. And it results from this that under each of the first intellects there are three things: an intellect, a soul of a sphere, and its body. Therefore, there must be in its principle a trinity necessarily, and multiplicity is not conceivable in the first effect except from one aspect, namely that it knows its principle, and knows itself, and it is in itself possible of existence, because the necessity of its existence is through another, not through itself. And these are three different meanings. The noblest of the three effects should be attributed to the noblest of these meanings. So from it issues the intellect insofar as it knows its principle; and from it issues the soul of the sphere insofar as it knows itself; and from it issues the body of the sphere insofar as it is possible in itself. So it should be said: This trinity—from where did it come about in the first effect while its principle is one? So we say: Nothing issued from the First Principle except one thing, which is the essence of this intellect by which it knows itself, and it necessarily follows—not from the direction of the principle—that it knows the principle and that it is in itself possible of existence, and its possibility is not from the First Principle, rather it is. And we do not consider it far that from the one only one issues, and there follow from the essence of the effect—not from the direction of the principle but from its own direction—necessary matters, relational or non-relational, so multiplicity comes about through it, and it thereby becomes a principle for the existence of multiplicity. Thus in this way it is possible for the composite to meet the simple, for connection is necessary and cannot be otherwise, and this is what must be judged. So this is the statement explaining their doctrine.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' emanationist cosmology. It begins by stating that from the First Principle overflows the First Intellect, which is a self-subsistent immaterial being. This intellect necessarily brings about three things: another intellect, the soul of the outermost sphere, and the body of that sphere. The process continues through nine spheres, each producing a lower intellect, a soul, and a body. The final intellect is the Active Intellect, which gives rise to the sublunary matter. The passage then explains the rationale for triplicity: the first intellect knows its principle (producing the next intellect), knows itself (producing the soul), and is possible in itself (producing the body). This shows how multiplicity arises from the simple First Principle, and it concludes that the total number of principles after the First is nineteen (ten intellects and nine spheres). The explanation is detailed and follows the sequence of emanation.

Key Terms
العقل الأول
النفس
جرم الفلك
العقل الفعال

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many intellects exist after the First Principle?
A) 9
B) 10
C) 19
D) 20

Key Takeaway
The philosophers propose a necessary emanation of ten intellects and nine spheres from the First Principle, culminating in the sublunary world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage describes the cosmos as a necessary series of emanations from the First Principle. Do you find this deterministic view comforting or troubling? How does it compare to your own belief in God's absolute free will and power?
?
The intellects are said to know themselves and their principle. Where in your daily life do you actively seek to know yourself and your Creator, and where do you passively rely on assumptions?
?
If the philosophers' chain of causality is wrong, then the world is not necessary but contingent upon God's will. How does that affect your sense of purpose and dependence on God in every moment?

Causality and Created Things

The Philosophical Justification of Multiplicity from the One
Ghazali explains the philosophers' rationale for the triplicity of each intellect's effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers explain the emergence of multiplicity (intellect, soul, body) from a single intellect, and how this is consistent with the principle that from the One only one proceeds.

Core Idea
Multiplicity in the effect arises from its own nature, not from the First Principle.

The philosophers' emanation scheme seems to violate the principle that from the simple One only one can proceed. Here Ghazali presents their defense: the three outcomes of each intellect are not direct emanations from the First Principle but necessary concomitants of the intellect's own essence. This passage explains the triplicity and how it is possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the explanation of the three aspects
Understand that the first intellect has three aspects: knowing its principle, knowing itself, and being possible in itself. Each aspect gives rise to a different effect.
The three aspects are distinct, explaining the three outcomes.
2
See how each aspect produces a different effect
The intellect's knowledge of its principle produces the next intellect; its knowledge of itself produces the soul; its possibility produces the sphere's body.
The noblest effect (intellect) corresponds to the noblest aspect (knowledge of principle).
3
Understand the response to the objection
The objection is that multiplicity cannot come from the simple One. The response: the One directly causes only one thing (the intellect's essence), but the intellect's own multiple aspects lead to multiple effects necessarily, without implying multiplicity in the First Principle.
This preserves the principle 'from the one only one proceeds'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that all multiplicity and imperfection in your life comes from your own possible nature and not from Allah, would you complain less about your circumstances and instead turn inwards to purify your soul? Write down one complaint you have made recently and ask yourself whether its root is in your own possible existence.
?
The noblest effect is attributed to the noblest cause. In your daily worship, do you consciously attribute the good you do to Allah's guidance, or do you take personal credit? How can you remind yourself that every noble action is ultimately from Him?
?
The first intellect’s knowledge of itself produced a soul. When you reflect on your own self-knowledge, does it lead you to more spiritual growth or to pride? What concrete step can you take today to ensure your self-awareness draws you closer to your Creator?

Ghazali's First Objection: The Dilemma of Possibility
Ghazali presents his first objection: the dilemma of possibility vs existence in the first intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first objection to the emanation scheme: the dilemma of whether the intellect's possibility is identical to its existence or distinct, and the implications for multiplicity.

Core Idea
Ghazali traps the philosophers: either possibility equals existence (no multiplicity) or it's distinct (why not in the First?).

In this passage, Ghazali begins his detailed refutation of the emanation theory. He first dismisses the entire scheme as arbitrary and baseless, then zeroes in on a specific logical problem: the claim that the first intellect has 'possibility of existence' as one of its meanings of multiplicity. He asks a simple but devastating question that exposes a dilemma, though the argument is cut short on this page.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the full translation to grasp Ghazali's tone and the structure of his objection.
Notice the strong dismissive language at the beginning.
2
Identify the two parts: dismissal and objection
The passage has a clear break: first Ghazali attacks the philosophers' method, then he presents his first numbered objection. Mark where the objection starts.
The objection begins with 'الأول : هو أنا نقول'.
3
Analyze the dilemma
Ghazali presents a conditional question: is the intellect's possibility identical to its existence or distinct? Consider the consequences of each horn. Ask yourself why this poses a problem for the philosophers.
The text cuts off, but the logical force of the dilemma is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : ما ذكرتموه تحكمات، وهى على التحقيق ظلمات فوق ظلمات، لو حكاها الإنسان عن منام رآه، لاستدل به على سوء مزاجه؛ أو لو أورد جنسه في الفقهيات التى قصارى المطلب فيها تخمينات، لقيل إنه ترهات، لا تفيد غلبات الظنون . ومداخل الاعتراض على مثلها لا تنحصر، ولكنا نورد وجوهاً معدودة : الأول : هو أنا نقول : ادعيتم أن أحد معانى الكثرة في المعلول الأول أنه ممكن الوجود، فنقول : كونه ممكن الوجود، عين وجوده أم غيره ؟! فإن كان عينه، فلا تنشأ عنه كثرة، وإن كان غيره، فهلا قلتم : في المبدأ الأول
We said: What you have mentioned are arbitrary assertions, and in truth they are layers of darkness upon darkness. If a man related them about a dream he had, it would indicate his bad temperament; or if he presented their kind in juridical matters, where the utmost goal is conjectures, it would be said they are nonsense that do not yield even prevailing opinions. And the avenues of objection to such things are not limited, but we will present several numbered aspects. The first: We say: You claim that one of the meanings of multiplicity in the first effect is that it is possible of existence. We say: Is its being possible of existence identical to its existence or other than it? If it is identical, then no multiplicity arises from it. And if it is other, then why do you not say concerning the First Principle...
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is Ghazali's refutation of the emanation scheme. He begins by dismissing their claims as arbitrary (تحكمات) and likens them to nonsense. Then he presents the first of several objections: regarding the concept of possibility (إمكان الوجود) in the first intellect. He asks whether this possibility is identical to its existence or distinct. If identical, it cannot be an additional source of multiplicity. If distinct, then why not attribute a similar multiplicity to the First Principle itself? The argument cuts off here, leaving the objection incomplete.

Key Terms
تحكمات
ممكن الوجود
كثرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali rejects emanation outright based on this single objection.
Correction: Ghazali presents one of several objections; his approach is dialectical, not a wholesale rejection without argument.

Checkpoint
What is the dilemma Ghazali presents regarding the first intellect's being possible of existence (ممكن الوجود)?
A) If possibility is the same as existence, no multiplicity arises; if different, why not attribute multiplicity to the First Principle?
B) If possibility is necessary, then the intellect is eternal; if contingent, the First Principle is not truly one.
C) If possibility is an attribute, it must be eternal; if not, it is created.
D) The intellect's possibility proves it is separate from its essence, leading to infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first objection exposes a logical flaw in the emanation scheme: the concept of possibility cannot serve as a source of multiplicity unless it is distinct from existence, but that would imply multiplicity in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter arguments that seem complex, do you tend to dismiss them as nonsense like Ghazali initially describes, or do you engage with their logical structure? Which approach is closer to the truth in your life?
?
Ghazali traps the philosophers with a dilemma: is possibility identical to existence or not? In your own faith, are there doctrines you hold without examining whether they introduce multiplicity in God's simplicity? Examine one such belief today.
?
The philosophers claimed multiplicity from possibility. Where in your own life have you attributed multiplicity to God's actions without realizing it simplifies His unity? How will you correct that?

The Philosophers' Triple-Aspect Theory of Emanation
Ghazali presents the philosophers' triple-aspect theory of emanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers explain the emergence of multiplicity from a single first principle through the three aspects of the first intellect.

Core Idea
Three aspects in the first intellect produce intellect, soul, and celestial body.

In this passage, Ghazali faithfully presents the philosophers' emanation scheme. He explains how the first effect, the first intellect, has three aspects that give rise to three distinct realities. This theory is the cornerstone of their attempt to reconcile the oneness of God with the multiplicity of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the exposition of the triple-aspect theory
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Ghazali structures the presentation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three aspects and their effects
The first intellect has three aspects: intellecting its principle, intellecting itself, and being possible of existence. Each aspect yields a different effect: the second intellect, the soul of the sphere, and the body of the sphere.
3
Understand the mechanism of multiplicity
Notice that multiplicity arises not from the first principle but from the effect's own aspects. This is supposed to preserve the principle of 'from one proceeds only one.'
The philosophers argue that the first effect's intrinsic multiplicity allows it to be the source of many things.
4
Reflect on the claim
Consider how the philosophers attempt to bridge the gap between the simple and the composite. They claim this is the only way meeting is possible.

من وجه واحد ، وهو أنه يعقل مبدأه ، ويعقل نفسه ، وهو باعتبار ذاته ممكن الوجود ، لأن وجوب وجوده بغيره ، لا بنفسه ، وهذه معان ثلاثة مختلفة ، والأشرف من المعلولات الثلاثة ينبغى أن ينسب إلى الأشرف من هذه المعانى ، فيصدر منه العقل ، من حيث إنه يعقل مبدأه ، وتصدر منه نفس الفلك ، من حيث إنه يعقل نفسه، ويصدر منه جرم الفلك، من حيث إنه ممكن الوجود بذاته . فينبغي أن يقال : هذا التثليث من أين حصل في المعلول الأول ، ومبدؤه واحد ؟ ! فنقول : لم يصدر من المبدأ الأول إلا واحد ، وهو ذات هذا العقل الذي به يعقل نفسه ، ولزمه - ضرورة ، لا من جهة المبدأ - أن عقل المبدأ وهو في ذاته ممكن الوجود ، وليس له الإمكان من المبدأ الأول ، بل هو ونحن لا نبعد أن يوجد من الواحد واحد ويلزم ذات المعلول – لا من جهة المبدأ بل من جهته - أمور ضرورية ، إضافية أو غير إضافية ، فتحصل بسببه كثرة ، ويصير بذلك مبدأ لوجود الكثرة ؛ فعلى هذا الوجه يمكن أن يلتقى المركب بالبسيط ، إذ لابد من الالتقاء ، ولا يمكن إلا كذلك ، فهو الذي يجب الحكم به . فهذا هو القول في تفهيم مذهبهم
قلنا : ما ذكرتموه تحكمات ، وهى على التحقيق ظلمات فوق ظلمات ، لو حكاها الإنسان عن منام رآه ، لاستدل به على سوء مزاجه ؛ أو لو أورد جنسه في الفقهيات التى قصارى المطلب فيها تخمينات ، لقيل إنه ترهات ، لا تفيد غلبات الظنون .
From one aspect: it intellects its principle, and intellects itself, and it is in itself possible of existence, because the necessity of its existence is from another, not from itself. These are three different meanings. The noblest of the three effects should be attributed to the noblest of these meanings. So from it proceeds the intellect, inasmuch as it intellects its principle; from it proceeds the soul of the sphere, inasmuch as it intellects itself; and from it proceeds the body of the sphere, inasmuch as it is possible of existence in itself. So it should be said: whence came this triplicity in the first effect, while its principle is one? We say: from the first principle only one proceeded, which is the essence of this intellect by which it intellects itself, and it necessarily follows—not from the principle—that it intellects the principle while it is in itself possible of existence, and possibility does not come from the first principle. Rather, we do not deny that from the one proceeds one, and that from the effect—not from the principle but from itself—necessary, additional or non-additional, matters follow, thus multiplicity arises through it, and it thereby becomes the principle for the existence of multiplicity. So in this way the composite can meet the simple, for meeting is necessary and only possible thus; this is what must be judged. This is the statement in explaining their school. We said: what you have mentioned are arbitrary judgments, and in reality they are layers of darkness upon darkness. If a man related them from a dream he saw, it would indicate bad temperament; or if he brought their kind in jurisprudential matters, where the utmost is conjectures, it would be said they are nonsense, not even producing conjectural likelihoods.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage first presents the philosophers' doctrine of triple aspects in the first intellect (the 'first effect' of the first principle). According to them, the first intellect has three aspects: (1) it intellects its principle (God), (2) it intellects itself, and (3) it is possible in itself. From these aspects, three things proceed: the intellect (the second intellect) from the first aspect, the soul of the sphere from the second, and the body of the sphere from the third. Ghazali then transitions to his critique: after stating that this is the explanation of their school, he responds sharply. He calls their arguments 'arbitrary judgments' and 'layers of darkness,' comparing them to a madman's dream and saying they wouldn't even be accepted in jurisprudence, which relies on conjectures. This sets the stage for his detailed objections.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the philosophers believed multiplicity comes directly from God.
Correction: The passage shows that multiplicity arises from the first intellect's own aspects, not directly from God. Only one thing proceeds from God.

Checkpoint
What three aspects does the philosophers' first effect have?
A) Existence, necessity, and possibility
B) Intellecting its principle, intellecting itself, and being possible of existence
C) Intellect, soul, and body
D) Good, evil, and neutral

Key Takeaway
The philosophers explain multiplicity by attributing different effects to different aspects of the first intellect, all while maintaining that only one thing proceeds directly from God.

?
Ghazali carefully presents this theory before refuting it. Are you quick to accept sophisticated arguments that sound logical, or do you critically examine whether they align with divine truth? What steps can you take to ensure your beliefs are grounded in revelation rather than speculation?

Ghazali's Dismissal of the Triple-Aspect Theory
Ghazali dismisses the philosophers' triple-aspect theory as arbitrary and irrational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali dismisses the philosophers' triple-aspect theory as arbitrary and nonsensical.

Core Idea
Ghazali calls the theory 'layers of darkness upon darkness' and likens it to a madman's dream.

After presenting the philosophers' emanation scheme, Ghazali turns to critique. His dismissal is swift and devastating: he does not even argue against the details but declares the entire construction arbitrary. His use of strong language indicates his view that such theories are not merely wrong but intellectually absurd.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's harsh dismissal
Read the short passage where Ghazali responds. Notice the tone and the imagery he uses.
He uses the words 'arbitrary judgments' and 'layers of darkness'.
2
Understand the nature of his critique
Ghazali does not engage with the logical details. Instead, he attacks the theory as entirely unfounded, comparing it to a dream from a sick mind.
This is a rhetorical strategy to delegitimize the entire approach.
3
Consider the implications
By saying it wouldn't even pass in jurisprudence (which relies on conjecture), Ghazali is undermining the philosophical claim to certainty.
