Refuting the Claim That the First Effect Must Know Its Cause
Ghazali refutes the claim that the first effect must know its cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that knowledge of the cause is not a necessary attribute of the effect, and how he refutes the philosophers' claim using their own emanation principle.

Core Idea
An effect does not inherently know its cause; such knowledge would require an additional cause.

Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers' emanation model. In this passage, he addresses the claim that the first intellect, upon knowing itself, must know the First Principle. He dismantles this by showing it violates the principle 'from the One only one proceeds' and that knowledge is not a necessary concomitant of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objector's claim: the first effect, once it exists and knows itself, must necessarily know its cause (the First Principle).
This is the claim Ghazali will refute.
2
Identify Ghazali's disjunction
Ghazali responds by asking: does this knowledge follow from a cause or without a cause? Trace both branches and see where each leads.
This is the key logical move.
3
Follow the consequences of each branch
If from a cause, that cause is the First Principle, but from the One only one can emanate—already the essence, so knowledge cannot be a second. If without a cause, then why not allow many things to emanate without cause? Both contradict their system.
Ghazali forces a dilemma.
4
Understand the effect's status
Ghazali then examines whether the effect is necessary or possible, and shows that either way leads to contradiction or impossibility.
5
Reflect on the analogy
Ghazali compares with the cause's knowledge of the effect: it is not necessary for the cause's existence, so similarly the effect's knowledge of the cause is not necessary. This seals the refutation.
The philosophers' move is groundless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لما وجد وعقل ذاته لزمه أن يعقل المبدأ الأول . قلنا : لزمه ذلك بعلة أو بغير علة ؟ فإن كان بعلة فلا علة إلا المبدأ الأول وهو واحد فلا يتصور أن يصدر منه إلا واحد وقد صدر وهو ذات المعلول فالثاني كيف يصدر منه ؟ وإن لزم بغير علة فليلزم من وجود الأول موجودات كثيرة بلا علة ولا يلزم منها الكثرة فإن لم يعقل هذا من حيث إن واجب الوجود لا يكون إلا واحدا والزائد على الواحد ممكن والممكن يفتقر إلى علة . فهذا اللازم في حق المعلول وإن كان واجب الوجود بذاته فقد بطل قولهم واجب الوجود واحد وإن كان ممكنا فلابد له من علة ولا علة له فلا يعقل وجوده . وليس هو من ضرورة المعلول الأول لكونه ممكن الوجود فإن إمكان الوجود ضروري في كل معلول أما كون المعلول عالما بالعلة ليس ضروريا في وجود ذاته فكما أن كون العلة عالمة بالمعلول ليس ضروريا في وجود ذاته بل لزوم العلم بالمعلول أظهر من لزوم العلم بالعلة فبان أن الكثرة الحاصلة من علمه بالمبدأ محال فإنه لا مبدأ له وليس هو من ضرورة وجود ذات المعلول وهذا أيضا لا مخرج منه .
If it is said: When it (the first effect) came to be and intellected its essence, it necessarily follows that it intellects the First Principle. We say: Does that follow from a cause or without a cause? If from a cause, then there is no cause except the First Principle, and it is one, so it cannot be imagined that from it emanates more than one, and it has already emanated, which is the essence of the effect, so how can a second emanate from it? And if it follows without a cause, then let there follow from the existence of the First many existents without a cause, and multiplicity would not follow from it! For if this is not understood due to the fact that the Necessary Existent is only one, and what is additional to the one is possible, and the possible needs a cause. So this necessary consequence regarding the effect: if it is necessary of existence by itself, then their statement 'the Necessary Existent is one' is invalidated; and if it is possible, then it must have a cause, but it has no cause, so its existence is inconceivable. And it is not a necessary concomitant of the first effect by virtue of its being possible existence, for possibility of existence is necessary in every effect. As for the effect's being knowing of the cause, it is not necessary in the existence of its essence, just as the cause's being knowing of the effect is not necessary in the existence of its essence; rather, the necessity of knowledge of the effect is more apparent than the necessity of knowledge of the cause. Thus it became clear that the multiplicity resulting from its knowledge of the principle is impossible, for it has no principle (cause from which it acquires that knowledge) and it is not from the necessity of the existence of the essence of the effect. And this also has no way out.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a further objection-and-answer. The objector argues that the first effect (the first intellect), once it exists and knows itself, must necessarily know its cause (the First Principle). Ghazali's response dissects this claim: does this necessity arise from a cause or without a cause?

If from a cause, that cause can only be the First Principle, which is one. But from the One only one thing can emanate, and that one thing is the essence of the effect itself. So how can a second thing (the knowledge of the cause) emanate from the same cause? This would imply multiplicity from the One, which is impossible in their system.

If without a cause, then there is no reason why many other things should not emanate from the First without a cause, leading to an unbounded multiplicity. This also contradicts their principle that from the One only one follows.

Ghazali then drives home the logical consequences: if the effect is considered necessary of existence by itself, then the claim that the Necessary Existent is only one is false (since now we have two necessary existents). If it is possible, then it needs a cause—but it has no cause for this knowledge (since the only cause is the First, which already has given all it can). So its existence is impossible.

Crucially, Ghazali clarifies that knowledge of the cause is not a necessary concomitant of the effect's existence. Possibility of existence is necessary for every effect, but knowledge of the cause is not. He draws an analogy: the cause's knowledge of the effect is not necessary for the cause's existence, so similarly the effect's knowledge of the cause is not necessary for its existence. In fact, the necessity for a cause to know its effect is more apparent than the necessity for an effect to know its cause. Therefore, the attempt to derive multiplicity from the effect's supposed knowledge of the First is groundless. The objector's position has no escape.

العلة
المبدأ الأول
واجب الوجود
الممكن
الكثرة
العلم بالعلة
العلم بالمعلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An effect necessarily knows its cause simply because it exists and is connected to it.
Correction: Knowledge is not a necessary concomitant of existence; it requires an additional cause, which, in the philosophers' system, cannot be provided without violating the principle that from the One only one proceeds.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's main argument against the claim that the first effect must know its cause?
A) The effect cannot know anything because it is only a material intellect.
B) If the knowledge followed without a cause, then many other things would also follow without a cause from the First.
C) The effect already knows its cause by necessity of its essence.
D) The First does not want the effect to know Him.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of the cause is not a necessary concomitant of an effect's existence; attempting to derive it violates the principle of emanation and leads to contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you assumed that knowing the source of something means you automatically know it fully? How might this assumption have led you to neglect seeking deeper understanding from God? Will you now acknowledge that knowledge is a gift He gives, not something inherent in what you study?
?
This argument shows that derived knowledge requires a separate cause. Consider your own faith: Do you treat your knowledge of God as something that came from yourself or as a gift that He caused? How does that realization change your humility before Him?
?
The philosophers thought the first effect's self-knowledge necessarily included knowledge of its cause. Have you ever mistaken your own self-awareness for complete understanding of your Creator? What would it mean for you to admit that your knowledge of God is not automatic but must be sought through His guidance?

The Fourth Objection: The First Heaven's Composition
Ghazali objects that the first heaven's composition requires multiple causes beyond the simple First.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's objection that the first heaven's composition (form/matter, specific magnitude) shows it cannot proceed from a simple First Principle, and grasp his refutation of the philosophers' order-based defense.

Core Idea
Composition demands multiple causes, not a single simple emitter.

In this passage, Ghazali launches his fourth objection against the philosophers' emanation scheme. He argues that the first heaven, being composite in three ways, cannot be the direct effect of the simple First Principle. This forces the philosophers to admit additional causes beyond the First, undermining their claim of a single necessary effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let Ghazali's argument unfold.
2
Identify the three aspects of composition
Ghazali enumerates three ways the first heaven is composite: form-and-matter, specific magnitude, and the need for a cause of composition. Notice how each aspect requires a separate causal principle.
Focus on his reasoning for the second aspect: the specific size is contingent and needs a particularizer.
3
Examine the dialectical exchange
Ghazali anticipates an objection: the size is determined by the cosmic order. He then turns this into a dilemma: either order suffices (eliminating all causes) or order needs a cause, which still fails to explain particular size.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this objection means for the philosophers' emanation scheme: if the first effect is composite, the simple First cannot be its sole cause. This strengthens Ghazali's critique of necessary emanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الرابع : أن نقول التثليث لا يكفي في المعلول الأول ، فإن جرم السماء الأولى ، لزم عندهم في معنى واحد ، من ذات المبدأ ، وفيه تركيب من ثلاثة أوجه : أحدها : أنه مركب من صورة وهيولى ، وهكذا كل جسم عندهم ، فلابد لكل واحدة منهما من مبدأ ، إذ الصورة تخالف الهيولى ، وليست كل واحدة على مذهبهم علة مستقلة للأخرى ، حتى يكون أحدهما بوساطة الآخر ، من غير علة أخرى زائدة عليها . الثاني : أن الجرم الأقصى على حد مخصوص في الكبر ، واختصاصه بذلك القدر من بين سائر المقادير ، زائد على وجود ذاته ، إذ كان ذاته ممكناً أصغر مما هو عليه ، وأكبر ، فلابد له من مخصص بذلك المقدار ، زائد على المعنى البسيط الموجب لوجوده لا كوجود العقل ، لأن العقل وجود محض ، لا يختص بمقدار ، مقابل لسائر المقادير ، فيجوز أن يقال : العقل لا يحتاج إلا إلى علة بسيطة = فإن قيل : سببه أنه لو كان أكبر منه لكان مستغنى عنه في تحصيل النظام الكلى ، ولو كان أصغر منه لم يصلح للنظام المقصود فنقول : وتعين جهة النظام هل هو كاف فى وجود ما فيه النظام ، أم يفتقر موجودة ؟ ! فإن كان كافياً فقد استغنيتم عن وضع العلل ، فاحكموا بأن كون النظام في هذه الموجودات ، اقتضى هذه الموجودات بلا علة زائدة ، وإن كان ذلك لا يكفى ، بل افتقر إلى علة ، فذلك أيضاً لا يكفى للاختصاص بالمقادير ، بل يحتاج أيضاً إلى علة التركيب
The fourth objection: We say that the trinity is not sufficient for the first effect. For the body of the first heaven, according to them, follows from the essence of the Principle in one meaning, but it has composition in three aspects: First: It is composed of form and matter, and thus every body according to them, so each of them needs a principle, for form is different from matter, and according to their doctrine, each is not an independent cause for the other, unless one mediates the other, without an additional cause. Second: The outermost body has a specific limit in magnitude, and its particularization to that quantity among all quantities is additional to the existence of its essence, for its essence can be smaller or larger, so it needs a particularizer (mukhassis) for that quantity, beyond the simple meaning that necessitates its existence—unlike the existence of the intellect, because the intellect is pure existence, not specified by a quantity, opposite to all quantities. So it is permissible to say: The intellect needs only a simple cause. Then if it is said: The reason is that if it were larger, it would be unnecessary for achieving the universal order, and if it were smaller, it would not be suitable for the intended order. We say: Does the determination of the aspect of order suffice for the existence of that which contains the order, or does it need something existent? If it suffices, then you have dispensed with positing causes, so rule that the order in these existences necessitates these existences without an additional cause. And if that does not suffice, but needs a cause, then also that is not sufficient for the particularization of quantities, but also needs a cause for the composition.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a fourth objection (i'tirad rabi') against the philosophers' emanation scheme. Ghazali argues that the first heaven (the outermost celestial sphere) cannot proceed from the simple First Principle alone because it has three aspects of composition. First, it is composed of form and matter, each needing a principle; according to the philosophers, form and matter are not mutual causes. Second, the heaven has a specific size (e.g., its magnitude), which is not necessary to its essence since it could be larger or smaller, so it requires a particularizer (mukhassis) beyond the simple cause that gives existence. The intellect, by contrast, is simple and has no magnitude, needing only a simple cause. Then Ghazali anticipates an objection: the specific size is determined by what is needed for the cosmic order. He responds by asking: Does the consideration of order itself suffice to bring the ordered thing into existence, or does it need an existent cause? If order suffices, then no causes are needed at all, which is absurd. If order requires a cause, that cause still does not explain the particular size and composition; an additional cause for composition is still required.

Key Terms
الصورة والهيولى
المخصص
النظام
علة التركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali is denying that God can create composite things.
Correction: Ghazali is not denying creation of composites; he is arguing that the philosophers' emanation scheme, where a simple First directly produces a composite effect, is incoherent because composition requires multiple causes.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's fourth objection, why does the first heaven require a particularizer (mukhassis) for its size?
A) Because its size is necessary for its existence.
B) Because its essence could be larger or smaller, so its specific size is contingent and needs a cause.
C) Because the First Principle cannot cause anything with magnitude.
D) Because size is not relevant to causation.

Key Takeaway
The first heaven's composition (form/matter and specific magnitude) shows it cannot be the direct product of a simple First Principle; multiple causes are required, undermining the philosophers' emanation scheme.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali exposes that what seems like a simple, direct emanation actually hides a composite structure requiring multiple causes. In your own life, what 'simple outcome' (like your faith or a good habit) have you been assuming comes easily, without acknowledging the many components (effort, repentance, divine help) that Allah has ordained for it? Will you trace each component's cause back to Him today?
?
The philosophers thought 'order' alone could determine magnitude, but Ghazali shows that order itself needs a cause. Consider an area of your life where you rely on 'order' (a routine, a plan) to produce spiritual growth. Do you act as if the mere presence of order suffices, or do you recognize that you need the additional cause—your sincere intention and Allah's acceptance? Which will you actively seek today?
?
The first heaven's specific magnitude could have been different, yet it isn't. What specific 'magnitude' in your life—the number of your prayers, the extent of your charity, the severity of your trials—might you mistakenly view as random or self-determined? How does realizing that Allah is the particularizer of every detail change your gratitude or your plea to Him?

The First Aspect: Composition of Form and Matter
Ghazali argues the celestial sphere cannot derive from one simple intellect due to composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the celestial sphere's composition of form and matter refutes the philosophers' emanation scheme.

Core Idea
Duality in the effect demands duality in the cause.

Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers' emanation theory with a fourth objection. He focuses on the first effect—the first intellect—and argues that it cannot be the source of the celestial sphere because the sphere exhibits composition. The first aspect of this composition is the combination of form and matter, which, according to the philosophers' own principles, requires multiple causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Ghazali introduces the fourth objection and the first aspect of composition.
2
Identify the key elements
Find the terms 'form' (صورة) and 'matter' (هيولى). Recognize that Ghazali says each needs its own principle (مبدأ).
3
Understand the logical move
Why is form different from matter? And why can't one be the cause of the other? This forces the need for an additional cause, which contradicts the philosophers' claim of a single simple emanation.
4
Reflect on the broader argument
Consider how this objection fits into Ghazali's overall attack on the philosophers' theory of emanation. It challenges the notion that a simple first cause can produce a composite effect.

الاعتراض الرابع : أن نقول التثليث لا يكفي في المعلول الأول ، فإن جرم السماء الأولى ، لزم عندهم في معنى واحد ، من ذات المبدأ ، وفيه تركيب من ثلاثة أوجه : أحدها : أنه مركب من صورة وهيولى ، وهكذا كل جسم عندهم ، فلابد لكل واحدة منهما من مبدأ ، إذ الصورة تخالف الهيولى ، وليست كل واحدة على مذهبهم علة مستقلة للأخرى ، حتى يكون أحدهما بوساطة الآخر ، من غير علة أخرى زائدة عليها
The fourth objection: that we say the trinity is not sufficient for the first effect, for the body of the first heaven, according to them, necessarily proceeds from a single meaning from the essence of the First Principle, yet it contains composition from three aspects. The first: that it is composed of form and matter, and thus every body according to them, so each of them must have a principle, since form is different from matter, and neither, according to their doctrine, is an independent cause of the other, such that one could be through the mediation of the other without another additional cause.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage begins Ghazali's fourth objection against the philosophers' emanation scheme. He states that the first effect (the first intellect) cannot be the source of the celestial sphere because the sphere has composition. He identifies the first aspect: the sphere is composed of form and matter. Since form and matter are distinct, each requires its own cause, and according to the philosophers themselves, neither is the independent cause of the other. Thus a single simple cause (the first intellect) cannot produce both, for it would need an additional cause to account for the duality.

Correction: Actually, Ghazali's objection is that the philosophers' own principles require distinct causes for distinct realities, so a simple cause cannot produce both form and matter without additional principles.

The Specific Size and the Need for a Particularizer
Ghazali argues the sphere's definite size needs a particularizer beyond a simple cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the sphere's specific size requires a particularizer beyond a simple cause, and how he counters the philosophers' appeal to order.

Core Idea
A specific size cannot be explained by a simple cause; it needs a particularizer.

Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers' account of the celestial sphere. They claim a simple intellect suffices to explain the sphere's existence. But Ghazali points out that the sphere has a specific magnitude, which is not entailed by its essence. This demands an additional particularizer. He then anticipates and refutes the objection that the size is determined by the needs of the universal order.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage along with the translation to grasp Ghazali's argument.
Notice the structure: claim, objection, response.
2
Identify the main claim
Ghazali first states that the sphere's specific size is additional to its essence, so it needs a particularizer.
3
Understand the objection
The philosophers might say the size is determined by the order. Find this objection in the passage.
Look for 'فإن قيل'.
4
Follow Ghazali's response
He asks whether the order itself suffices for existence or not, and shows that either way the philosophers' position is problematic.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument undermines the philosophers' reliance on a single simple cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانى : أن الجرم الأقصى على حد مخصوص في الكبر ، واختصاصه بذلك القدر من بين سائر المقادير ، زائد على وجود ذاته ، إذ كان ذاته ممكناً أصغر مما هو عليه ، وأكبر ، فلابد له من مخصص بذلك المقدار ، زائد على المعنى البسيط الموجب لوجوده لا كوجود العقل ، لأن العقل وجود محض ، لا يختص بمقدار ، مقابل لسائر المقادير ، فيجوز أن يقال : العقل لا يحتاج إلا إلى علة بسيطة . فإن قيل : سببه أنه لو كان أكبر منه لكان مستغنى عنه في تحصيل النظام الكلى ، ولو كان أصغر منه لم يصلح للنظام المقصود فنقول : وتعين جهة النظام هل هو كاف فى وجود ما فيه النظام ، أم يفتقر موجودة ؟ ! فإن كان كافياً فقد استغنيتم عن وضع العلل ، فاحكموا بأن كون النظام في هذه الموجودات ، اقتضى هذه الموجودات بلا علة زائدة ، وإن كان ذلك لا يكفى ، بل افتقر إلى علة ، فذلك أيضاً لا يكفى للاختصاص بالمقادير ، بل يحتاج أيضاً إلى علة التركيب
The second: that the outermost body has a specific limit in magnitude, and its being particularized by that amount among all magnitudes is additional to the existence of its essence, since its essence is possible as smaller than it is, and larger. So it must have a particularizer for that amount, additional to the simple meaning that necessitates its existence, not like the existence of the intellect, for the intellect is pure existence, not particularized by magnitude, relative to other magnitudes, so it may be said: the intellect only needs a simple cause. If it is said: its cause is that if it were larger, it would be dispensed with in achieving the universal order, and if it were smaller, it would not be suitable for the intended order, we say: the determination of the aspect of order—is it sufficient for the existence of that which contains the order, or does it need an existent? If it were sufficient, then you have dispensed with positing causes, so judge that the existence of order in these existents necessitates these existents without an additional cause. And if that is not sufficient, but needs a cause, then that also is not sufficient for particularization in magnitudes, but also needs a cause of composition.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents the second aspect of composition: the celestial sphere has a specific magnitude, which is not necessitated by its essence alone (since it could be larger or smaller). Therefore, it needs a particularizer (مخصص) that determines this specific size, beyond the simple cause that gives it existence. Ghazali anticipates a philosopher's objection: if the sphere were a different size, the universal order (system) would be disrupted. Ghazali responds by asking whether the requirement of order is sufficient to bring the existent itself into being or merely to specify its size. If it is sufficient, then they have abandoned the need for distinct causes; if it is not sufficient, then even with order, they still need a cause for the specific magnitude and also for the composition (تركيب). Thus the philosophers' appeal to order does not remove the need for additional causes.

Key Terms
حد مخصوص (specific limit)
مخصص (particularizer)
نظام (order)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the philosophers' appeal to final causality (the order) fully explains the sphere's size without needing an efficient cause.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the order only specifies the size but does not bring the sphere into existence. Therefore, an efficient cause is still required for existence and for the particular size.

Key Takeaway
The specific size of the celestial sphere requires a particularizer, and the appeal to order does not obviate the need for an additional cause.

Third Aspect: Poles and Equatorial Parts
Ghazali argues fixed poles and moving equator require a cause beyond the simple.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fixed poles and moving equator of the outermost sphere present a logical problem for the philosophers' emanation theory.

Core Idea
The simple cannot produce difference: poles and equator force a choice.

Ghazali continues his attack on the philosophers' emanation scheme by pointing to an observable feature of the celestial sphere: it has fixed poles and a moving equator. This differentiation, he argues, cannot arise from a single simple cause. The passage presents a dilemma that challenges the very foundation of their cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: a question followed by a dilemma.
The passage contains a key question mark.
2
Identify the dilemma
Ghazali presents two possibilities: either all parts are similar, or they are different. Write them down.
3
Understand the conclusion
He then states that the outermost sphere can only come from a simple cause, which cannot produce differentiation. See why this is a problem.
This connects to the larger aim of the Incoherence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أن الفلك الأقصى انقسم إلى نقطتين ، هما القطبان ، وهما ثابتا ، لا يفارقان وضعهما ، وأجزاء المنطقة يختلف وضعها ، فلا يخلو : إما أن تكون جميع أجزاء الفلك الأقصى متشابهة ، فلم لزم تعين نقطتين من بين سائر النقط لكونهما قطبين ؟ ! أو أجزاؤه مختلفة ، ففي بعضها خواص ليست في البعض ، فما مبدأ تلك الاختلافات ؟ ! والجرم الأقصى لم يصدر إلا من معنى واحد بسيط ، والبسيط لا يوجب إلا بسيطاً فى الشكل ، وهو الكرى ، ومتشابها في المعنى وهو الخلو عن الخواص المميزة ، وهذا أيضاً لا مخرج منه
The third: that the outermost sphere is divided into two points, which are the poles, and they are fixed, never changing their position, while the parts of the equator differ in position. It is inevitable: either all parts of the outermost sphere are similar—so why are two points necessarily determined among all points to be poles? Or its parts are different—so some have properties not in others, so what is the principle of those differences? Yet the outermost body only proceeds from one simple meaning, and the simple only necessitates something simple in shape, which is spherical, and similar in meaning, which is being devoid of distinguishing properties. And this also has no escape.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents the third aspect of composition: the celestial sphere has fixed poles and a moving equator. This differentiation poses a problem. Ghazali offers a dilemma: either all parts of the sphere are similar, in which case there is no reason why only two points are poles; or the parts are different, meaning some have special properties not found in others, which requires a cause for those differences. However, the sphere emanates from a single simple principle, which can only produce something simple in shape (spherical) and uniform in nature (free of distinguishing properties). Therefore, the observed differentiation cannot arise from the simple First Intellect alone. Ghazali concludes that there is no way out of this argument for the philosophers.

خواص مميزة (distinguishing properties)

Correction: Ghazali is making a metaphysical point: any differentiation in the spheres requires a cause of that differentiation, which cannot be the simple First Intellect.

Refuting the Objection of Latent Multiplicity
Ghazali refutes the claim that latent multiplicity in the first intellect allows many effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that allowing unknown multiplicities in the first effect leads to the logical conclusion that the First Cause is unnecessary.

Core Idea
Latent multiplicity in an effect makes the First Cause dispensable.

This passage presents an objection from the philosophers: perhaps the first principle contains hidden types of multiplicity, which would allow many effects to proceed from it. Ghazali's response is incisive: if you permit that, you can derive all existents from the first effect alone, making the First Cause redundant. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The philosophers propose that the first principle might have types of necessary multiplicity not from its essence, explaining how many effects arise. Note the claim: multiplicity proceeds from multiplicity.
The objection tries to save the principle that from one only one proceeds by positing hidden multiplicity.
2
Identify Ghazali's reductio
Ghazali shows that if you allow such latent multiplicity, then you could derive all existents (thousands) from the first effect alone, not just the outer sphere. This leads to independence from the second cause.
The move is to extend the logic to all effects, not just a few.
3
Follow the consequence to the First Cause
If the first effect can have necessary multiplicities without a cause, then the First Cause could also be posited without a cause, breaking the causal chain entirely.
Ghazali underscores that there is no temporal or spatial separation between the two, so the same reasoning applies.
4
Reflect on the argument's force
The objection backfires: the philosophers' suggestion undermines their own emanation scheme by making the First Cause dispensable.
This demonstrates the strength of Ghazali's critique of necessary causal connections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لعل فى المبدأ أنواعاً من الكثرة لازمة ، لا من جهة المبدأ ، وإنما ظهر لنا ثلاثة أو أربعة والباقى لم نطلع عليه ، وعدم عثورنا على عينه ، لا يشككنا فى أن مبدأ الكثرة كثرة ، وأن الواحد لا يصدر عنه كثير . قلنا : فإذا جوّزتم هذا ، فقولوا : إن الموجودات كلها على كثرتها - وقد بلغت آلافاً - صدرت من المعلول الأول ، فلا يحتاج أن يقتصر على جرم الفلك الأقصى ونفسه ، بل يجوز أن يكون قد صدرت منه جميع النفوس الفلكية ، والإنسانية ، وجميع الأجسام الأرضية ، والسماوية . بأنواع كثيرة لازمة فيها لم تطلعوا عليها ؛ فيقع الاستغناء بالمعلول الأول ، ثم يلزم منه الاستغناء بالعلة الأولى ، فإنه إذا جاء تولد كثرة ، يقال إنها : لازمة لا بعلة ، مع أنها ليست ضرورية فى وجود المعلول الأول ، جاز أن يقدر العلة الأولى ، ويكون وجودها لا بعلة ، ويقال : إنها لزمت ولا يدرى ذلك مع عددها ، وكلما تخيل وجودها بلا علة مع الأول ، تخيل ذلك بلا علة مع الثاني ، بل لا معنى لقولنا مع الأول والثانى ، إذ ليس بينهما مفارقة في زمان ولا في مكان ، فما لا يفارقهما فى زمان ولا مكان ، ويجوز أن يكون موجوداً بلا علة ، لم يختص أحدهما بالإضافة إليه.
If it is said: perhaps in the [first] principle there are types of necessary multiplicity, not from the principle itself, but only three or four have appeared to us, and the rest we have not discovered, and our not stumbling upon them does not make us doubt that multiplicity proceeds from multiplicity, and that the one does not produce many. We say: if you allow this, then say: all existents in their multiplicity—and they have reached thousands—proceed from the first effect, so it is not necessary to restrict it to the body of the outermost sphere and its soul, but rather it is possible that all the celestial souls, human souls, and all earthly and heavenly bodies proceed from it, with many necessary types in them that you have not discovered; thus independence from the first effect is attained, and then from that independence from the First Cause follows. For when a multiplicity is generated, and it is said that it is necessary without a cause, while it is not necessary for the existence of the first effect, then it becomes permissible to posit the First Cause, and its existence would be without a cause, and it would be said that it became necessary and one does not know that along with its number. And whenever one imagines its existence without a cause with the first, one imagines that without a cause with the second. Rather there is no meaning to our saying 'with the first and the second,' since there is no separation between them in time or place. So what does not separate from them in time or place and may exist without a cause is not particularized in relation to one of them.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage contains an objection from the philosophers and Ghazali's refutation. The objector suggests that the first principle (the First Intellect) might contain latent types of multiplicity (e.g., three or four known kinds) that are necessary but not from its essence, and that we may not know all of them. This would allow many effects to arise from a single principle. Ghazali responds that if they allow such unknown multiplicities, then they could derive all existents (thousands) from the first effect, not just the celestial sphere and its soul. This would make the first effect self-sufficient, and consequently the First Cause would be dispensable. Worse, if they claim that these multiplicities are necessary without a cause (just arising from the first effect), then by the same logic the First Cause could exist without a cause, breaking the causal chain. Since there is no temporal or spatial separation between the First Cause and the first effect, any attribute that could exist without a cause for one could be claimed for the other, undermining the whole emanation scheme.

Key Terms
كثرة لازمة (necessary multiplicity)
معلول أول (first effect)
علة أولى (First Cause)
استغناء (independence)
لا بعلة (without a cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is that latent multiplicity is possible, so the first cause can produce many effects.
Correction: Ghazali shows that accepting this leads to the logical consequence that the First Cause is unnecessary, thus the objection backfires.

Checkpoint
What consequence does Ghazali draw from allowing latent multiplicity in the first effect?
A) The first effect becomes simple again
B) All existents could proceed from the first effect
C) The First Cause becomes unnecessary
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's refutation shows that the objection of latent multiplicity, if accepted, leads to the absurd conclusion that the First Cause is unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed the good in your life to secondary causes (e.g., your effort, luck, people) in a way that made you feel independent of Allah? How does Ghazali's argument challenge you to see that nothing is truly independent of the First Cause?
?
If you truly believed that Allah alone is the Cause of everything—even the means—what would change in how you respond to success and failure? Are you willing to examine one area of your life where you have acted as if a secondary cause is self-sufficient?
?
The philosophers' objection allowed them to posit unknown multiplicities to preserve their system. In your own heart, what 'unknown multiplicities'—excuses or justifications—do you use to distance yourself from full dependence on Allah? Will you abandon them today?

The Problem of Differentiation in the Outermost Sphere
Ghazali poses a dilemma about the poles and zone of the outermost sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the outermost sphere's differentiation cannot be explained by a simple cause.

Core Idea
A simple cause cannot produce a differentiated effect.

Ghazali presents a powerful dilemma: if the outermost sphere's parts are all similar, why are two points poles? If they are different, what causes that difference? Since the sphere's cause is absolutely simple, it can only produce something uniform. This challenges the philosophers' emanation scheme.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Observe the structure of the dilemma.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Understand the dilemma's two horns
Identify the two possibilities: all parts similar vs. parts different. Notice the questions Ghazali poses for each.
The first horn leads to 'why these poles?', the second to 'what is the principle of differences?'
3
Analyze the implication about the cause
Ghazali then states that the sphere's cause is simple, and a simple cause produces only something simple in form and uniform in meaning.
This rules out both horns, leaving no explanation for the observed differentiation.
4
Recognize the force of the argument
Reflect on how this argument undermines the philosophers' claim that the emanation from a simple First Cause can produce a complex cosmos.
Ghazali ends with 'this also has no way out,' indicating the dilemma is fatal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوضع الثالث : أن الفلك الأقصى انقسم إلى نقطتين ، هما القطبان ، وهما ثابتان لا يفارقان وضعهما ، وأجزاء المنطقة يختلف وضعها ، فلا يخلو : إما أن تكون جميع أجزاء الفلك الأقصى متشابهة ، فلم لزم تعين نقطتين من بين سائر النقط لكونهما قطبين ؟ ! أو أجزاؤه مختلفة ، ففي بعضها خواص ليست في البعض ، فما مبدأ تلك الاختلافات ؟ ! والجرم الأقصى لم يصدر إلا من معنى واحد بسيط ، والبسيط لا يوجب إلا بسيطاً فى الشكل ، وهو الكرى ، ومتشابهاً في المعنى وهو الخلو عن الخواص المميزة ، وهذا أيضاً لا مخرج منه .
The third position: that the outermost sphere is divided into two points, which are the two poles, and they are fixed, not leaving their position, while the parts of the zone differ in position. So it is inevitable: either all parts of the outermost sphere are similar, so why is it necessary that two points are determined from among the other points to be poles? Or its parts are different, so in some there are properties not in others, so what is the principle of those differences? And the outermost body only issued from a single simple meaning, and the simple necessitates only something simple in form, which is spherical, and uniform in meaning, which is being devoid of distinguishing properties. And this also has no way out.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's third argument against the philosophers' emanation scheme. He points out that the outermost sphere (the ninth sphere) is divided into two fixed poles and a zone whose parts change position. He then poses a dilemma: either all parts of the sphere are similar, in which case why are only two points poles? Or they are different, requiring a principle of differentiation. But the sphere itself issued from a simple cause, and a simple cause can only produce something simple in form (spherical) and uniform in meaning (without distinguishing properties). Therefore, differentiation in the sphere cannot be explained by its simple cause, and the philosophers have no way out.

The Absurdity of Hidden Multiplicities Without Cause
Ghazali refutes the claim that hidden multiplicities can exist in the first principle without a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why allowing hidden multiplicities in the first effect without a cause leads to the absurd conclusion that the First Cause itself could exist without a cause.

Core Idea
Hidden multiplicities without cause undermine the necessity of the First Cause.

In this passage, a hypothetical objector proposes that the first principle may contain latent multiplicities that are necessary but not caused by the principle itself. Al-Ghazali responds with a powerful reductio ad absurdum: if such uncaused multiplicities are allowed in the first effect, then they could be extended to the First Cause itself, destroying the very foundation of the philosophers' emanationist model.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: perhaps the first principle contains hidden multiplicities that are necessary but not from its essence. The objector claims we only see a few such multiplicities.
Notice that the objector does not deny that the one produces many; they just want to keep the first principle simple.
2
Follow Ghazali's reductio
Ghazali says: if you allow this, then extend the logic to the first effect. Claim all existents came from it, and then you can dispense with the First Cause.
This is a slippery slope argument.
3
Notice the key point about separation
There is no temporal or spatial separation between the first effect and the First Cause. So if uncaused multiplicities are allowed for the first effect, the same must be allowed for the First Cause.
This collapses the distinction between caused and uncaused.
4
Recognize the absurd conclusion
The conclusion is that the First Cause would exist without a cause, which is impossible both philosophically and theologically.
This refutes the objection by showing it leads to a contradiction.
5
Reflect on the principle of causality
Ghazali upholds that every multiplicity must have a cause, and the first principle must be absolutely simple. Otherwise, the entire causal chain becomes incoherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لعل في المبدأ أنواعاً من الكثرة لازمة ، لا من جهة المبدأ ، وإنما ظهر لنا ثلاثة أو أربعة والباقى لم نطلع عليه ، وعدم عثورنا على عينه ، لا يشككنا في أن مبدأ الكثرة كثرة ، وأن الواحد لا يصدر عنه كثير.
قلنا : فإذا جوّزتم هذا ، فقولوا : إن الموجودات كلها على كثرتها - وقد بلغت آلافاً - صدرت من المعلول الأول ، فلا يحتاج أن يقتصر على جرم الفلك الأقصى ونفسه ، بل يجوز أن يكون قد صدرت منه جميع النفوس الفلكية ، والإنسانية ، وجميع الأجسام الأرضية ، والسماوية . بأنواع كثيرة لازمة فيها لم تطلعوا عليها ؛ فيقع الاستغناء بالمعلول الأول ، ثم يلزم منه الاستغناء بالعلة الأولى ، فإنه إذا جاء تولد كثرة ، يقال إنها : لازمة لا بعلة ، مع أنها ليست ضرورية في وجود المعلول الأول ، جاز أن يقدر العلة الأولى ، ويكون وجودها لا بعلة ، ويقال : إنها لزمت ولا يدرى ذلك مع عددها ، وكلما تخيل وجودها بلا علة مع الأول ، تخيل ذلك بلا علة مع الثاني ، بل لا معنى لقولنا مع الأول والثاني ، إذ ليس بينهما مفارقة في زمان ولا في مكان ، فما لا يفارقهما في زمان ولا مكان ، ويجوز أن يكون موجوداً بلا علة ، لم يختص أحدهما بالإضافة إليه.
If it is said: Perhaps in the principle there are kinds of necessary multiplicity, not from the aspect of the principle, and only three or four have appeared to us, and the rest we have not come to know, and our not coming across it does not make us doubt that the source of multiplicity is multiplicity, and that the one does not produce many.
We say: So if you allow this, then say that all existents in their multiplicity - and they have reached thousands - issued from the first effect, so there is no need to confine it to the body of the outermost sphere and its soul, but rather it is possible that from it issued all the celestial souls, human souls, and all earthly and celestial bodies, with many kinds necessary in them that you have not come across; then there would be dispensing with the first effect, and then from that follows dispensing with the first cause. For if a multiplicity comes to be generated, which is said to be necessary without a cause, while it is not necessary in the existence of the first effect, then it is permissible that the first cause be supposed, and its existence be without a cause, and it is said that it came to be necessarily and one does not know that with its number. And whenever one imagines its existence without a cause with the first, one imagines that without a cause with the second. Rather, there is no meaning to our saying 'with the first and the second', since there is no separation between them in time or place. So what does not separate from them in time or place, and is possible to exist without a cause, is not specific to one of them in addition to the other.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and Ghazali's response. The objector suggests that the first principle (the First Intellect or First Cause) may contain latent necessary multiplicities not derived from the principle itself, and we only see a few. Ghazali responds that if such hidden multiplicities are allowed, then one could claim all existents issued directly from the first effect, dispensing with intermediaries. This would also lead to dispensing with the First Cause, because if a multiplicity can accompany the first effect without a cause, the same could be said of the First Cause. Since there is no temporal or spatial separation between the first and second effects, the argument applies equally to both, leading to the absurdity that the First Cause would exist without a cause.

الكثرة اللازمة
المعلول الأول
العلة الأولى
الاستغناء

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hidden multiplicities can exist without a cause in the first principle, preserving the principle's simplicity.
Correction: Ghazali shows this leads to the absurdity that the First Cause itself would need no cause, destroying the necessity of the First Cause.

What does Ghazali say would follow if we allow hidden multiplicities in the first effect?
A) The first effect would become a creator.
B) All existents could be from the first effect directly, and the First Cause could be dispensed with.
C) The outermost sphere would have more poles.
D) The philosophers would be correct about emanation.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that allowing uncaused multiplicities in the first effect logically leads to the First Cause itself being uncaused, which is impossible.

Will you examine each one until you find the root, or will you let hidden contradictions undermine your faith?
?
This passage shows that allowing one exception to the principle of causality destroys the entire edifice. In your own heart, is there a single belief or practice that you excuse from being accountable to God's oneness? If you permit that, where does the chain end? Will you today identify that one exception and uproot it?
?
You claim to love truth, but do you love it enough to follow an argument to its end—even if it convicts you? Refuting the Limits of Multiplicity from the First Effect
Ghazali refutes the probability argument and uses the second effect as a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that probability is not a rational limit in metaphysical causality, and how he uses the second effect to refute the philosophers' objection.

Core Idea
Once you allow any number of concomitants, no rational limit remains.

The philosophers argued that it is improbable for the first effect to produce thousands of things, so they introduced intermediate levels. Ghazali responds that improbability is not a rational argument; once you admit that the first effect can have two or three concomitants, there is no rational stopping point. He then uses the example of the second effect—the sphere of the fixed stars with over 1200 distinct stars—to show that such multiplicity is already present, so the same could apply to the first effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Ghazali's initial response
The objector says it's unlikely the first effect has so many things. Ghazali calls this a guess and asks for a rational proof for a limit.
Notice the shift from probability to impossibility.
2
Understand the 'no limit' argument
Ghazali argues that once you allow any number beyond one, you cannot rationally stop at a specific number like a thousand. The burden is on the opponent to show why four is allowed but five is not.
3
Examine the refutation by the second effect
Ghazali points to the second effect (the sphere of fixed stars) which already contains over 1200 stars with different forms, positions, natures, etc. This shows that such multiplicity is possible in a single effect.
Consider the variety: size, shape, position, color, influence, etc.
4
Connect the argument to the First Cause
If such multiplicity can be conceived in the second effect, it can be in the first effect, and then there would be no need for the First Cause—undermining the philosophers' own system.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لقد كثرت الأشياء حتى زادت على ألف، ويبعد أن تبلغ الكثرة في المعلول الأول إلى هذا الحد، فلذلك أكثرنا الوسائط. مختلفة
قلنا : قول القائل : (يبعد) هذا رجم أظن ، لا يحكم به في المعقولات إلا أن يقول : إنه يستحيل ، فنقول : لم يستحيل ؟ ! وما المرد والفيصل مهما جاوزنا الواحد ، واعتقدنا أنه يجوز أن يلزم المعلول الأول – لا من جهة العلة – لازم واثنان وثلاثة ؟ ! فما المحيل الأربعة وخمسة وهكذا إلى الألف ؟ ! وإلا فمن يتحكم بمقدار دون مقدار ؟ ! فليس بعد مجاوزة الواحد مرد ، وهذا أيضاً قاطع . ثم نقول : هذا أيضاً باطل بالمعلول الثانى، فإنه صدر منه فلك الكواكب، وفيه من الكواكب المعروفة المسماة، ألف ونيف ومائتا كوكب، وهي العظم، والشكل، والوضع، واللون، والتأثير، والنحوسة، والسعادة؛ فبعضها على صورة الحمل والثور والأسد، وبعضها على صورة الإنسان، ويختلف تأثيرها في محل واحد من العالم السفلى، من التبريد والتسخين، والسعادة والنحوسة، وتختلف مقاديرها في ذاتها. فلا يمكن أن يقال : الكل نوع واحد، هذا الاختلاف؛ ولو جاز هذا لجاز أن يقال : كل أجسام العالم نوع واحد الجسمية، فتكفيها علة واحدة، فإن كان اختلاف صفاتها وجواهرها، وطبائعها؛ دل على اختلافها، فكذا الكواكب مختلفة لا محالة، ويفتقر كل واحد إلى علة لصورته، وعلة لهيولاه، وعلة لاختصاصه بطبيعته المسخنة أو المبردة، أو المسعدة أو المنحسة، وعلة لاختصاصه بموضعه، ثم لاختصاص جملتها بأشكال البهائم المختلفة، وهذه الكثرة إن تصور أن تعقل في المعلول الثانى، تصور في المعلول الأول ووقع الاستغناء
If it is said: Things have become so many that they exceed a thousand, and it is unlikely that multiplicity in the first effect reaches this limit, therefore we increased the intermediate [levels] - different.
We say: The saying of the one who says 'it is unlikely' is a guess, in my opinion. It is not judged in intelligibles unless he says that it is impossible. So we say: Why is it impossible? And what is the return and the decisive criterion once we go beyond the one, and we believe that it is permissible that a concomitant, two, or three accompany the first effect - not from the aspect of the cause? So what prohibits four and five and so on up to a thousand? Otherwise, who decides on a quantity without a quantity? So there is no return after exceeding the one, and this is decisive. Then we say: This is also refuted by the second effect, for from it issued the sphere of the planets, and in it are from the known named planets, one thousand and something over two hundred planets, and they have greatness, shape, position, color, influence, auspiciousness and inauspiciousness; some are in the form of Aries, Taurus, and Leo, and some in human form, and their influence differs in one place of the lower world, in cooling and heating, auspiciousness and inauspiciousness, and their magnitudes differ in themselves. So it cannot be said that all are one type, given this difference. And if this were allowed, then it would be allowed to say that all bodies of the world are one type of corporeality, so one cause would suffice for them. For if the difference of their attributes, essences, and natures indicates their difference, so similarly the planets are undoubtedly different, and each one needs a cause for its form, a cause for its matter, a cause for its specification with its heating or cooling nature, or auspicious or inauspicious, a cause for its specification with its position, and then for the specification of their totality with the forms of various animals. And this multiplicity, if it can be conceived in the second effect, it can be conceived in the first effect, and there would be dispensing [with the First Cause].
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage contains another objection and Ghazali's extended reply. The objector argues that it is improbable for the first effect to produce thousands of things, so the philosophers introduced intermediate levels. Ghazali responds that improbability is not a rational argument; once you allow that the first effect can have more than one concomitant (two, three), there is no rational limit to stop at any number. He then provides a powerful refutation using the second effect (the sphere of the fixed stars). This sphere contains over 1200 known stars, each differing in size, shape, position, color, influence, auspiciousness, etc. They cannot all be one type; each requires its own cause for form, matter, nature, position, etc. If such vast multiplicity can be conceived in the second effect, it can equally be conceived in the first effect, and then there would be no need for the First Cause (the argument would lead to dispensing with it).

المعلول الأول
المعلول الثاني
فلك الكواكب
الوسائط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Probability can serve as a rational limit in metaphysical arguments.
Correction: Ghazali shows that probability is not a rational proof; only impossibility is. Once you allow any number, there is no rational limit.

Checkpoint

A) He says a thousand is not a large number.
B) He argues that if you allow any number, you cannot rationally stop at a specific limit, and he points to the second effect as already containing such multiplicity.
C) He says the philosophers are lying.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that probability is not a rational criterion in metaphysics; the multiplicity in the second effect shows that the first effect could analogously have vast multiplicity.

The Fifth Objection: Arbitrariness of the Philosophers' Scheme
Ghazali criticizes the philosophers' scheme linking the first effect's modality to the sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers the philosophers' emanation scheme arbitrary and how he uses an analogy to expose its lack of logical justification.

Core Idea
The philosophers' scheme is arbitrary—no logical necessity links the first effect's attributes to specific celestial existents.

Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers by attacking the heart of their emanation model. After conceding their positions for argument's sake, he argues that their specific claims about how the first effect gives rise to the sphere, its soul, and its intellect are completely unsupported. He then draws a powerful analogy to show the absurdity of their reasoning. This passage reveals Ghazali's method of exposing unsupported assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let Ghazali's sarcastic tone and argument come through.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the core objection is clear.
2
Identify the three specific claims
The philosophers claim that three attributes of the first effect each necessitate a specific celestial existent. Find all three and note how Ghazali lists them.
3
Understand the analogy
Ghazali draws an analogy with an absent human. See how the analogy parallels the philosophers' claims and why it reveals arbitrariness.
Ask yourself: does the analogy show that the philosophers' scheme lacks logical necessity?
4
Reflect on the objection's force
Consider why Ghazali calls their positions 'cold' and 'corrupt.' This objection strikes at the rational basis of emanation.
Connect to the larger theme of the Tahafut: the philosophers' reliance on unproven assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الخامس : هو أنا نقول : سلمنا لكم هذه الأوضاع الباردة ، والتحكمات الفاسدة ، ولكن كيف لا تستحيون من قولكم : إن كون المعلول الأول ممكن الوجود ، اقتضى وجود جرم الفلك الأقصى منه ، وعقله نفسه ، اقتضى وجود نفس الفلك منه ، وعقله الأول اقتضى وجود عقل الفلك منه ؛ وما الفصل بين قائل هذا ، وبين قائل - عرف وجود إنسان غائب ، وأنه ممكن الوجود ، وأنه يعقل نفسه ، وصانعه فقال - : يلزم من كونه ممكن الوجود وجود
The fifth objection: That is, we say: We concede to you these cold positions and corrupt arbitrary judgments, but how do you not feel ashamed of your statement that the fact that the first effect is possible in existence necessitated the existence of the body of the outermost sphere from it, and its intellection of itself necessitated the existence of the soul of the sphere from it, and its intellection of the First necessitated the existence of the intellect of the sphere from it? And what is the distinction between the one who says this and the one who, knowing the existence of an absent human, that he is possible in existence, and that he intellects himself and his Maker, says: From his being possible in existence follows the existence
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's fifth objection to the philosophers' emanation scheme. He sarcastically concedes their 'cold positions and corrupt arbitrary judgments' but then attacks the specific claim that the first effect's possibility necessitates the existence of the outermost sphere, its self-intellection necessitates the soul of that sphere, and its intellection of the First necessitates the intellect of that sphere. Ghazali argues that this is completely arbitrary. He draws an analogy: if someone knows an absent human exists, that this human is possible and intellects himself and his Maker, would it follow that from his possibility comes the existence of something else? The philosophers have provided no logical justification for connecting these attributes to specific existents. The passage is cut off, but the argument is clear: the philosophers' scheme lacks rational grounding.

عقل الفلك

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That there is a necessary logical connection between a thing's possibility, its intellection, and specific external existents.
Correction: Ghazali shows this is arbitrary; the philosophers provide no justification for linking these attributes to specific celestial entities.

B) The outermost sphere is made of fire.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali exposes the arbitrariness of the philosophers' emanation scheme by showing that no logical necessity ties the first effect's attributes to specific celestial entities.

?

?
The philosophers' scheme seemed logical but collapsed under scrutiny. In your life, are you building your actions or beliefs on foundations that are just as arbitrary? What concrete step will you take to ground your convictions in truth and sound reasoning?

The arbitrariness of limiting the number of effects
Ghazali refutes the claim that many effects cannot proceed from one cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument against the philosophers' claim that the First Cause can only produce one effect, and why he considers the limitation arbitrary.

Core Idea
Once you accept one effect, there is no rational stop to any number.

In this passage, Ghazali directly challenges the philosophers' 'from one only one' principle. He argues that if they claim it is improbable for many effects to proceed from one cause, they must prove it impossible. He then shows that there is no rational basis for limiting the number of effects once you go beyond one. This lesson examines his decisive refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
Read the passage and find the statement that the opponent says 'this is improbable—a conjecture'.
The opponent is a philosopher who argues against multiple effects.
2
Notice Ghazali's rhetorical questions
Ghazali asks: Why is it impossible? What prevents four, five, up to a thousand? These questions push the opponent to specify a limit.
3
Understand the logical dilemma
If one effect is possible, there is no rational ground to stop at two, three, or any number. The opponent must either prove impossibility or accept arbitrariness.
Ghazali's point is that the limitation is not rationally justified.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ghazali concludes: 'There is no recourse after going beyond the One.' This means once you accept the possibility of one effect, you cannot rationally limit the number.
This argument is decisive according to Ghazali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : قول القائل : « يبعد هذا رجم أظن » ، لا يحكم به في المعقولات إلا أن يقول : إنه يستحيل، فنقول : لم يستحيل ؟ ! وما المرد والفيصل مهما جاوزنا الواحد، واعتقدنا أنه يجوز أن يلزم المعلول الأول – لا من جهة العلة – لازم واثنان وثلاثة ؟ ! فما المحيل الأربعة وخمسة وهكذا إلى الألف ؟ ! وإلا فمن يتحكم بمقدار دون مقدار ؟ ! فليس بعد مجاوزة الواحد مرد، وهذا أيضاً قاطع.
We said: The statement of the one who says, “This is improbable—a conjecture, I think—one should not judge by it in intellectual matters unless he says that it is impossible.” So we say: Why is it impossible? And what is the recourse and the decisive criterion when we go beyond the One, and we hold that it is permissible that there follow from the first effect—not from the aspect of the cause—a concomitant, and two, and three? So what prevents four and five and so on up to a thousand? Otherwise, who decides upon a quantity without a quantity? There is no recourse after going beyond the One. This too is decisive.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that it is improbable for more than one effect to proceed from the First Principle. Ghazali begins by quoting an opponent who says that such a claim is merely conjecture. He then asks: why is it impossible? He points out that once we accept the possibility of one effect proceeding from the First, there is no rational ground to stop at two, three, or any number. The decisive criterion cannot be arbitrary. He concludes that there is no recourse after going beyond the One, and this argument is decisive.

Misconception: Some may think Ghazali denies the principle that from one only one proceeds, but he only denies its necessity.
Correction: Ghazali does not reject the possibility that one cause could produce one effect; he argues that the claim it must produce exactly one is not rationally necessary.

A) It is impossible for many effects to proceed from one cause.
B) There is no rational basis for limiting the number of effects from the First Cause.
C) The First Cause can only produce one effect.
D) The philosophers' argument is based on necessary knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that once you accept the possibility of one effect, there is no rational basis to stop at any number, so the limitation is arbitrary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
When you think about Allah’s power, do you subconsciously put a ceiling on what He can do in your situation, based on what you think is 'reasonable'? What would change if you truly believed there is no rational limit to what He can accomplish?
?
If you genuinely internalized that once you accept even one effect from Allah, there is no rational stopping point to what He can do, how would your prayer and trust in Him change starting today?

The Example of the Second Intellect Proves Multiplicity
Ghazali uses the diversity in the sphere of fixed stars as proof that many can proceed from one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the diversity of the fixed stars to challenge the philosophers' principle that only one effect can come from one cause.

Core Idea
Diversity in a single effect refutes the claim that one cause must produce only one effect.

In this passage, Ghazali presents a concrete counterexample to the philosophers' causal principle. He points to the sphere of the fixed stars, which they themselves identify as the second effect, yet it contains an enormous variety of planets. If a single effect can contain such multiplicity, why can the First Cause not produce many directly? This example is central to Ghazali's refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the detailed description of the planets' differences in size, shape, influence, etc. Note that Ghazali is using the philosophers' own framework.
Don't try to analyze yet; just absorb the example.
2
Identify the philosophers' principle
The philosophers claim the First can only produce one effect. Ghazali uses that same principle to show that the second effect (which is one) contains many, so the principle is false.
This is the logical structure behind the example.
3
Examine the diversity described
Look at the list of differences: size, shape, position, color, influence, auspicious/inauspicious. Ghazali argues that such differences prove they are not one type, requiring multiple causes. Yet they come from one second effect.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Understand the reductio
Ghazali says if this multiplicity is possible for the second effect, it is possible for the first, so we don't need the chain of ten intellects. Ask yourself: Why does this refute the philosophers?
The conclusion is that the philosophers' principle is baseless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : هذا أيضاً باطل بالمعلول الثانى، فإنه صدر منه فلك الكواكب، وفيه من الكواكب المعروفة المسماة، ألف ونيف ومائتا كوكب، وهي العظم، والشكل، والوضع، واللون، والتأثير، والنحوسة، والسعادة؛ فبعضها على صورة الحمل والثور والأسد، وبعضها على صورة الإنسان، ويختلف تأثيرها في محل واحد من العالم السفلى، من التبريد والتسخين، والسعادة والنحوسة، وتختلف مقاديرها في ذاتها. فلا يمكن أن يقال : الكل نوع واحد، هذا الاختلاف؛ ولو جاز هذا لجاز أن يقال : كل أجسام العالم نوع واحد الجسمية، فتكفيها علة واحدة، فإن كان اختلاف صفاتها وجواهرها، وطبائعها؛ دل على اختلافها، فكذا الكواكب مختلفة لا محالة، ويفتقر كل واحد إلى علة لصورته، وعلة لهيولاه، وعلة لاختصاصه بطبيعته المسخنة أو المبردة، أو المسعدة أو المنحسة، وعلة لاختصاصه بموضعه، ثم لاختصاص جملتها بأشكال البهائم المختلفة، وهذه الكثرة إن تصور أن تعقل في المعلول الثانى، تصور في المعلول الأول ووقع الاستغناء.
Then we say: This also is invalidated by the second effect, for from it proceeded the sphere of the planets, and in it are of the known named planets a thousand and something over two hundred planets, and they vary in size, shape, position, color, influence, auspiciousness and inauspiciousness. Some of them are in the form of Aries, Taurus, and Leo, and some in the form of a human, and their influence varies upon a single place in the lower world, in cooling and heating, good fortune and bad fortune, and their magnitudes differ in themselves. So it cannot be said that all are one type given this difference. And if this were permissible, it would be permissible to say that all bodies of the world are one type of corporeity, so one cause would suffice them. For if the difference of their attributes, essences, and natures indicates their difference, then likewise the planets are different necessarily, and each one requires a cause for its form, a cause for its matter, a cause for its specification to its heating or cooling nature, or its auspicious or inauspicious nature, a cause for its specification to its position, and then for the specification of their totality to the various shapes of animals. If this multiplicity can be conceived in the second effect, it can be conceived in the first effect, and we are dispensed with (further argument).
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a concrete example that refutes the philosophers' principle. Ghazali points to the sphere of fixed stars, which according to the philosophers is the second effect (the first intellect). This sphere contains many planets with diverse attributes and influences. If such multiplicity can come from one effect (the second intellect), then why can the First not produce multiplicity directly? Ghazali argues that the difference in attributes proves that they are not one type, so they need multiple causes. But since they all come from the second effect, it shows that one thing can produce many. Therefore, the philosophers' claim that the First can only produce one is baseless.

Correction: Ghazali is using the philosophers' own framework (the second intellect) to refute their principle from within.

Key Takeaway
The diversity within a single effect proves that a cause can produce multiple effects, refuting the philosophers' necessary link between oneness of cause and oneness of effect.

?

What is one step you can take today to acknowledge His control over that area?

Ghazali's Fifth Objection: The Absurdity of Arbitrary Derivation from the First Intellect
Ghazali mocks the philosophers' arbitrary derivation of the sphere from the first intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique that the philosophers' explanation of the first intellect's effects is arbitrary and lacks rational necessity.

Core Idea
No rational connection exists between an attribute and its supposed effect.

Ghazali now turns to a specific philosophical claim about the first intellect. The philosophers assert that from different aspects of the first intellect—its possibility, its self-intellection, and its intellection of the First—different cosmic entities arise. Ghazali finds this utterly arbitrary and mocks it with a vivid analogy. This passage demonstrates the heart of his attack on the philosophers' causal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read Ghazali's fifth objection carefully, noting his sarcastic tone.
Notice how he begins by sarcastically granting the philosophers' premises.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
Identify the three aspects of the first intellect (possibility, self-intellection, intellection of the First) and the corresponding effects (body, soul, intellect of the sphere).
These are the specific claims Ghazali attacks.
3
Understand Ghazali's analogy
Consider Ghazali's counterexample of a human being with the same attributes. Why does he think this analogy is devastating?
The same logic would absurdly produce a sphere from a human.
4
Grasp the logical principle
Recognize that possibility of existence is a universal predicate, so it cannot produce a specific effect without a rational connection. The philosophers' derivation is arbitrary.
5
Reflect on the rhetorical style
Notice how Ghazali uses rhetorical questions and ridicule to strengthen his argument. What effect does this have on the reader?
He concludes that only a madman would accept such reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الخامس : هو أنا نقول : سلمنا لكم هذه الأوضاع الباردة، والتحكمات الفاسدة، ولكن كيف لا تستحيون من قولكم : إن كون المعلول الأول ممكن الوجود، اقتضى وجود جرم الفلك الأقصى منه، وعقله نفسه، اقتضى وجود نفس الفلك منه، وعقله الأول اقتضى وجود عقل الفلك منه؛ وما الفصل بين قائل هذا، وبين قائل - عرف وجود إنسان غائب، وأنه ممكن الوجود، وأنه يعقل نفسه، وصانعه فقال - : يلزم من كونه ممكن الوجود وجود فلك، فيقال : وأى مناسبة بين كونه ممكن الوجود، وبين وجود فلك منه ؟ ! وكذلك يلزم من كونه عاقلا لنفسه، ولصانعه، شيئان آخران، وهذا إذا قيل في إنسان ضحك منه، فكذا فى موجود آخر؛ إذ إمكان الوجود قضية لا تختلف باختلاف ذات الممكن، إنساناً كان أو ملكاً أو فلكاً، فلست أدرى كيف يقنع المجنون من نفسه بمثل هذه الأوضاع، فصلا عن العقلاء الذين يشقون الشعر بزعمهم (1) في المعقولات. (1) عبارة أجمعت عليها الأصول، وهي غير ظاهرة المعنى.
The fifth objection: It is that we say: We grant you these cold postures and corrupt arbitrary judgments, but why are you not ashamed of your statement that the first intellect's being possible in existence necessitated the existence of the body of the outermost sphere from it, and its intellection of itself necessitated the existence of the soul of the sphere from it, and its intellection of the First necessitated the existence of the intellect of the sphere from it? What is the distinction between the one who says this and the one who—knowing the existence of an absent human, that he is possible in existence, and that he intellected himself and his Maker—said: It follows from his being possible in existence the existence of a sphere, and it would be said: What is the connection between his being possible in existence and the existence of a sphere from him? Likewise, it follows from his intellection of himself and his Maker two other things. If this is said about a human, it is laughable; so likewise for another existent. For possibility of existence is a predicate that does not differ with the difference of the essence of the possible, be it human, angel, or sphere. I do not know how a madman would be satisfied with such postures, let alone the rational ones who split hairs (as they claim) in intellectual matters. (1) An expression agreed upon by the sources, and its meaning is not apparent.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is the fifth objection Ghazali levels against the philosophers. He sarcastically grants them their 'cold postures and corrupt arbitrary judgments' and then attacks their specific explanation of how the first intellect gives rise to the sphere. The philosophers had asserted that from the first intellect's possibility of existence comes the body of the outermost sphere, from its self-intellection comes the soul of the sphere, and from its intellection of the First comes the intellect of the sphere. Ghazali finds this totally arbitrary. He draws an analogy: if one said the same about a human being—that his possibility necessitates a sphere, it would be ridiculous. The predicate 'possibility of existence' is the same for all possible beings, so there is no reason why it should produce a sphere in one case and not another. He concludes that only a madman would accept such reasoning, let alone the philosophers who pride themselves on their rationality. The footnote indicates that the expression 'يشقون الشعر' (split hairs) is an agreed-upon expression but its meaning is not apparent.

عقل الفلك

Correction: Ghazali does not deny the first intellect's existence; he is rejecting the philosophers' arbitrary derivation of effects from its attributes.

Checkpoint

A) The claim that the first intellect exists.
B) The attribution of different effects to different aspects of the first intellect without any rational connection.
C) The idea that the first intellect can think.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that the philosophers' derivation of cosmic effects from the first intellect is logically arbitrary and lacks any necessary connection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You claim to love God and trust His wisdom. But do you sometimes assume that certain blessings or trials must come from specific actions of yours, similar to the philosophers' arbitrary causation? How can you submit to God's sovereignty without fabricating links between His attributes and worldly events?
